HARD THRUST
by Joshua Cox

========================================

CONTENT NOTE: This novel contains explicit sexual content, psychological manipulation, violence, and themes of trauma and memory.

# CHAPTER 1: ARRIVAL
## October 22 — Josh

---

The road turned to gravel three miles back, and with each switchback, the world below disappeared. I'd left the last town—a gas station, a diner, a handful of houses—forty-five minutes ago, climbing steadily into elevations where cell service flickered to almost nothing. October light slanted through the pines—that particular amber that only exists in the hour before sunset—and I kept my eyes on the curves instead of the rearview mirror. I'd been driving since dawn, and the long drive had left me eager to arrive. Hours of podcasts and coffee, my mind running through everything I'd researched about this place. I'd first mentioned the trip nearly two years ago—"planning something big, a remote Oregon estate with a dark history"—and my listeners had been asking about it ever since. Life kept getting in the way. Other projects, other cases. But Thornewood had never stopped pulling at me. They had no idea what I was really looking for.

Rachel, my agent, had been skeptical about this retreat. "Six months is a long time to be isolated, Josh. You sure about this?" But I was. Six months at Thornewood was exactly what I needed. Time to focus. Time to write. Time to dig into the story that had been taking shape for months. A book on cold cases at remote estates—the disappearances, the unsolved murders, the way isolation seemed to breed tragedy as much as eccentricity.

The groundskeeper’s name was Eleanor Moore. I’d found a few photos online when I booked the stay—a local diner's Facebook page, a single social media account she'd abandoned three years ago. Dark hair, pale skin, eyes that held depths I couldn't name. In my notes, I'd written: *Guarded. Private. The kind of person who chooses isolation deliberately.* The perfect caretaker for a place like this.

I shifted in the driver's seat, my jacket creaking. Brown leather, worn and comfortable. I'd picked it up years ago—Portland or Seattle, one of those research trips that blurred together after too many drinks. Already broken in when I found it, like it had been waiting for me.

The trees opened.

Thornewood Estate sat on a promontory like something out of a fever dream. Three stories of weathered stone and dark wood, Gothic revival bones dressed in dying ivy. The western face caught the sunset, windows blazing gold, while the opposite side had already fallen into evening shadow. Steep gables and a widow's walk crowned the roof. A turret rose from the northwestern corner, its windows narrow as judging eyes.

I’d seen photographs of this place too. But photographs couldn't capture the *weight* of it. The sheer presence. I’d chosen it for the book precisely for this reason—it was the quintessential example of the “American Gothic” style I wanted to capture.

I stopped the truck at the break in the trees, engine idling, and let myself look. Really look.

This was where it would happen. Where everything would come together for the manuscript.

My camera bag sat in the passenger seat—a nice DSLR I'd splurged on last year, two lenses, my podcast recording equipment. Extra batteries, memory cards, a spare microphone. I'd learned from past projects that capturing B-roll footage helped with the writing process, gave me footage to review when the words wouldn't come. Visual notes to supplement the written ones.

The gravel crunched beneath my tires as I rolled forward. A wrought-iron gate stood open—black metal curled into thorns and roses, beautiful and sharp. The driveway curved through what must have been formal gardens once. Now they were wild things, perennials gone to seed, ornamental grasses bleached silver by autumn sun.

A smaller building sat a hundred yards west of the mansion. Low stone cottage, smoke curling from its chimney. Eleanor's residence. The rental listing mentioned she lived on-site to maintain the grounds, a solitary guardian for a solitary place.

I parked in the circular drive, killed the engine. Silence rushed in—the kind of quiet that only exists far from civilization. No traffic hum. No distant sirens. Just wind in the pines and the tick of my engine cooling.

I sat for a moment, hands on the wheel, and breathed.

This was it. Six months to write, to research, to lose myself in the history and the atmosphere of this place. The lease was simple: writer's retreat, total isolation, Eleanor would provide meals twice a week and otherwise leave me alone. The solitude would be good for the work. And being here, surrounded by so much dark history—maybe that would help me understand what had really happened.

My heart kicked against my ribs.

Not fear. Anticipation.

I grabbed my camera bag, my laptop case, the duffel with a week's worth of clothes. The rest could wait. The air hit me when I stepped out—clean and cold, laced with wood smoke and decay. Apples, maybe. Rotting leaves. The mineral bite of approaching winter.

Thornewood loomed.

I walked toward the entrance—a massive double door, oak gone nearly black with age, iron hinges thick as my wrist. A bronze knocker in the shape of a bird. Crow, maybe. Or raven. Above the door, carved into the lintel: *THORNEWOOD – 1897*.

The key was in my pocket. Eleanor had mailed it a week ago with a note in precise handwriting: *Ground and second floors are yours. Third floor and attic are closed for repairs. Make yourself at home.*

I pulled it out. Antique brass, the bow shaped like a rose.

The key slid into the lock like it had been waiting for me.

The door swung open on smooth hinges—someone maintained them—and I stepped into the entrance hall. My footsteps echoed on marble. Black and white checkered tile, scuffed by a century of use. A grand staircase swept upward, splitting at the landing to embrace the second floor. Above me, a chandelier hung dark and dusty, crystals catching what little light filtered through the transom window.

Doorways opened to my left and right. Straight ahead, at the end of the hall, what looked like a dining room waited.

The air smelled like old wood and dust. Almost romantic—the way old houses held onto their histories.

I set my bags down.

"Hello?" My voice fell flat, swallowed by the house.

No answer.

Of course not. Eleanor was in the cottage. I was alone.

I moved left, through a doorway into what had to be the library. And stopped.

Floor-to-ceiling shelves, every wall, dark wood and leather spines. A fieldstone fireplace dominated the far wall, big enough to stand in. Two wingback chairs faced it, upholstered in burgundy velvet worn smooth by use. Persian rug, faded to gentle colors. A reading table by the window, scattered with—

I crossed to it.

Books. Someone had been reading here recently. The top volume was a history of Pacific Northwest architecture, a ribbon marking a page halfway through. A mug sat beside it, faint coffee ring staining the wood.

I touched the mug's rim. Cold.

My fingers brushed the book's page. The library had a timeless quality—the kind of room that felt like stepping into a novel, all dark wood and crackling fire potential. Better than anything I'd imagined when I'd booked this place.

I turned slowly, taking it in. The library smelled like a church—old leather, candle wax, the ghost of a thousand fires. Underneath it, something fainter: pipe tobacco, maybe, or cedar. The scent of men who'd spent lifetimes in this room. Light fell through the western window in golden bars.

I walked to the window. The cottage sat below. Smoke still rising. Lights on in the windows—warm yellow against the gathering dusk.

Movement.

A figure emerged from the cottage. Dark hair, long. Jeans and a canvas jacket. She crossed the yard with purpose, heading toward the far side of the property where the gardens gave way to forest.

Eleanor.

Too far to see her face, but I knew. The way she moved—efficient, deliberate. She carried a basket, gathering from the woods before dark.

I should go introduce myself properly. Play the eager writer grateful for this opportunity.

But I stayed at the window and watched her disappear into the tree line.

My phone buzzed. I pulled it from my pocket: one bar of service, holding on by sheer will.

A text from my agent, Rachel: *Hope you arrived safely. Remember—six months, no distractions, 50K words. Don't make me come up there.*

I typed back: *Here. It's perfect. Talk soon.*

The message hung, sending, sending, then finally: delivered.

Fifty thousand words. A book about cold cases at remote estates—the disappearances that never got solved, the bodies that never got found. That's what Rachel thought I was writing. That's what the contract said. Close enough to the truth that I could almost believe it myself.

I pulled out my phone again, opened the camera, and took a photo of the library. Documentation for the book. Then another of the view from the window. The cottage. The tree line where Eleanor had vanished. I thought about flipping to the selfie camera—a quick shot for the podcast social media—but the light was wrong, and I put the phone away.

Outside, the light was dying fast. That's how October worked in the mountains—one moment golden, the next moment gone. I should unpack. Find the kitchen, see what supplies Eleanor had stocked. Settle in.

Instead, I went back outside.

The air had dropped ten degrees. I zipped my jacket and followed the path Eleanor had taken.

Not following her. Just... exploring.

The gardens were wilder up close. Rose bushes grown leggy and tall, hips gone dark red. Lavender gone woody. Someone had planted perennials with an eye for succession: asters still blooming purple, sedum gone rust-colored, dried hydrangea heads rattling in the wind.

Beautiful. Deliberate. Maintained just enough to stave off chaos.

The path led toward an orchard. Apple trees, ancient and gnarled. Fruit rotted on the ground—that sweet-sick smell I'd noticed earlier. Beyond the orchard, the forest pressed close. Douglas fir, western hemlock, the undergrowth thick with salal and fern.

"You're early."

I spun.

She stood ten feet away, basket on her hip. Must have circled back while I was wool-gathering. In the flesh, Eleanor Moore was nothing like her photographs.

Taller than I'd expected. Five-eight, maybe. The photos had shown dark hair; in person, it was nearly black, falling past her shoulders. Pale skin—almost translucent in the fading light. And her eyes—pale green, like sea glass held to light. Looking at me with an intensity I wasn't prepared for.

I'm tall enough—six-five—that most people have to look up when they talk to me. Eleanor did tilt her head slightly, but there was no deference in it. The fading light made it hard to read her expression, but the weight of her stare suggested cataloging. Assessment. Like she was making notes I couldn't read.

"You must be Eleanor." My voice came out steady. Good.

"And you must be Josh." She didn't smile. "You're early."

"Wanted to settle in before dark. The roads are... winding."

"They are." She shifted the basket—mushrooms, I saw now. Chanterelles. "Easy to lose your way up here."

"I have a good sense of direction."

Now she smiled. Slight. One corner of her mouth. "We'll see."

She closed the distance between us and extended her hand. "I'm Eleanor. Eleanor Moore. I take care of the grounds."

Her handshake was firm, her palm cool and dry. The contact lasted maybe two seconds, but something passed between us—a current, an awareness. We stood there in the dying light, two strangers taking each other's measure, and tension settled between us. Whatever this was. Whatever was going to happen.

There was an intensity to her I hadn't expected. A watchfulness. It was... intriguing.

"I left supplies in the kitchen," she said finally. "Basics. I'll bring a proper meal tomorrow evening. If you need anything before then, I'm in the cottage."

"I saw."

Her eyebrow lifted slightly. "From the library window?"

How did she—

"It's the best view of the property," she continued. "Most people find that room first."

"It's beautiful. The whole place is."

"It is." She said it with certainty. With possession. "Thornewood has a way of getting under your skin. Fair warning."

She moved past me then, close enough that I caught her scent: wood smoke, earth, rosemary. Her shoulder nearly brushed mine.

"Eleanor."

She paused, looked back.

"Thank you," I said. "For letting me stay. I know you value your privacy."

"I do." Those green eyes held mine. "I expect you'll value it too."

Then she was walking away, back toward the cottage, basket swinging. I watched her go—the efficient stride, the dark hair catching the last of the light.

She'd said nothing strange. Done nothing wrong.

But every instinct I had was screaming. Not with suspicion, but with a different kind of warning.

This was going to be more complicated than I thought.

# CHAPTER 2: WATCHING
## October 22 — Eleanor

He's coming today.

I stood at the cottage window, watching the road through the trees. The writer—Joshua Caine. He'd be here late afternoon. Six months I'd been at Thornewood waiting, and now he was finally coming.

I'd flagged the estate's rental inbox months ago, set alerts for any booking inquiries. When his application came through under a fake name—Joshua Michaels, a "writer seeking solitude"—I'd known immediately. A true crime podcaster sneaking into my territory under a thin cover story? He'd walked right into the trap.

My stomach twisted. Anticipation. Hunger. The need to finish what I started. I'd studied his photos for so long I could draw his face from memory—the line of his jaw, the way his copper hair fell across his forehead, the exact shade of his blue-green eyes. Months of waiting, of imagining this moment. Now that moment was finally here.

I checked my reflection in the window glass. Flannel shirt, jeans worn soft from work, boots scuffed and dirty. Hair pulled back, loose strands escaping. No makeup. I looked like someone who belonged to this place. Just a groundskeeper. Harmless. The kind of woman a man would trust.

I filled the percolator, measured grounds carefully. The routine helped. Coffee, work, solitude. Simple rhythms to maintain focus.

Through the window, a flash of dark metal between the trees.

He was here.

My pulse kicked up. It had been weeks since I'd had to interact with anyone besides the grocery driver. Playing normal required effort I wasn't used to anymore. Smiling. Making small talk. Pretending to be the woman I used to be.

I watched the truck wind up the circular drive, parking beside the mansion's front steps. The engine cut. For a long moment, nothing. Then the door opened.

He got out—copper hair catching the light, trimmed beard framing his jaw, muscular build filling out that leather jacket—and the photos hadn't prepared me. Not really. They'd captured his features but not the way he *moved*, the easy confidence in his broad shoulders, the way late afternoon light caught the planes of his face. He stretched, arms reaching overhead, and his shirt pulled taut across his chest. The flat images I'd memorized were suddenly flesh and blood and breath, and heat coiled low in my belly.

He looked up at the mansion with what seemed like genuine appreciation for the architecture. Oblivious.

I should go introduce myself. Welcome him. That was the role I was playing.

But my hands were shaking. I set down the coffee pot. Took a breath. The headache was back—third day in a row now. I dry-swallowed two ibuprofen and told myself it was the altitude, the stress, the sleepless nights. He has no idea why he's really here.

I grabbed my gathering basket. Something to do with my hands. Chanterelles grew in the eastern woods this time of year—a reason to be outside, away from the cottage, while he settled in. Caught my reflection one more time—tired, worn. Someone who'd been alone too long, focused on a singular goal.

The cameras I'd installed during my first months here were already recording—hidden in the eaves of the mansion, in the trees along the drive, on the cottage porch with its view of the main house. I'd know every move he made. Every room he entered. Every expression on his face when he thought he was alone.

I shook it off and let myself out the back door.

The forest was quiet. I moved through the trees toward the chanterelle spots, basket on my hip, trying to steady my breathing. Maybe he'd stay inside today. Maybe I could delay the first interaction.

I found a cluster of golden chanterelles near a fallen log, knelt to cut them. The familiar work steadied my hands. A few minutes of this, and I'd almost convinced myself I was just a groundskeeper doing her job.

Then I saw him.

He was walking through the orchard, following the path from the mansion. Tall, well-built. Copper hair windblown from the drive. He seemed lost in thought, looking at the rotting apples, the gnarled trees. He hadn't noticed me yet.

I circled through the tree line. Came up behind him.

"You're early."

He spun. The look on his face—startled, assessing, intrigued. Blue-green eyes taking me in. The performance would need to be flawless.

"You must be Eleanor." His voice came out steady. Controlled.

"And you must be Josh." I didn't smile. "You're early." The words came out again before I could stop them—a rehearsed line stuck on repeat. Steady. Don't let him see.

"Wanted to settle in before dark. The roads are... winding."

"They are." I shifted the basket—chanterelles I'd gathered on the way. "Easy to lose your way up here."

"I have a good sense of direction."

Now I smiled. Slight. One corner of my mouth. "We'll see."

He seemed... kind. Open. The type who'd be easy to talk to.

Good. The friendly ones were always easier. They trusted without question, filled silences with their own assumptions, never looked too closely at the person listening.

I closed the distance between us and extended my hand. "I'm Eleanor. Eleanor Moore. I take care of the grounds."

His handshake was warm, firm. The first time I'd touched him after all those months of distance—all those photographs and imagining what his skin might feel like. Now I knew. Warm. Solid. Real. His palm pressed against mine and I felt it everywhere, a current running up my arm and spreading through my chest. I held on a beat too long before letting go.

Something heavy settled between us. Whatever this was. Whatever was going to happen.

"I left supplies in the kitchen," I said finally. "Basics. I'll bring a proper meal tomorrow evening. If you need anything before then, I'm in the cottage."

"I saw."

My eyebrow lifted. "From the library window?"

Good. He'd already been exploring. Predictable.

"It's the best view of the property," I said. "Most people find that room first."

"It's beautiful. The whole place is."

"It is." I said it with certainty. With possession. "Thornewood has a way of getting under your skin. Fair warning."

I moved past him then, close enough that I caught his scent: coffee, road dust, clean soap underneath. My shoulder nearly brushed his.

"Eleanor."

I paused, looked back.

"Thank you," he said. "For letting me stay. I know you value your privacy."

"I do." I held his gaze. "I expect you'll value it too."

A perfect first contact.

I walked back toward the cottage. Could feel his eyes on me. Was I walking normally? Did I seem strange? Did he sense anything wrong?

I reached the cottage, let myself inside, locked the door. Took a shaky breath.

That wasn't so bad. He seems predictable. I'd barely have to interact with him—he'd be busy with his project, I'd be working. I could observe from a distance.

I walked to the bathroom, splashed cold water on my face.

The woman in the mirror looked exhausted. Six months of this isolation had honed me to a sharp edge. The sleepless nights weren't helping, but sleep was a luxury I couldn't afford right now.

I dried my face, poured coffee. Stood at the window facing the mansion. A light came on in the library. Josh moving around, unpacking probably. Setting up his workspace.

The friendly exterior was perfect. Made him easy to read. Easy to predict. Nobody came to a place like this without something broken inside.

Six months I'd been here. Six months waiting for this. The time for waiting was almost over.

And now there was someone else here. Someone who'd be my sole focus. Someone I'd have to perform for every single day.

I took a sip of coffee. The bitterness helped.

Tomorrow I'd wake up, make coffee, work the gardens. Keep the routine going. Everything was finally beginning.

I climbed to the loft. The boxes were still there. Still sealed. Everything I'd brought with me—things I should have sorted through when I first arrived. Things I still couldn't face.

I turned away from them, sat at my desk. Pulled out a fresh notebook. Stared at the blank page.

*October 22. He's here. Joshua Caine. Seems friendly enough. Open. Trusting. Good. He can't know why I'm really here. Not yet. Not until I'm ready.*

I closed the journal. Put it away.

Downstairs, I changed, showered, made dinner alone. The same routine for six months. Just existing. Preparing.

Outside, darkness fell early. I kept the lights on like always.

At nine o'clock, the mansion lights went out one by one. Josh going to bed.

I turned off my lights. Sat in darkness.

Tomorrow would be the same. Work. Solitude. And watching.

I climbed into bed and stared at the ceiling.

I should sleep. Tomorrow would require focus—playing the part, staying in control, watching him without giving myself away. I needed to be sharp. Careful not to let him see how much I wanted this. How long I'd waited for this moment. For the chance to be alone with him, far from anyone who might interfere with what needed to happen.

But my mind kept drifting back to him.

The way his shirt had pulled across his chest when he stretched. The warmth of his palm against mine—that first touch after all those months of watching from a distance. The timbre of his voice saying my name.

My fake last name. The one I'd chosen specifically because it was nothing like who I used to be.

A candid shot someone had posted—him at a coffee shop, head bent over his laptop, jaw sharp in profile. The author photo where he stared directly into the camera, a knowing look in those blue-green eyes.

I'd told myself studying those photos was research. Preparation. Part of understanding him before I got close.

But I'd looked at them too many times for that to be true.
