# CHAPTER 3: TAKING ROOT
## October 23 — Josh

---

I woke to unfamiliar darkness.

For a moment, I didn't know where I was. The ceiling above me was too high, the shadows wrong, the silence too complete. Then memory assembled itself: Thornewood. The mansion. Eleanor.

My phone screen read 6:47 AM. Outside the tall windows, the world was still dark—October mornings came late this far north, this deep in the mountains. Wind tested the eaves. The house groaned and settled. Beneath it all, that particular quality of silence that only exists far from other human beings.

I'd slept poorly. Dreams I couldn't quite remember had left residue—a tightness in my chest, the sense of pursuit. My T-shirt was damp with sweat despite the room's chill.

I sat up, ran my hands through my hair, and tried to orient myself.

The master bedroom. Four-poster bed, dark wood, fireplace cold since I hadn't bothered to light it. My duffel sat on the floor, half-unpacked. Laptop closed on the desk. Camera bag beside it, already cataloged and ready.

I should establish a routine. Routines created structure, and structure created the mental space for observation. That's what my therapist had told me years ago, back when the nightmares had been worse. *Routine is control, Josh. Control is safety.*

Right. Because that had worked out so well before. All that control, all that structure. Until everything fell apart anyway.

I pushed the thought away with the ease of long practice. Ancient history. Water under the bridge. I'd lost my mom young—murdered when I was three, though I don't remember any of it. Spent a couple years in foster care before the Caines adopted me and gave me a real childhood. They did their best. Not their fault I turned out to be a drunk who writes about other people's tragedies for a living. Maybe I'm still looking for something in these cases. Maybe I just like the darkness because it's familiar.

I pushed off the blankets and stood. Cold floor beneath bare feet. The floorboards creaked—old house, old wood, everything here had a voice. I moved to the window and looked out at the grounds.

Dawn was just beginning. Gray light seeping into the valley, mist rising from the forest like breath. Eleanor's cottage was visible from here. A light glowed in one window. Kitchen, probably. She was an early riser too.

I watched for movement but saw none.

My reflection caught in the window glass—pale, haggard, looking five years older than I'd looked this morning. I looked away.

The bathroom was down the hall, shared, vintage fixtures and black-and-white tile. I grabbed my toiletry bag and headed out, conscious of every creaking board, every sound I made in the sleeping house.

The hallway was dimmer than I'd expected. Only one window at the far end, and the light from it was weak. I passed closed doors—guest rooms, presumably. One was slightly ajar. I paused, listening, but heard nothing. An old house with old doors that didn't latch properly.

The bathroom mirror was fogged at the edges, condensation from some temperature differential. I washed my face, brushed my teeth, tried not to meet my own eyes. Exhaustion made me look older. Or maybe I was finally looking my age. Approaching thirty. Not young anymore, not old yet. Somewhere in the middle, where time started to matter.

I splashed cold water on my face. More awake now.

Back in my room, I dressed in jeans and a flannel shirt, layered a sweater over it. The mansion was cold despite the heating system rumbling somewhere in the basement. This kind of building—stone and timber, built for a different era—would never quite be warm.

Seven-thirty. I should eat. Explore the house properly in daylight. And at some point, I needed to set up my workspace in the library.

The library.

The thought of it pulled at me. That room had been right yesterday in a way I couldn't quite articulate. The smell of old books, the fireplace, the light through the windows. I'd spent maybe ten minutes in there, but some part of me wanted to go back immediately.

I grabbed my laptop and camera bag and headed downstairs.

---

The kitchen was at the back of the main floor, connected to the dining room through a butler's pantry. Morning light was stronger here, windows facing east. The appliances were dated—1980s at a guess—but functional. Eleanor had stocked the basics as promised: coffee, bread, eggs, butter, milk in the fridge. A note on the counter in that same precise handwriting: *Help yourself. I'll bring dinner tonight around 6. —E*

I made coffee in a French press I found in the cupboard. The smell filled the kitchen, rich and bitter. While it brewed, I explored.

The pantry was well-stocked. Canned goods, dry pasta, rice, flour. Enough for weeks if I needed it. A wine rack held maybe a dozen bottles, dusty, labels faded. I pulled one out—Bordeaux, 1987. Older than some of my students back when I'd taught journalism.

Those two years at Portland State before the podcast took off seemed like another lifetime now—grading papers, department meetings, students who didn't read the syllabus. I'd traded stability for investigation. Best decision I'd ever made.

Research has taken me to strange places over the years. Lock-picking lessons from a fourth-generation locksmith in Portland—I'd spent three days learning the basics for a profile piece. A week inside a fishing trawler to grasp the patterns of coastal disappearances. The skills accumulated, useless until they weren't.

The coffee was strong and hot. Perfect.

I poured a second cup into a heavy ceramic mug and took it to the small breakfast nook. The path to the cottage stretched out below. Still no sign of Eleanor, though the light in her window was still on.

I pulled out my phone. One bar of service, flickering. A text from Rachel had come through overnight: *First day check-in. How's the gothic mansion treating you?*

I typed back: *Perfect. Quiet. Eleanor seems accommodating. Starting work today.*

The message sat there, sending, sending. Finally: failed.

I'd try again later from a different part of the house. Cell service was going to be unreliable here—I'd known that coming in. The estate had no coverage until a few years ago when they put up a tower in Brightwood. This far out in the mountains, it barely reached—one bar if I was lucky, and even that flickered in and out. Part of the appeal, actually. Isolation. Focus.

And access.

I sipped the coffee and let myself think through why I was really here.

Thornewood Estate. Four years ago, a young woman had been murdered on these grounds. The case had never been solved. The victim's identity had been withheld from official reports to protect the family's privacy—some forums and amateur sleuths had found the first name, but the full details had been kept out of official coverage. The case information was sparse, carefully controlled.

But the location wasn't private. Thornewood Estate. Public record. And anyone who Googled "Thornewood Estate Oregon" would find the headlines: "Body Found at Remote Estate." "Murder at Historic Oregon Mansion." "Case Remains Unsolved After Four Years."

That unsolved murder was exactly why I was here.

The estate's dark reputation had made it affordable. Murder sites rarely recover their property value, and Thornewood was no exception—priced desperately low, marketed as "historic" and "secluded" without mentioning why it had been sitting empty. I'd applied under a pseudonym—Joshua Michaels instead of Joshua Caine—because my real name would pull up my podcast, my true crime work, and that might make the property manager nervous about renting to someone who'd obviously want to dig into the murder.

I'd spent months researching this case, following threads through true crime forums and cold case databases. The victim had been a hiker, or maybe a caretaker—accounts varied. She'd been found in the woods near the estate, her identity withheld at the family's request. No suspects. No arrests. The kind of case that haunted me, the kind that deserved answers.

The groundskeeper, Eleanor Moore claimed she'd been here six months—at least, that's what the rental listing said. I didn't know much about her—only a name on the rental listing, a few old photos I'd found online. She seemed like any other caretaker at a place like this: someone who valued solitude, who'd chosen isolation deliberately. She'd be around, providing meals twice a week, but otherwise I'd have the place to myself.

Six months to research. To dig into local records, interview people in town, walk the grounds where a woman had died. Maybe I'd find something the police had missed. Maybe I'd finally crack a case that had gone cold.

My phone buzzed. The text had gone through. Rachel's reply came immediately: *Good. Focus. Write. I want pages by end of month.*

I smiled despite myself. Rachel Winters, my agent, was the best kind of handler—demanding, protective, completely convinced I was writing a straightforward cold case book instead of trying to solve the murder myself.

The book would come later, once I had answers. Once I understood what had really happened here.

I finished my coffee and rinsed the mug. Time to work.

---

The library drew me like gravity.

I pushed open the door and stopped, taking it in by morning light. The windows faced west, so the room was still relatively dim, but details emerged now. The floor-to-ceiling bookshelves were even more impressive than I'd realized. Hundreds—maybe thousands—of books. I moved closer, reading spines.

Classics. History. Philosophy. True crime.

I paused at the true crime section. Capote, Mailer, Rule. Standard collection for this kind of place.

The library was exactly what I needed. The desk by the window was perfect—solid wood, drawers that slid smooth, enough surface area for my laptop and notebooks. I set up there, plugging in my laptop, arranging my notes.

From this vantage point, the cottage, the gardens, the tree line spread out below. Perfect sightline for observation.

I opened a new document and started typing.

*Day 2 - Thornewood Estate*

*Eleanor Moore - first impressions*

The cursor blinked. I didn't know what to write. I'd barely spoken to Eleanor. One conversation at dusk, her eyes like sea glass in fading light, her voice careful and knowing.

She'd said the library had the best view. She'd known I'd find this room first.

How?

I looked around. The room was well-designed. Good light. Good proportions. Historic estates like this understood how to create comfortable spaces.

It would be a good workspace.

I turned back to the laptop and made myself focus.

*She's smart. Watchful. Guarded. Lives alone in the cottage, maintains the estate. Questions: Why is she here? What's her connection to the previous owner? What happened to the people who disappeared?*

A floorboard creaked behind me.

I spun, heart jumping.

Empty doorway. Empty hall beyond.

Just the house settling. Old buildings did that. Expansion, contraction, wood and stone finding equilibrium with temperature and humidity.

I stood anyway, moved to the door, looked out. Nothing. No one.

But I'd heard it. That specific creak, like someone stepping carefully on an old board, trying not to make noise.

"Hello?" My voice fell flat in the empty house.

No answer.

Of course not. I was alone. Eleanor was in the cottage. I'd seen her light on, she was probably having breakfast, getting ready for her day of... whatever groundskeepers did in late October.

I went back to the desk, but the focus was broken.

Maybe I needed to explore more. Map the house properly. Understand the space.

I saved the document and closed the laptop.

---

The second floor revealed itself slowly, room by room.

I started with a methodical approach—opening doors, cataloging contents, looking for anything unusual. Four guest bedrooms besides the master. All furnished with the same dark wood aesthetic, heavy curtains, vintage fixtures. Everything maintained but unused, that particular quality of preservation that suggested no one had slept in these beds for years.

In the third bedroom, I found water damage on the ceiling—a stain spreading from the corner, plaster bubbling. Recent, by the look of it. A roof leak Eleanor hadn't mentioned. I made a mental note to ask about it.

The fourth bedroom had a window seat overlooking the eastern woods. I paused there, looking out at the forest. Dense. Dark even in daylight. Easy to get lost in. Easy to hide things in.

The tower room drew me before I'd planned to explore it. Its entrance was tucked at the end of the second-floor hallway—still within my permitted area, according to the lease.

The door was narrower than the others, the stairs beyond steep and winding. I climbed into a circular space maybe twelve feet across, tall windows facing outward on three sides. The view was dizzying—forest in every direction, mountains in the distance, and below, the cottage. Eleanor's cottage.

From this vantage point, her front yard lay directly below. She was stepping out onto her porch with a steaming mug. Coffee, probably. She looked up—not at the tower exactly, but in the general direction of the mansion—and I stepped back from the window.

She went back inside. Through the cottage window, movement—Eleanor at her kitchen table. She pulled out a pill bottle, shook two—no, three—pills into her palm. Then she set them on the table and used the back of a spoon to crush them into powder.

  I watched, unable to look away. She scraped the powder into something I couldn't see from this angle—a glass, maybe, or a small container.

  The deliberate, practiced way she did it made my skin prickle. Not someone fumbling with a new prescription. Someone who'd done this many times.

Then Eleanor glanced up toward the loft, gathered whatever she'd prepared, and moved out of frame.

  I waited, but she didn't return to the kitchen.
No. I was being paranoid. Eleanor took medication—so what? I took medication too, when the insomnia got bad enough. There was nothing sinister about it.

But I stepped further back from the window anyway.

A few minutes later, movement caught my eye. Eleanor climbed the ladder to her loft—the small raised space visible through the cottage's upper window. She cracked the window for air, and for a moment, I had a clear view inside.

Boxes. Lots of them, stacked along one wall. Dark shapes in the shadows, contents unclear. Storage, probably. Everyone had boxes of old things they couldn't quite throw away.

My chest tightened anyway.

Those boxes were significant. The way they were arranged. The sheer number of them. Like Eleanor had brought an entire life here and locked it away in that dark loft.

Running from something. Or someone.

The grief I'd seen in her eyes made more sense now. She was hiding here, same as me. Whatever was in those boxes was part of what she couldn't face.

Eleanor closed the window, and the loft went dark again.

Being caught watching her was wrong. Even though that was exactly what I was here to do.

I turned away from the window and examined the room itself. Boxes. Old furniture covered with sheets. A steamer trunk pushed against one wall, probably full of more forgotten belongings from whoever had lived here before.

I had time. Months of winter isolation stretching ahead. Plenty of opportunity to explore every corner of this place.

I headed back downstairs, my footsteps echoing through the empty house.

---

By noon, I'd set up my recording equipment in the library.

The podcast had been dormant for nearly two years—since I'd announced the Thornewood trip and then gotten sidetracked—but I'd kept the gear. Good microphone, digital recorder, laptop with editing software. Maybe I'd revive it once I had the story—the real story about Eleanor and the disappeared. A documentary series, maybe. Something that would finally establish my credibility.

But mostly, I kept the equipment because it was familiar. Because setting up a workspace, arranging technology, creating the infrastructure for documentation—that gave me control.

I positioned the microphone on the desk, angled just so. Tested the levels. My voice played back at me, hollow and strange.

*"Day two at Thornewood. Eleanor's behavior is controlled. Careful. No red flags yet. But she's watching me as much as I'm watching her."*

I stopped the recording and deleted it. Too early. Nothing to report.

My phone buzzed—a text from Rachel had finally gone through.

*Getting settled in? Remember, I need pages by end of month. Don't get too comfortable in your gothic fantasy.*

I smiled despite myself and typed back: *Comfortable isn't the word I'd use. Atmospheric, maybe. Lots of material here.*

The response came immediately: *Material for the cold cases book, remember? Document the history, don't get obsessed with solving it yourself.*

Rachel didn't know the real reason I was here. She thought I was writing about historic cold cases, researching disappearances that happened years ago. A nice, safe, publishable project about unsolved mysteries at remote estates—the kind of book that documented tragedy without getting personally involved in solving it.

The lie had been easy. Rachel wanted the safe version—document the history, interview some locals, produce a tidy cold case narrative I could actually finish. She didn't need to know I was here trying to solve a murder.

I set the phone aside and looked around the library. It really was perfect. The desk. The light. The fireplace. The isolation.

I could afford to be here, at least. That was something. My grandmother had died two years ago and left me enough money to live frugally for a while. Not a fortune—she'd been a schoolteacher, lived modestly—but enough to cover rent, food, and the freedom to chase stories that didn't pay. Enough to spend a winter in the mountains digging into a case the police had given up on.

Sometimes I wondered if Grandma would approve of what I was doing with her money. Probably not. She'd wanted me to be stable, to teach, to have a normal life.

But I'd never been good at normal.

A knock on the doorframe startled me out of the thought.

Eleanor stood there, framed by the doorway, holding a covered basket. She was dressed for work—jeans, flannel shirt, work boots—but her hair was down, dark waves falling past her shoulders. She looked younger like that. Less guarded.

"Didn't mean to interrupt," she said. "Brought you some lunch. Figured you might forget to eat."

"I was setting up," I said, gesturing at the equipment.

She stepped into the room, eyes scanning the recording gear with interest. "You're a podcaster?"

"Was. Taking a break from it."

"What kind of podcast?"

I hesitated. The truth was dangerous. "True crime, mostly. Cold cases. Unsolved mysteries."

Something flickered in her expression. Gone before I could read it.

"I know," she said. "I listened to a few episodes after you booked the stay. Wanted to know who I'd be sharing the property with." She gave a small shrug. "You have a good voice for it. Very... trustworthy."

I didn't know how to take that. If she'd listened to the podcast, she'd heard me talk about Thornewood—about the dark history, the disappearances. My cover as just another writer seeking solitude was thinner than I'd thought.

"Thanks. I think."

"Popular genre," she added, her tone shifting to neutral. She set the basket on the desk. "There's sandwiches, some fruit, coffee in the thermos."

"You didn't have to do that." The lease said meals twice a week. This was already the second in two days.

"Part of the job. Keeping the writer fed so he can work." She moved to the window, looked out at the view I'd been admiring. "This really is the best room in the house."

I should keep my mouth shut. But the question came out anyway. "Can I ask you something?"

She turned, eyebrow raised. "Sure."

"I was up in the tower earlier. Looking at the view." I gestured vaguely toward the windows. "I saw you taking some medication. You were crushing pills. I just—" I stopped, realizing how creepy this sounded. "Sorry. That's invasive. None of my business."

Eleanor's expression didn't change, but her eyes shifted. Calculation, maybe. Or just wariness.

"Prescription," she said simply, holding my gaze without blinking. No further explanation offered.

Something in her tone told me not to push.

"Well, I hope the isolation helps," I said carefully. "With... whatever you're dealing with."

"We'll see." She gave me a faint smile. "So far, so good."

It made sense. Totally plausible. But the way she'd answered—too smooth, too practiced—made me wonder if she'd been expecting the question.

"You've spent time in here."

It wasn't a question, but she answered anyway. "When I first started. The old owner, Mr. Hartwell, he'd let me read in here sometimes. Before he passed."

"What was he like?"

Eleanor was quiet for a moment, still looking out the window. "Old. Kind of sad. Lived here alone for thirty years after his wife died. I think he was lonely."

"And you kept the place up after he died?"

"Someone had to." She turned back to face me. "Why all the questions? Research for your book?"

The way she said *your book* made it clear she didn't quite believe that's all I was doing here.

"Curious," I said. "Getting to know the place. The history."

"The history." She smiled, but it didn't reach her eyes. "Thornewood has plenty of that. Not all of it good."

"The disappearances."

I said it before I could stop myself. Direct. Unsubtle. Exactly the kind of thing I'd planned not to do.

Eleanor's expression didn't change, but her posture shifted. Became more alert. "You know about those."

"Hard not to. I did some research before I came."

"And you came anyway."

"I'm not easy to scare."

"Maybe you should be." She said it lightly, almost teasing, but there was an edge underneath. "People disappear from places like this. Remote estates, isolated positions. It happens more than you'd think."

"You've researched it?"

"I've lived here long enough to hear the stories," she said. "The woods are dangerous. People underestimate them. Go hiking alone, get turned around, hypothermia sets in. Search and rescue can't cover every acre. Bodies don't get found." She paused. "At least two or three disappearances per year in this area alone. Most are eventually found—exposure, falls, getting lost. But some aren't. The terrain is unforgiving."

It was a plausible explanation. Too plausible. Rehearsed.

"Is that what happened to the young woman who was killed here four years ago? The one they found in the woods?"

The question hung in the air between us. Eleanor's expression didn't change, but her eyes shifted. Wariness. Calculation.

"You did do your research," she said quietly.

"That's what the sheriff thinks," I continued. "That she got lost in the woods. But you and I both know that's not what happened."

"What do you think?"

Eleanor held my gaze for a long moment. "I think the woods around here are dangerous. I think people should be more careful."

"Is that a warning?"

"It's advice." She moved toward the door, then paused. "The equipment. The recording gear. Planning to interview me for your podcast?"

"Would you agree to an interview?"

"Depends on what you want to know." She tilted her head, studying me. "But I get the feeling you've already decided what you think about me."

"I haven't decided anything."

"Sure you have." She smiled again, warmer this time, and I couldn't tell if it was genuine or performance. "Enjoy the lunch, Josh. Try not to let the house get to you. It has a way of making people paranoid."

She left before I could respond.

I stood there, heart beating faster than it should, and tried to catalog what had just happened. She'd deflected. Changed the subject. Used humor to defuse tension. Classic techniques.

And she'd known I was here to dig into the murder. Not just write about it. Solve it.

The question was why that made her nervous. Was she a private person who didn't want a true crime podcaster poking around her home? Or was there more?

I opened the basket. Turkey sandwich, apple, thermos of coffee that smelled better than the instant I'd been making.

She was feeding me. Taking care of me. Building trust.

I was probably being paranoid. She was the groundskeeper. But her responses—too smooth, too practiced—stuck with me.

---

The afternoon light was already fading when I finally forced myself to eat. October days were short this far north, darkness creeping in by four-thirty.

I'd spent hours reviewing my notes, cross-referencing timelines, trying to build a coherent picture of the disappearances. But the more I looked, the less clear it became.

*Jennifer Wallace, 41, disappeared over four years ago in late spring. Teacher, last seen on hiking trail.*

*Robert Fischer, 56, disappeared January 2021. Trail volunteer, never returned home.*

A related news article popped up in my search results: *Hiker's Body Found After 3-Month Search - Mt. Hood National Forest, August 2024*. I clicked it, skimmed the article. A 42-year-old experienced hiker who got lost during a sudden late-season snowstorm last May. Body found in August by hunters. Cause of death: exposure and hypothermia. No signs of foul play.

I stared at the article. This was recent. Just two months ago. A completely natural death—someone who underestimated the terrain, got caught in bad weather, and didn't make it out.

It happened. Eleanor was right. People died in these woods all the time from perfectly ordinary causes.

But that didn't mean the pattern I was seeing wasn't real.

I closed the article and returned to my notes.

I'd compiled a list of every incident at Thornewood going back decades. The estate had earned its dark reputation:

*1983 — Original caretaker found dead at bottom of main staircase. Ruled accidental fall. Some locals had doubts.*
*1987 — Hiker disappears on ridge trail above property. Body found three months later in ravine.*
*1991 — Guest drowns in ornamental pond during summer party. Ruled accidental.*
*1994 — Ski lift accident at resort down the mountain. Not on the property, but locals lump it in with "the Thornewood curse."*
*1998 — Young woman murdered in east wing. Case never solved.*

Another tragedy. This place seemed to collect them the way old houses collect dust. I made a note and kept scrolling.

The library had gone quiet around me, the way old houses do when the light changes. I moved on to 2003.

*2003 — Contractor falls through rotted floor during renovations. Survives but paralyzed.*
*2008 — Hiker goes missing on eastern trails. Found deceased from exposure two weeks later.*
*2012 — Previous owner's wife falls from widow's walk. Ruled accidental. Owner sells estate shortly after.*

*2020 — Young woman found dead on the grounds. Strangulation. Case unsolved.*

The 2012 case caught my attention. A woman falling from the widow's walk—a classic gothic tragedy. Was it really an accident? The owner sold immediately after, which suggested guilt or grief or both. I made a mental note to research it later, see if anyone had looked deeper into that death.

Another tragedy in a house full of them. This place had earned its dark reputation.

The list went on. Years of nothing after 2012—the estate sat mostly empty, occasional short-term rentals—and then the more recent disappearances started. The ones I was really here to investigate.

*Sarah Martinez—*

I pulled up the news articles. Sarah Martinez, 34, Portland resident, disappeared October 2019.

I stared at the evidence I'd compiled.

Sarah Martinez had posted on a hiking forum two weeks before her disappearance: "Planning a visit to Thornewood Estate next week. Anyone know good trails in that area?" The post included a photo she'd taken from an earlier visit—the mansion's library, visible through the trees. The same estate where, just months after Sarah vanished, another woman had been murdered.

Martinez was an experienced hiker. Knew the mountains. She wouldn't have gotten lost on a marked trail.

And the timing—October 2019. Eleanor claimed she'd only been at Thornewood six months, but she'd mentioned working as a caretaker at various properties in the region before that. If she'd been in the area longer than she let on, the connection might be there.

I flagged it for deeper research and refocused.

I'd started with ten possible cases—ten people who'd disappeared in the region over the past decade after visiting or working at remote estates. But three were likely runaways, teenagers with histories of instability. Two others had been found alive in different states, starting new lives far from here.

That left five I couldn't account for. Five disappearances or deaths that clustered around this area, this estate, over the past few years.

Maybe more I hadn't found yet. Maybe fewer that actually connected. The data was messy. Incomplete.

And Eleanor Moore, the woman who'd been working in this region through the same period. The woman who'd ended up here, at the center of it all.

The woman who'd just brought me lunch and looked at me with those careful, knowing eyes.

The woman I was supposed to be investigating, not letting distract me with sandwiches and cryptic warnings.

Sometimes, late at night when the research consumed me, I wondered if I was losing perspective. Of all the cold cases in the database, all the missing persons files, this one had consumed me completely. Maybe it was the isolation of the location. Maybe it was the unsolved mystery. Maybe it was that the patterns fit together in ways that hinted at something bigger.

I told myself it was the pattern. The connections. The evidence pointing to Eleanor.

But deep down, I wondered if I was seeing connections that weren't there. The obsessive quality of my research. The way I'd convinced myself Eleanor was dangerous based on patterns that could mean coincidence. Maybe I was just another true crime obsessive, tilting at windmills.

I pushed the thought away and kept working.

I needed to stay focused. Document everything. Trust nothing.

My phone buzzed again. Another text from Rachel: *Seriously though, how's it going? You sound weird in your messages.*

I typed back: *I'm fine. Just adjusting to the isolation. Lots of good material. I'll send pages soon.*

The lie came easily.

Everything was a lie lately. My reason for being here. My credentials. My name on the rental application—Joshua Michaels instead of Joshua Caine, because a Google search of my real name would pull up too much, raise too many questions.

It was necessary—or so I reasoned. Investigation required deception. You couldn't get close to a subject by announcing your real purpose.

But sometimes, late at night, I wondered who I was becoming. Wondered if the line between investigative journalism and something darker was thinner than I wanted to admit.

The light from Eleanor's cottage flickered on as dusk deepened. A shadow moved past the window—Eleanor, a shape in motion.

Watching her made me a voyeur. But that's what I was, wasn't it? Someone who observed. Someone who documented. Someone who couldn't stop looking for the truth even when the truth might destroy him.

I turned away from the window and lit the fire in the library fireplace. The wood caught quickly, flames casting moving shadows across the walls.

The house settled around me. Creaked and groaned. Spoke in the language of old timber and colder nights.

And somewhere in the silence, it pressed against me: the sense of being watched.

Just the isolation getting to me. First-day nerves in a strange place. Nothing more.

But when I looked back at the window, my own reflection stared back. Pale. Tired. Already looking like I belonged to this place.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 4: PROXIMITY
## October 27 — Eleanor

I woke at dawn to the sound of rain on the cottage roof. Almost a week since Josh arrived. Steady, relentless rain, the kind that would last all day. Through the window, the mansion's dark shape loomed against gray sky, lights still off. Josh was probably still sleeping.

Morning routine: coffee, try to feel human.

I dry-swallowed my morning pills, chased them with water from the tap. The bitter taste was familiar now. The usual morning fog was lifting slowly—nothing that coffee wouldn't fix.

I'd chosen this. Chosen to spend my time here, doing what needed doing. Some choices didn't leave room for regret.

I made coffee standing at the window, watching the rain. Waiting for the medication to settle in my stomach, for the edge to dull.

My phone showed three missed calls. I silenced it without looking at who. Didn't matter. No one needed to know where I was or what I was doing.

Not until it was done.

This was the rhythm I'd fallen into over five days—wake before dawn, pills, coffee alone, watch the rain. The isolation was getting to me. I caught myself watching for lights in the mansion, wondering when Josh would wake up, whether I'd see him today.

It was pathetic, really. Six months alone hadn't broken me, but five days with someone just out of reach had made the silence unbearable.

I hadn't slept well in weeks. Months, maybe. The isolation wore on a person, even when you chose it.

I took my coffee up the narrow ladder to the loft.

---

The room was small, cramped. Dark even in daytime with the window covered. I'd told the property manager I needed somewhere private to work. She hadn't asked questions. People rarely did when you seemed sad enough, desperate enough for the quiet.

My old life was packed in boxes along one wall. I'd meant to sort through it all when I first arrived, but six months later the boxes were still sealed.

I set my coffee down and opened my laptop. Checked the weather forecast first—rain through tomorrow, clearing Wednesday. The rain made everything harder. Made the isolation crushing.

I opened my journal. Stared at the blank page. Started writing:

---

*Day 6. Still raining. He went running this morning despite the weather—watched him from the window just after dawn. He's been friendly the past few days. Asked about the trails, the history of the estate. Getting comfortable. Getting closer. Soon.*

*What am I doing? I came here to end this. I can't let him distract me. Can't let myself soften now.*

I closed the journal and stared at the wall.

What was I doing?

The question wouldn't go away. I'd come here to do what needed doing. And being around him was making it harder to stay focused.

I'd left everything behind—my job, the apartment, the life that had stopped meaning anything. No one knew where I really was. No one could reach me here.

Now I was here. Eleanor Moore, reliable groundskeeper. Quiet, competent, harmless. The perfect cover. Close enough to watch him. Close enough to take what I needed.

Some mornings I woke up and couldn't remember what normal was.

I closed the laptop and climbed down from the loft, locking it behind me.

---

The rain had picked up by the time I pulled on work clothes and boots. I stood on the cottage porch, looking toward the mansion. Lights on now—kitchen, then the library. Josh was awake.

I should go inside. Wait out the rain. Any sensible person would.

But I couldn't stand another hour alone in the cottage. Couldn't stand the silence and the thoughts that came with it.

I grabbed my tool bag and headed out.

The cutting garden needed work. Late-season roses to deadhead, beds to prepare for winter. Work that had to be done rain or shine. Work that would keep my hands busy and my mind occupied.

The library windows glowed from here. Wondered if Josh could see me through the rain. Wondered if he thought I was crazy for working in this weather.

Maybe I was.

The rain soaked through my jacket within minutes. I kept working.

---

"You're dedicated."

I looked up. Josh stood at the garden's edge, coffee mug in hand, hair damp from the short walk from the mansion's back door. He'd thrown on a jacket but hadn't bothered with a hood.

"Groundskeeper job doesn't stop for weather," I said, smiling. "These roses won't deadhead themselves."

"Seems like miserable work in the rain."

"Therapeutic, actually. Gives you time to think."

He nodded, sipped his coffee. Watching me. His eyes pressed on me like weight, and I kept my head down, focusing on my work.

"I was going to make more coffee," he said after a moment. "Want some? Real coffee, not the instant stuff."

An excuse to go inside. To be warm and dry. To talk to another human being instead of spending another day alone with my thoughts.

"That sounds great, actually." I pulled off my gloves, tucked them in my back pocket. "Give me five minutes to finish this section?"

"Take your time," he said. "Twenty minutes?"

"Perfect."

He jogged back toward the mansion. I watched him go, then turned back to the roses.

My hands were shaking. Not from the cold. From nerves.

It was coffee. A conversation with a neighbor. Nothing to be anxious about.

So why did it feel like more?

---

Twenty-three minutes later I knocked on the mansion's back door. Josh opened it almost immediately, hair still damp but combed now, wearing a clean flannel shirt. He smelled like soap and cedar. Clean and warm and...

I shouldn't be noticing things like that.

"Come in, come in," he said, stepping back. "Coffee's ready."

The kitchen was warm, windows slightly fogged. He'd set two mugs on the counter—he'd expected me to come. The thought made my chest flutter.

"You're efficient," I said, pulling off my wet jacket and hanging it by the door. "You've only been here, what, less than a week? And you already look like you own the place."

"I'm good at settling in." He poured coffee into both mugs. "How do you take it?"

"Black's fine."

We stood on opposite sides of the kitchen island. The marble counter between us like a buffer. Or a barrier.

"So," he said, "how'd you end up at Thornewood? Seems like a pretty isolated gig."

First personal question. I'd prepared for this.

"Needed a change," I said, which was true. "I was working in Portland—landscaping company, commercial properties. Too many people, too much noise. Saw the listing for this place and figured it was exactly what I needed. Time alone, space to think."

"Running from something?" He said it lightly, but the question landed with a little too much force.

The question hit closer than he knew.

"Aren't we all?" I met his eyes. "What are you running from?"

His smile didn't quite reach his eyes. "Deadlines. Expectations. The sense that I should be further along in my career by now."

I didn't believe him. But I nodded, sipped my coffee. Let the moment settle.

"I think solitude helps with that," I said. "Lets you figure out what you actually want, instead of what everyone else expects."

"Exactly." He relaxed slightly. "That's what I'm hoping for here. Six months of no distractions, no obligations. Just work and quiet."

Six months. His timeline.

"You'll definitely get quiet," I said. "Come mid-November the road becomes impassable for weeks at a time. It'll be just us and acres and acres of snow."

I let that sink in. The kind of quiet he was looking for was the kind that could drive a person mad if they weren't careful. Most people would show some anxiety. Josh nodded thoughtfully.

"Good," he said. "That's exactly what I need."

---

We talked for another fifteen minutes. Small talk about the estate, the weather forecast, where to buy supplies in town before the roads got bad. I tried to be friendly, warm, normal.

But there was a wall up with him, too. He was easy enough to talk to, but he kept it light, skating around any talk of his family or his past. He was a man with secrets—it radiated off him.

He seemed kind. Easy to talk to.

My coffee had gone cold. I'd been sitting here for twenty minutes—watching the easy smile, the thoughtful pauses, the way he listened without interrupting. A man who'd ask about your day and remember your answer. The kind you'd trust completely.

And that scared me.

Because he was making me forget why I was here. The kind ones were dangerous that way—they made you want to believe people could be what they seemed. I couldn't afford that. Not now. Not when I was so close to finishing this.

"You okay?" Josh asked.

I'd gone silent. Staring into my coffee. My hands white-knuckled around the mug.

"Sorry," I said, forcing myself to look up and smile. "Thinking about my to-do list. The gutters need cleaning before the snow comes."

"I can help with that if you need an extra pair of hands," he offered.

"That's kind of you. I'll let you know."

We talked for another few minutes, then I thanked him for the coffee and said I had work to get back to. Left through the back door.

My hands were steady until I reached the cottage.

Then I locked the door behind me and stood in the dark kitchen, breathing hard.

I'd smiled at him. Laughed at his jokes. Let him see me as harmless.

The performance was exhausting. Being around him, pretending to be someone I wasn't—it took everything I had. I splashed cold water on my face and stared at my reflection.

Against my better judgment, I was starting to like him. He had a quick, dry sense of humor. I'd caught myself studying the deeper green flecks in his blue-green eyes when he'd leaned close over his coffee mug.

That made what I had to do so much harder.

This was why I came here. Why I'd stayed for six months in this isolated place, alone. To focus. To finish what I'd started. These feelings were dangerous. A weakness I couldn't afford.

I couldn't afford to forget that. I couldn't let loneliness make me weak.

I dressed in clean work clothes. Braided my hair wet. Looked at myself in the mirror.

Eleanor Moore. Thirty-two years old. Groundskeeper. Friendly, competent, slightly sad. Someone who worked here.

Just Eleanor.

I climbed up to the loft and sat at my desk.

---

My journal sat where I'd left it that morning. I pulled it toward me, flipped past the earlier entry, and pressed the pen down before I could talk myself out of it.

**October 27**

*Had coffee with him today. Small talk and careful smiles. He's charming. Dangerously charming. The kind of man who makes you want to trust him.*

*I almost slipped. Almost let myself forget why I'm here. His questions about the trails, about the history of this place—innocent enough. But the way he watches everything, cataloging details, makes me uneasy. He's not just a writer on retreat. He's looking for something.*

*I'm seeing exactly what I need to see. The isolation is sharpening me. Or maybe it's making me reckless. I can't decide which is more dangerous.*

*I find myself wanting to trust him. That's the most dangerous thing of all. I have to remember what he is. What happens when I stop pretending. What I want to do to him.*

*This is going to get so much worse before it gets better.*

I stopped. Read what I'd written. Felt sick.

What was I doing? Getting too close. Letting myself feel things I couldn't afford to feel.

My stomach turned again. I closed the journal and shoved it in the drawer.

---

The afternoon passed in genuine work—clearing drainage ditches, repairing a section of fence, preparing equipment for winter. Physical labor that let my mind quiet.

But I was always aware of the mansion. Of Josh moving through rooms—I could tell by which lights flickered on through the windows. Of how careful I had to be. Of how exhausting it was to try to seem normal when I was so broken inside.

Late afternoon I worked in the cottage's side yard, splitting firewood. Visible from the library if he happened to look. Physical labor that was honest, at least.

I didn't look up at the mansion. Kept my rhythm steady. Split, toss, reset.

Eventually his attention shifted elsewhere. I waited a bit longer, then carried an armload of split wood to the cottage porch.

I wondered if I seemed normal. If I looked like what I was supposed to be. Or if he could tell something was off with me—the way I was always off these days.

---

By 6:30 PM full darkness had fallen. I lit the cottage's interior lamps, deliberately leaving curtains open enough that the warm glow was visible. Made dinner—soup and bread. Ate alone at my small table.

Through the window, the mansion's lit windows beckoned. The library glowed gold. Josh was there, working probably.

I wondered what he was writing. What stories he told himself about who he was and what he was doing here.

I wondered if he ever suspected that someone might be watching him the way he watched the world.

At 8:15 PM I climbed to the loft. Sat at my desk. Stared at the locked drawer in the corner.

I couldn't open it tonight. If I started going through what I'd brought—what I'd been collecting for years—I wouldn't sleep at all. The materials had a gravity to them. Once I started, I couldn't stop.

I took a sleeping pill from the bottle on my nightstand. One. Enough to quiet my mind.

Tomorrow I'd be sharper. More focused. Tomorrow would be easier.

---

At 10:47 PM the mansion's lights went out one by one. Kitchen, then hallway, then upstairs. Josh going to bed.

I sat in darkness and listened to the cottage settle. Wind in the trees. The stove ticking. The silence that was never quite silent when you'd spent months alone.

Tomorrow was Day 7. More work. More avoiding Josh or seeking him out—I couldn't decide which was healthier.

More trying to figure out if my instincts were warning me about danger or screaming from loneliness.

I thought about the locked drawer. About what was inside. About what I'd come here to do.

I thought about Josh, sleeping peacefully a hundred yards away, probably not thinking about me at all.

Both of us running from something. Both of us hiding in this isolated place.

I closed my eyes and waited for sleep that probably wouldn't come.

And tried not to think about how badly I wanted someone to talk to. How badly I wanted to trust someone again.

How dangerous that wanting could be.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 5: PATTERNS
## October 28 — Josh

---

By the end of my first week, the library had become my domain.

I'd claimed the leather chair nearest the fireplace, positioned my laptop on the reading table where the light from the western window hit perfectly at two in the afternoon. My notes spread across the surface—research on the victims, timeline notes, everything I'd found about Eleanor. Everything I'd compiled over months, now laid out in the one place where I could think clearly.

The fire crackled. Outside, rain had started sometime before dawn—that steady October rain that turned the world gray and made the mansion seem even more isolated. I'd been reading since six AM, and now it was almost noon. The handyman Roy had stopped by yesterday to check on the heating system—friendly guy, somewhere in his fifties, with a hardware store in town and the kind of local knowledge that came from decades in one place. He'd mentioned he checked on the property regularly for Eleanor. Another person in her orbit I'd need to assess eventually.

I should eat. Probably skipped breakfast again.

I could afford to forget meals. Between Grandma's inheritance and the small trust fund my adoptive parents set up, the basics were covered—rent, travel, equipment. I supplemented with occasional freelance articles for my editor Derek at TrueCrimeWeekly.com, though those had become rarer as I'd become more focused on this investigation. Derek kept emailing about a book manuscript, but I wasn't ready to pitch the Thornewood story yet. Not until I had proof. The caretaker positions helped more than the writing ever had. Free housing, utilities, sometimes meals. Six months at Thornewood meant six months of near-zero expenses while I worked.

The trust wouldn't last forever. Maybe another two years if I was careful. Maybe less if I kept taking unpaid "research trips" to isolated estates. But I'd figure that out later. Right now, the investigation was everything.

The Thornewood Estate archives were more complete than I'd expected. Someone—probably Eleanor, or whoever managed the property—had kept meticulous records. I'd found boxes in the study: guest books dating back to 1924, property tax records, newspaper clippings about the original owners. Nothing sinister on the surface. The slow accumulation of history.

But patterns emerged if you knew how to look.

I'd cross-referenced the guest books with missing persons databases. Found three names that matched. Three people who'd signed the register between 2015 and 2022, stayed at Thornewood for extended periods, then vanished from the public record within weeks of leaving.

Three more disappearances connected to this place that the local sheriff had never bothered to investigate.

I made detailed notes in my journal, documenting everything methodically. This was solid investigative work. I was building a real case here.

The library door opened.

Eleanor stood in the doorway, rain-damp hair pulled back, carrying a basket covered with a kitchen towel. She wore jeans and a worn leather jacket, both wet. She must have walked from the cottage in the downpour.

"Lunch," she said. "Figured you'd forget to eat."

How did she know that?

"You look exhausted," Eleanor said. She set the basket on the table beside my notes. Her fingers lingered near the edge of my journal—not touching, but close. "When's the last time you slept properly?"

I looked up at her. Rain had darkened her hair, made it curl slightly at the ends. Water beaded on her worn leather jacket. This close, the depth in her green eyes was startling.

"Last night," I said. "Been at this table until 3 AM reading through guest books. Time disappears when you're chasing patterns."

"What were you reading?" She nodded at the archives spread across the table.

"Guest books. Property records." I closed my journal casually. "Research for the book. The history of the place."

"Find anything interesting?"

Everything about her tone was light, conversational. But she was watching me the same way I watched her. Looking for tells. Inconsistencies.

"Three names," I said. "People who stayed here and then dropped off the grid. I'm thinking there might be a chapter about Thornewood's draw—why certain people are attracted to isolated places like this."

"Certain people." She unpacked the basket—sandwiches wrapped in brown paper, two apples, a thermos. "What kind of people?"

"Ones who need to disappear."

The words hung between us. Eleanor poured coffee from the thermos into two tin cups, handed me one. Her expression didn't change.

"Everyone here is running from something," she said. "Or running toward it. Sometimes it's the same thing."

"What are you running from?"

She smiled. "I'm not running. I'm exactly where I choose to be."

I took the coffee. Our fingers brushed. The contact lasted maybe half a second, but I felt it like voltage.

"Lunch," she said, gesturing to the sandwiches. "You need to eat."

I did need to eat. My hands were shaking slightly—low blood sugar, probably. I took a sandwich and unwrapped it. Turkey, cheese, lettuce. Simple. She sat across from me, not eating yet, watching.

"Can I ask you something?" she said.

"Of course."

"Do you ever feel like you're going through the motions?" she asked. "Like you're present but not really *there*? I've had that lately. Whole days where I've been working but it all feels... distant. Like I'm watching myself from outside."

The question seemed to come from somewhere personal. I set down my sandwich.

"Like you're just numb?" I said.

"Yeah. Exactly." She was quiet for a moment, looking out the window. "Do you think that's normal? Living out here alone, I mean. Or is it... I don't know. Something to worry about?"

"I think isolation does that," I said. "When you're alone too much, your brain starts to... I don't know. Go numb. Like you're not quite tethered to reality anymore."

"That's what I'm afraid of." She wasn't looking at me now. "That I've been alone too long. That I'm losing perspective on what's real and what isn't."

The admission was raw. Vulnerable.

"Has that been happening to you?" I asked carefully.

"Sometimes," she said quietly. "I'll finish a day of work and realize I barely remember doing it. Like I was on autopilot the whole time."

I understood that better than I wanted to admit. "Where does your mind go?"

She was quiet for a long moment. "The past. Things I can't change. Mistakes that can't be undone." She picked up her sandwich, but didn't take a bite. Held it, looking at it like she'd forgotten what it was for.

The question had unsettled her. Like I'd touched a nerve.

"Eat," she said finally, more to herself than to me. "Before it gets cold."

I finished the coffee—still hot, bitter, strong—and set down the empty cup. Eleanor immediately reached for the thermos, tilted it as if to pour more.

"I'm good, thanks," I said.

She nodded and screwed the cap back on.

---

We ate in silence for a while. The rain kept falling. The fire crackled. Outside, the world was gray and close.

"My mother was murdered when I was three," I said suddenly. I hadn't planned to say it. The words came out.

Eleanor looked up. "I'm sorry."

"It was a long time ago. I don't even remember her."

"Doesn't mean it doesn't matter."

I took a bite of sandwich, chewed, swallowed. "I was there when it happened. Or so they tell me. I don't have any memory of it. Just... nothing. A blank where a mother should be."

Eleanor was quiet, watching me.

"The case went cold," I continued. "They never found who did it. I was too young to be a witness. Spent a couple years in foster care, then the Caines adopted me. Good people. But I always wondered."

The confession sat between us, heavy and cold. I'd never told anyone that directly. My therapists had known, of course. Rachel knew some version of it. But I never said it out loud like that. Flat. Factual.

I'd spent so many years not talking about it that saying it out loud was almost freeing. Like finally putting down something I'd been carrying too long.

Eleanor's expression shifted—soft and terrible. "Josh."

"It's why I write about crime," I said. "Trying to make sense of it. Looking for answers that probably don't exist. What breaks inside someone that makes them capable of—" I stopped again. Looked at her. "I'm sorry. I don't usually talk about this."

"It's okay."

"I should get back to work."

"Josh." She reached across the table, touched my hand. "You don't have to carry that alone."

Her hand was warm. Real. I looked at where our skin touched and felt my chest crack open.

"Thank you for lunch," I said.

She squeezed once, then withdrew. Stood. "There's more in the thermos if you want it. I'll be working in the gardens if you need anything."

She left the basket, gathered her jacket, and walked to the door. Paused there.

"I'm making stew tonight," she said. "Come to the cottage around six. If you want."

"I want."

She smiled—brief, genuine—and then she was gone.

I sat alone at the table and looked at my hands. They were still shaking.

---

I tried to work.

Opened my laptop. Read through my notes. I'd found three names in the guest books of people who'd stayed at Thornewood over the past several years, then vanished. Credit cards going dark, social media stopping, trails going cold.

Not moved. Not relocated. Vanished. The kind of disappearance that doesn't happen by accident.

The most recent case was what brought me here. A young woman, four years ago during winter. The details were sparse in the public record—the family had requested privacy, and the local paper had respected it. She'd been staying at Thornewood for the winter. Reported missing after she stopped responding to calls. The initial search had been cursory. Adult, no signs of foul play, probably just moved on. Case initially closed. But three months later, remains were discovered on the grounds. Cause of death: strangulation. Homicide investigation opened. No suspects. Case went cold.

But I'd found journal entries online, archived blog posts about coming to Thornewood. About how perfect it was. About the isolation. About being watched.

I should feel triumph. I was here, inside Eleanor's domain, getting close to her. She'd invited me to dinner.

But all I could think about was the way she'd looked at me when I told her about my parents. That expression—soft and terrible and unnameable.

Focus.

I pulled up the background check I'd run on her. Limited digital footprint. No criminal record. Nothing beyond the diner photo and that dead social account I'd found before arriving. Employment records were sparse—some administrative work, property management, nothing that explained how she ended up here.

The kind of thin history you'd see from someone who'd deliberately erased themselves. Or built a new identity.

My eyes drifted to the bookshelves.

I stood and walked over, running my fingers along the spines. Victorian literature. True crime. Psychology. Someone who lived here had eclectic taste. *The Stranger Beside Me* by Ann Rule. *In Cold Blood*. Collections of Edgar Allan Poe.

The kind of books I would choose. The kind of collection I'd build if I lived in a place like this.

That thought unsettled me more than it should have.

I stepped away from the shelf, suddenly uncomfortable. Looked at my watch: 3:48 PM.

Late afternoon already. I'd been working since lunch. My coffee cup was empty, cold. I should take a break. Get some air.

I closed the laptop, gathered my things. I needed air. Space. A walk would help clear my head.

I grabbed my jacket and headed outside, following one of the trails Eleanor had mentioned. The woods were dense here, pine and fir crowding close. About twenty minutes in, I found something.

A campsite. Or what was left of one.

Tent poles jutting from the dirt, fabric torn and flapping in the wind. A sleeping bag soaked through with rain. Backpack leaning against a tree, partially covered by fallen leaves.

My pulse kicked up. Someone had been here. Left in a hurry, or never left at all.

I pulled out my phone and took photos. Documented everything. Tent poles at odd angles, as if pulled down fast. Sleeping bag still inside the collapsed tent. Backpack with personal items visible through the open pocket—wallet, keys, phone.

People didn't abandon their wallets and keys. Not unless something went very wrong.

I marked the GPS coordinates and kept hiking. I'd ask Eleanor if she knew about it. Watch her reaction.

---

I walked through the mansion, room to room, trying to orient myself. The dining room with its long table, chairs pushed in neatly. The kitchen where Eleanor had stocked supplies. The entrance hall with the grand staircase leading up to the second floor—which I'd already explored—and beyond that, the third floor. Off limits, she'd said. Closed for repairs.

I stopped at the base of the stairs and looked up past the second-floor landing toward the shadowed staircase that continued higher.

Something about the third floor pulled at me. Curiosity. The writer's instinct to explore every corner of a story.

I put my hand on the bannister.

"Don't."

I turned.

Eleanor stood in the doorway, backlit by gray afternoon light. I hadn't heard her come in.

"The third floor," she said. "I told you. It's not safe."

"I wasn't going up."

"Your hand is on the railing."

I looked down. She was right. I pulled my hand back.

"Sorry. I was..." What had I been doing? "Lost in thought."

She crossed the hall to me. Studied my face. "Are you feeling okay? You look pale."

"I'm fine. Just tired."

"Maybe you should rest before dinner."

"I don't need rest. I need to work."

"Josh." She touched my arm. "You're pushing too hard. I can see it."

I wanted to argue. But exhaustion hit me suddenly, like a wave. My legs were weak. When had I last slept through the night?

"Maybe you're right," I said.

"Go lie down. I'll come get you at six."

I nodded. Started toward the bedroom, then stopped. "Eleanor?"

"Yeah?"

"You ever get so focused on work you forget to eat? Look up and realize you've been sitting for hours?"

She was very still. "Sure. Everyone does that sometimes. Why?"

"I was at the desk all afternoon. Lost track of time completely. Must've been in that writing flow state—you know, when you're so absorbed in the work that everything else just disappears. Looked at the clock and realized it was almost dinner time." I smiled. "Happens to me a lot when I'm deep into research. One of the perks of being a writer, I guess. Total immersion. Hours can just vanish."

"Your body telling you to slow down."

"Maybe."

"Six o'clock," she said. "Don't make me drag you over there."

She smiled when she said it, but her eyes were serious. Concerned.

Or calculating.

---

At 5:45, I washed my face, changed my shirt, and headed to the cottage.

---

The rain had stopped. The evening was clear and cold, stars just beginning to show. I walked the path to Eleanor's cottage with my hands in my pockets, my breath fogging in the air.

Light glowed in her windows. Warm, inviting. Smoke curled from the chimney. I could smell wood fire and cooking food.

I knocked.

She opened the door in jeans and a sweater, barefoot, hair loose around her shoulders. "Hey," she said. "Come in."

The cottage was smaller than I'd expected. One main room—kitchen and living space combined—with doors leading to what must be a bedroom and bathroom. A ladder in the corner led to a loft. The fire crackled in a stone fireplace. A pot simmered on the stove.

Eleanor's cottage smelled different from the mansion. Pine soap. Coffee grounds. Wool drying near the woodstove. A woman's space, lived in and practical.

"It's not much," Eleanor said. "But it's home."

"It's perfect."

She gestured to the small table set for two. "Sit. Wine?"

"Please."

She poured red wine into two glasses, handed me one. Our fingers brushed again. This time it was deliberate.

"To isolation," she said, raising her glass.

"To finding what you're looking for."

We drank. The wine was good—rich, earthy. I realized I was starving.

Eleanor served stew in wide bowls—beef, potatoes, carrots, the kind of meal that took hours to make. She'd been cooking this all afternoon while I'd been absorbed in research in the library.

We ate and talked. She asked about my book. I gave her the cover story—cold cases at remote estates, the disappearances that never got solved, why tragedy seemed to haunt certain places. She seemed genuinely interested, asked good questions. Smart questions.

"So you're writing about why people are drawn to places like this," she said. "What's your theory?"

I took a sip of wine, buying time to think. "People come to isolated places for two reasons," I said. "Either they're running from something, or they're looking for something they can't find anywhere else."

"Which one are you?"

I met her eyes across the table. Candlelight made her face softer, warmer. The wine was making me honest. "Both."

"What are you running from?"

"The noise." I shrugged. "Deadlines, expectations, the constant pressure to produce. My agent thinks I'm having a quarter-life crisis at twenty-nine."

The answer came easily enough—a practiced deflection that happened to be partly true. But sitting across from her in this small cottage with the fire crackling and rain starting again outside, an unfamiliar vulnerability crept in—a kind of exposure that had nothing to do with my investigation.

"And what are you looking for?"

"I don't know yet." I paused. "Maybe I'll know when I find it."

She smiled. "Honest answer."

"You inspire honesty."

"Or wine does."

"Maybe both."

We finished eating. She poured more wine. We moved to the couch near the fire. The space between us was charged, deliberate.

The stew sat heavy in my stomach. Not bad—just rich, the kind of meal that made you want to curl up and sleep. I blinked slowly, fighting a wave of drowsiness. When had I gotten so tired? I'd slept well enough last night. Seven, maybe eight hours.

But my eyelids drooped, weighted. My thoughts moved sluggish, like honey.

"Josh? You okay?" Eleanor was watching me, concern in those green eyes.

"Yeah—food coma, I think. That was a lot of carbs." I forced a smile, took a sip of wine to shake off the fog.

The wine helped. Or maybe it was sitting up straighter, focusing. The drowsiness lifted slightly.

Just tired. Heavy meal, too much wine.

My eyes drifted to the ladder in the corner. "What's up in the loft?"

Eleanor followed my gaze. "Storage, mostly. My old life from Seattle. Boxes I haven't unpacked yet—teaching stuff, books, photos. Things I couldn't throw away but can't really look at either." She paused, swirling her wine. "Lost someone close to me. It's... hard to go through the memories. Easier to keep them locked away up there."

"I'm sorry," I said, and meant it. The grief in her voice was real.

"It's okay. Well, it's not okay. But that's why I came here—to figure out how to deal with it without everyone back home hovering and asking if I'm okay every five minutes." She smiled sadly. "Sometimes you need space to grieve, you know?"

I understood that. More than she knew. "Yeah. I do."

She looked at me for a long moment, then seemed to shake off the heavy mood. "Anyway. That's my deep dark secret. Boxes of painful memories I'm not ready to sort through. Thrilling, right?"

"Makes sense," I said. And it did. Grief storage. Painful memories locked away. It was exactly what someone would do.

"Can I ask you something?" I said, changing the subject.

"Seems to be the theme tonight."

"Why do you stay here? At Thornewood. It's so isolated."

"I like isolation."

"But why here specifically?"

She looked at the fire. "Someone needs to take care of it. The estate. The gardens. If I left, it would all fall apart."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the only answer I have."

I set down my wine glass. "Eleanor—"

She turned to me. We were close now. Firelight reflected in her eyes.

"I'm glad you're here," she said quietly.

"I'm glad I came."

This was happening too fast. I'd known Eleanor for what, six days?

Isolation did strange things to people. At least that was my rationalization. The remote setting, the winter darkness, the intensity of my work. Of course I'd fixate on the only other person for miles.

But it was more than that.

The space between us evaporated. She kissed me—slow, deliberate, like she'd been thinking about it for a while. Her lips were soft and she tasted like wine and wood smoke. Her hair smelled like pine needles and cold air. Like she'd been standing in the forest. Like winter itself. I kissed her back. My hand found her waist, felt the warmth of her skin through the thin sweater. Her fingers tangled in my hair, pulling slightly, and a wall in my chest fractured—a careful distance I'd maintained for years suddenly breached.

This was wrong. I was here to investigate a murder—and she was starting to seem like someone who knew more than she was saying. This was a violation of every journalistic principle I'd ever held.

But I couldn't stop.

Her hand moved to my chest, pressed flat against my heartbeat. She felt how fast it was racing. She pulled back slightly, just enough to look at me, and I saw it in her eyes—heat and calculation and an edge I couldn't read.

"Josh," she whispered.

Then she kissed me again, deeper this time, and for maybe thirty seconds I forgot everything else. Forgot why I was here. Forgot the investigation. Forgot the disappeared. Forgot myself.

When she pulled back, we were both breathing hard.

"I'm sorry," she said. "I shouldn't have—"

"Don't apologize."

"You're my tenant. This is unprofessional."

"I don't care about professional."

"Josh—"

I stood abruptly. Too abruptly. The room tilted slightly. Too much wine on too little food. Or something else. "I should go."

"You don't have to."

"I do. I need to think."

She stood too, more gracefully than I had. Walked me to the door. We paused on the threshold. Cold air rushed in, clearing my head slightly.

"Tomorrow?" she said.

"Tomorrow."

She touched my face, just once, fingertips against my jaw. "Get some sleep."

"You too."

"I will." But her voice said she wouldn't. That she'd be awake, watching, thinking.

I walked back to the mansion under stars so bright they hurt to look at. My lips still tingled. My heart hammered.

When I reached my bedroom, I checked my watch: 11:34 PM.

Wait. We'd finished dinner at... eight? Eight-thirty?

I'd been sitting with her by the fire. Talking. Laughing. Her hand on my knee. The wine glass in my hand—the warmth of the fire making me drowsy. The next clear memory was climbing the stairs to my room, exhausted. I must have said goodnight, though the words were fuzzy. Too much wine, probably.

The wine. How many glasses? Two? Three? Hard to keep track when conversation flowed so easily.

I sat on the edge of the bed. My head was thick, foggy. Had there been something in the wine?

Just tired. Just too much wine on an empty stomach. That's all.

I flexed my hands, stiff from too much typing. Probably should stretch more, but I was too tired to bother.

I opened the nightstand drawer and pulled out my journal. Wrote today's date. Wrote:

*Dinner with Eleanor. She kissed me. I think I'm falling for her. I think that's a mistake.*

I closed the journal.

Lay down in the dark.

Sleep came fitfully.

When I finally fell asleep, I dreamed of the abandoned campsite—the torn tent, the wallet left behind, someone running through darkness they'd never escape. I woke at 6 AM with those gray October clouds already threatening through the window.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 6: FIRELIGHT
## November 7 — Eleanor

---

I bring the wine at six.

Over two weeks since he arrived. Sixteen days of watching him settle into routines—morning coffee at the library window, afternoon walks on the eastern trails, the mansion lights moving room to room as he works late into the night. Sixteen days of small talk and careful smiles, of maintaining the performance while something shifts beneath the surface.

It's a simple gesture. Neighborly. Innocent. But nothing about this is innocent.

I've been watching him for weeks now. Studying his routines. His weaknesses. The way he sometimes stares at the eastern trail like he's remembering something he can't quite place. The gaps in his stories he fills with vague explanations.

I need to get closer. Close enough to make him vulnerable. Close enough to watch him squirm beneath that charming smile. Close enough to finish what I started.

The isolation will work in my favor either way.

He smiles when he sees me at the door. That slow, genuine smile that makes my stomach twist. I hate how much I like that smile.

"Hey. I was hoping you'd come over."

# CHAPTER 7: THE JOURNAL
## November 8 — Josh

---

I woke to the smell of coffee and the memory of her lips.

November had arrived—though I couldn't quite say when. The days since my arrival had started blurring together, meals and research and glimpses of Eleanor, the boundaries between them growing soft. I tried to count backward and couldn't quite pin down which day was which. November 8th, my phone said. Had it really been over two weeks already?

The kitchen. Last night. Not like the first time, weeks ago, when she'd kissed me and I'd kissed her back and we'd both pretended it was the wine. This was different. This was the kiss that had stopped the world and restarted it spinning in a different direction. Eleanor's hand in my hair, the sharp inhale when I'd pulled her closer, the way she'd tasted like wine and darkness, an edge I couldn't name.

The way she'd fled.

I checked my phone: 9:47 AM. Perfect. I'd set an alarm to make sure I got at least five hours of sleep.

After Eleanor left—her footsteps quick on the gravel path toward the cottage, the door closing behind her with a finality like a choice I couldn't unmake—I'd gone to the library to process everything. I'd worked until 4 AM, documenting every detail of our conversation, analyzing every word she'd said. I stretched, checking the view. Through the window, the cottage sat quiet—chimney smoking, curtains drawn. No sign of Eleanor. No movement.

I waited, watching. Twenty minutes. Thirty.

Nothing.

The investigation materials still covered the library table—victim profiles, Eleanor's background, the timeline I'd constructed. Everything I'd compiled over months of obsessive research, now complicated by the fact that I'd kissed the subject. That I'd wanted to kiss her again the moment she'd pulled away.

That I was standing here watching her cottage like some lovesick teenager instead of working the case.

The journal beside the laptop was full of the frantic scribbling I always produced during late-night sessions—the obsessive note-taking I fell into when deep in a case.

*Her behavior after intimacy is off. Warm during, but distant after. Like she's going through motions. Could be past trauma. Could be calculated. Need to pay attention to patterns.*

*Daily pattern: E. leaves cottage 6:47 AM, returns with firewood 7:12 AM. Routine is consistent. Wonder if she's avoiding me or being efficient. Check tomorrow to see if pattern holds.*

Normal investigator behavior. This was what the work required.

The next morning I found myself on the library sofa.

I'd been working on the timeline again. Cross-referencing victim disappearances with Eleanor's movements. The pattern was there—she'd been present for all six disappearances. But correlation wasn't causation. I needed more.

I gathered my notes, filed them away. The investigation was consuming me, I knew that. Late nights, obsessive documentation, seeing connections everywhere I looked.

But that's what good investigation required. Focus. Dedication. The willingness to chase leads others would dismiss.

I needed to be careful. Stay focused. Not let paranoia win.

# CHAPTER 8: SURRENDER

# CHAPTER 9: CROSSING LINES
## November 9-14 — Josh

---

The morning after Eleanor left my bed, I found myself standing in the entrance hall with cooling coffee, staring at nothing.

The night before replayed in fragments—her skin, her mouth, the way she'd looked at me after. Like I was someone worth seeing. The investigation seemed distant now, complicated by feelings I hadn't planned for.

This was getting messy. I'd come here to solve a murder, and instead I was falling for the groundskeeper. Classic investigator mistake: getting too close to someone involved in the case.

I needed to refocus. Separate the personal from the professional. Remember the case - the victim who died here, the truth I came to find.

I turned away from my reflection in the hall mirror and headed for the library.

By midday, I'd cataloged seventeen inconsistencies in Eleanor's background.

The research should have comforted me. Evidence, patterns, the slow accumulation of proof—this was what I was good at. But each new discovery rang wrong, like puzzle pieces that fit together but created an image I couldn't quite see.

**Inconsistency #1:** Eleanor claimed she'd been at Thornewood six months. But the property management company records (I'd called, pretending to be interested in hiring her) said the previous groundskeeper had left in March—seven months ago. One month unaccounted for.

**Inconsistency #2:** She knew the estate too well for six months. Knew which third-floor window stuck, which sections of the stone wall would crumble if you leaned on them, where the septic access was buried under two feet of ivy. That was years of knowledge.

**Inconsistency #3:** Her hands. I'd noticed them last night, cataloging every detail the way I always did. Calluses in the right places for groundskeeping work, yes. But also newer calluses on her fingertips—the kind you got from typing, not from shovels. And a small scar on her right palm that looked surgical, precise. Not from outdoor work.

**Inconsistency #4:** The books in her cottage. I'd glimpsed her shelf when I'd been there for coffee last week. Mostly nature guides and repair manuals—appropriate for the job. But I'd also seen a spine that looked like a psychology text. And what might have been a law enforcement manual, though I'd only caught it for a second before she'd shifted to block my view.

I wrote it all down. Filled three pages of my notebook with observations, timeline discrepancies, questions that needed answers. Most of it came from direct observation, a few phone calls during brief windows when the cell signal flickered to life, and the research files I'd downloaded before arriving. Working offline forced me to be methodical—every fact checked twice, every assumption questioned.

The pattern was emerging. Inconsistencies piling up. She wasn't who she claimed to be.

I needed more proof. Concrete evidence. Something undeniable.

Then I found it.

The guest book from 2020. Leather-bound, pages thick with age. I flipped to February, looking for the timeframe of the murder.

The page was missing.

Not just damaged or water-stained—*torn out*. Ragged edges where the paper had been ripped from the binding. February 13th, 14th, 15th. Gone. Deliberately removed.

I held the book up to the light, examining the tear. Clean near the spine, rougher at the outer edge. Someone had done this carefully. Recently, too—the exposed paper fibers weren't yellowed like the surrounding pages.

Who would tear out pages from an old guest book? And why specifically *those* dates?

I photographed the damage from multiple angles, added it to my growing file. Evidence of concealment, but concealment of *what*? The missing pages could have contained anything. Victim names. Witness signatures. Evidence that would point to whoever had been here during the murder.

Or evidence that Eleanor wanted to hide. Evidence she'd removed before I arrived.
