Eleanor left at 6 AM. I watched from the library window as her car disappeared down the driveway, headlights cutting through the pre-dawn darkness.

She'd told me yesterday she had "business in town." Medical appointment, she'd said. Would be gone most of the day.

Twelve hours round trip for a doctor's appointment? That didn't make sense.

I tracked her route using the security camera I'd hidden near the gate. At 7:42 AM, her car returned, driving slowly past the estate entrance without turning in. It continued down the old logging road—the dead-end track that went three miles into the forest and terminated at nothing.

Why would she go there?

I waited an hour to make sure she wasn't coming back, then grabbed my jacket and hiked the logging road myself.

The track was overgrown in places, barely more than two ruts through the undergrowth. Ferns brushed my legs. Douglas fir pressed close on both sides, blocking most of the light.

At the end, just where the road gave out completely, I found fresh tire tracks in the mud.

And something else.

Disturbed earth. A patch about six feet long, three feet wide. The dirt was loose, darker than the surrounding soil. Recently turned. Someone had been digging here. Very recently.

My heart hammered. I pulled out my phone, took photos from multiple angles, marked the GPS coordinates. The dimensions were too perfect. Too deliberate.

This wasn't random.

The estate seemed to be watching me. Every window a dark eye. Every shadow holding its breath.

I should have brought a shovel. Should have called the police right then.

Instead, I stood there, staring at what could be a fresh grave.

Who was buried here? One of the missing? Someone new?

Had Eleanor been here this morning?

I hiked back to the mansion, mind racing. At the cottage, I waited. Watched.

Eleanor's car returned at 6:15 PM. She emerged looking tired, mud on her boots, her hair pulled back in a messy bun. She carried nothing—no shopping bags, no evidence of having been to town.

She'd been somewhere else. Doing work that left mud on her clothes and exhaustion on her face.

About what might be buried three miles into the woods.

About the woman I was falling for, who disappeared for twelve hours and came back covered in dirt.

I waved back.

And wondered if I was looking at a murderer.

**Two:** A photo in the local hardware store's community board. "Thornewood Winter 2020" scrawled beneath it. Group shot of locals at some holiday event—Thanksgiving, maybe, or Christmas. Twenty people in winter coats, string lights in the background.

In the very back row, partially obscured by someone's raised arm, a blurred figure. Too far from the camera, the focus soft and indistinct. Could have been anyone—a dark coat and the vague suggestion of a face turned slightly away.

I'd stared at that photo for a few moments while the clerk rang up firewood supplies. The figure's posture had caught my eye—the way the shoulders angled, maybe—but the image was too grainy to make out any real details. Probably paranoia. Seeing patterns where there were none.

Now I sat at that desk, 12:17 AM according to my laptop, trying to make sense of the impossible.

I pulled up my calendar archives, email history, anything that would show where I'd been four years ago.

Sparse. A handful of emails sent from my phone, locations unavailable. A few credit card charges in Oregon. Almost no photos. No social media posts. No article bylines.

Two weeks of my life, barely documented.

At the time, I'd assumed I'd been working on a book. Deep research phase. I'd been focused, tunneled in. Hadn't bothered with social media or photos.

But now?

My phone buzzed. Eleanor.

**Can't sleep. You okay?**

I stared at the message. Typed and deleted three responses.

Finally: **Found some things. Need to think.**

**What things?**

**Evidence I might have been here before. Four years ago.**

The three dots appeared. Disappeared. Appeared again. Five minutes of typing and deleting.

Finally: **Can we talk? In person?**

**Now?**

**Yes.**

I looked at the clock. 12:34 AM. Looked out the window toward the cottage, where a single light burned in her bedroom.

Everything in me screamed this was a bad idea. Meeting her now, in the middle of the night, when I'd started suspecting the worst—about her, about myself, about everything.

But I needed answers more than I needed safety.

**Library. Ten minutes.**

**Ok.**

I closed my laptop. Looked around the library. The unease from earlier still lingered, but I pushed it aside.

If I had been here before, there would be records. Evidence. Something tangible.

But there was nothing. Just coincidences and paranoia.

The ten minutes stretched like hours. I paced, rehearsed what I'd say, tried to prepare for whatever came next.

At 12:44, Eleanor knocked softly on the library door.

"Come in."

She entered wrapped in a heavy wool coat, hair loose, face scrubbed of makeup. She looked younger. Tired. Real in a way that made my chest ache.

"Show me what you found," she said.

I showed her everything. The torn pages from the guest book. The photo from the hardware store. The glove. My empty calendar from early 2020.

Eleanor examined each piece in silence. I watched her face, looking for tells—surprise, recognition, fear. But she was unreadable, or I'd lost the ability to read her.

Finally, she looked up.

"The photo. Do you really think that's you? It's so blurry."

I wanted to say no. Wanted that to be the answer.

"I don't know. But my calendar from that time is almost empty. Like I was... somewhere. Doing something. Just not documenting it."

"Maybe you were here researching and it didn't register as significant at the time," she said carefully. "You investigate a lot of places. Maybe Thornewood was another stop on a research trip?"

The way she said it—like she was testing something.

"Maybe. I was working on several cases back then. It's possible I stopped here briefly and it didn't seem important enough to document."

"The mind is funny that way." She paused. "Sometimes we remember the big moments and lose track of the ordinary ones. Especially when we're stressed."

My throat tightened. "Why are you telling me this?"

Eleanor's eyes were devastating—full of understanding.

"Because I think something happened to you here. Something that affected you. And I think we need to figure out what. Together."

It was the "together" that broke me.

I'd come to Thornewood to expose her. To catch a killer. To be the investigator, the truth-seeker, the hero.

But if she was right—if I'd been here before and somehow had no idea—then everything I thought I knew was wrong.

The evidence I'd been collecting. The investigation. The certainty that Eleanor was hiding something dangerous.

What if I was the one hiding from the truth?

I sat down hard in the desk chair. "How long have you known?"

"Known what?"

"That something was wrong with me. That I was..." I couldn't finish.

Eleanor knelt in front of me, took my hands. Her touch was gentle but her eyes were steel.

"I didn't know anything for certain until tonight. I suspected something was wrong, but I didn't understand what." She squeezed my hands. "Josh, whatever happened in 2020—we'll figure it out. Someone went to a lot of trouble to make you think you were here."

"What if I was here? What if that's the one thing I'm remembering correctly?"

"Then we figure out what happened. What it means. But we do it together."

Her voice made me look up. Meet her eyes. See the pain there that mirrored mine.

"What's driving you?" I asked softly. "I can see it. Something eating at you. What is it?"

Eleanor's expression cracked. Just for a second. Then she pulled herself together with visible effort.

"That's a conversation for daylight. When we're both thinking clearly."

She stood, pulled her coat tighter. "Get some sleep, Josh. Tomorrow we start figuring this out properly. No more investigating each other. No more hiding. Just truth."

She left before I could ask what truth she was hiding.

I sat in the library until dawn, turning the glove over in my hands, trying to remember the months that weren't there.

The fire burned down to ash. The house settled. And outside, in the cottage across the lawn, Eleanor's light stayed on until morning.

Both of us awake.

Both of us terrified.

Both of us starting to realize that whatever we'd come here to find, we were finding something far worse.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 10: THE COST
## November 14 — Eleanor

---

I stood in the shower for forty minutes the morning after the storm.

The water ran cold. Then colder. Trying to wash away his hands on my skin. The memory of his voice in my ear.

I'd gotten close to him. Closer than I should have. Closer than was safe for either of us.

Somewhere in the night, I'd stopped pretending. And that was dangerous.

I got dressed mechanically. Flannel shirt. Jeans. Boots. The clothes of someone normal. Someone who wasn't doing what I was doing. My hand trembled as I buttoned the shirt—the cold made my fingers clumsy. I clenched my fist, waited for the shaking to stop, and didn't think about why it was getting worse.

The loft loomed heavy above me. Everything I'd prepared. Everything I needed to finish this.

I didn't go up. Couldn't face it. Not yet.

Instead, I made coffee. And I waited for him to wake. Knowing that whatever happened next would change everything.

---

The confrontation in the library was inevitable. He had found the notebook. My notes. Careless of me.

He confronted me with proof of my watching. His voice was flat. Hurt underneath the anger. I'd wounded him. The flicker of guilt surprised me.

"You've been watching me," he said.

"Yes."

"Why?"

"Because I'm scared," I said. "After what happened here in 2020, I needed to know who was on the property. If they were... safe."

"Did you know them? The victim?"

"I knew of them," I said. "It was a tragedy."

He didn't believe me. Accused me of lying. His paranoia was rising, and I couldn't blame him. I was lying. Just not about what he thought.

"Who was the victim to you, Eleanor?" he demanded.

I met his eyes. Let him see something real. "Someone who got in my way," I whispered. "And I don't let that happen twice."

He softened then. He was trying to understand me—trying to decide if I was dangerous or broken. The answer depended on who was asking.

I brought him to the tower room. Watched his face as he looked around. Waited for fear to flicker across his features.

He felt something. I could tell. But he buried it quickly.

"M+L 2020," he read from the carving on the windowsill. "Who's M? And who's L?"

Nothing. Just confusion in his face. Still blind to the danger he was in.

"I don't know," I said quietly. "I've always wondered."

He stared at the carving for a long moment, something working behind his eyes. Then he shook his head and turned away.

I left him there and walked back to the cottage. My thoughts were screaming now. The roads would close soon. Winter would seal us in. Everything would come to a head.

In the loft, I sat at my desk. Opened my journal.

**November 14.**

*He found my notes. Let him wonder. Let him worry.*

*The tower room. He found it and suspected nothing. No wariness. No caution. He has no idea what's coming. He walks right into my space and trusts me completely.*

*The roads will close soon. He'll be trapped here with me. And then I finish what I came to do.*

I closed the journal. The storm had passed. The world outside was silent and white.

Soon the roads would close. Then there would be no escape for either of us.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 11: FRACTURES
## November 15-17 — Josh

---

I woke to the sound of my own voice.

Not speaking. Humming. A low, tuneless melody I didn't recognize, coming from somewhere in the darkness of my room. I lay frozen in bed, listening, trying to orient myself to the space between sleep and waking where nothing made sense yet.

The humming stopped.

I sat up. The room was empty. Moonlight through the windows painted everything in shades of silver and shadow. The fireplace had burned down to coals. My laptop was open on the desk, screen dark.

I'd been dreaming. Had to be. People heard things in old houses, especially houses like Thornewood where every board groaned and settled with its own particular voice.

But the sound had been so clear. My voice, humming something I didn't know.

I checked my phone: 3:47 AM. I'd gone to bed at midnight. Three and a half hours of sleep, if I'd slept at all. The insomnia was getting worse.

I rubbed my eyes. The insomnia had gotten worse this past week—barely four hours of sleep, and those were restless. Stress, probably.

I'd been trying melatonin, but it wasn't helping much. Nothing seemed to help anymore.

I'd stopped taking it three days ago, to be safe. But the symptoms hadn't improved yet. Maybe they took time to clear from your system.

And thinking about Eleanor constantly. That didn't help either.

I got out of bed, pulled on jeans and a sweater. If I was awake, I might as well work. The insomnia had become productive, at least. Some of my best investigation happened in the dead hours when the rational mind loosened its grip and connections appeared that daylight obscured.

I opened my laptop. The screen flickered to life, and I stared at the document I'd apparently been working on before bed. My notes on Eleanor, organized into categories: Inconsistencies. Evasions. Lies.

The list had grown long.

It was one thing to have suspicions; it was another to have evidence. I was a journalist. I dealt in facts. And the most basic fact of all—who Eleanor Moore even was—was a complete blank. Last week, I’d spent fifty dollars on an online background check service, the kind that scrapes public records, social media, and employment histories. I’d plugged in the name and date of birth she’d given me when I’d jokingly asked to see her ID to prove she was old enough to drink the whiskey I’d offered.

The report came back in minutes. And it was a ghost.

No licensed groundskeeper named Eleanor Moore in the state of Oregon. No one with that name and birthdate in any public database I could find. No social media, no credit history, no digital footprint whatsoever. It was as if she hadn't existed before walking onto the grounds of Thornewood Estate. The only hits were for an Eleanor Moore in Ohio who was sixty-seven years old, and a teenager in Florida.

She was a ghost. A complete fabrication. The woman I was having dinner with, the woman whose hands I’d held, wasn’t real. The thought sent a chill through me that had nothing to do with the house's draftiness. This wasn't just evasion. This was a professional-grade false identity.

Eleanor claimed six months at Thornewood, but the previous groundskeeper had left seven months ago according to Tom Greaves. One month unaccounted for. When I asked her about it casually—over coffee I made myself, being careful about that—she shrugged and said she must have misremembered, that time moved differently up here. Plausible. But the way she'd avoided my eyes suggested otherwise.

She knew the estate too well. Knew which windows stuck, which floorboards creaked, which rooms held the cold longest. That kind of knowledge took years, not months. I'd documented seventeen instances of her navigating the mansion in the dark without hesitation, never once fumbling for a light switch or misjudging a doorway.

And then there was the locked room in her cottage.

I'd tried the door twice now when she'd gone to town for supplies. Solid core, professional deadbolt, no window. When I'd asked what she kept in there—casual, investigative journalist curiosity—she'd stepped close, brushed her hand along my jaw, and murmured, "Everyone needs their secrets, Josh. Don't you?" Before I could press, she'd changed the subject to my research, asking pointed questions about my work until I'd lost my train of thought entirely.

During my second visit to the cottage—twenty minutes while she drove to town for groceries—I'd cataloged everything I could. Nature guides and repair manuals on the bookshelf, like I'd noticed before. Psychology texts poorly hidden behind them. A laptop she kept locked in a drawer.

And on her desk: art restoration supplies. At least, that's what they appeared to be. A UV flashlight, the kind used to detect repairs in antique documents. Chemical solutions in brown bottles with labels like "Ferric Chloride" and "Tannic Acid"—compounds used to artificially age paper and ink. Cotton swabs, gloves, a magnifying glass with built-in light.

She'd claimed she was helping restore some of the estate's damaged books when I'd noticed a conservation guide on her shelf. Plausible. The library had plenty of volumes that needed attention—water damage, foxing, cracked bindings. But coupled with the locked room and the psychology texts, it seemed like another layer of deception.

Or I was becoming paranoid. Document restoration was a legitimate hobby. The UV light could be for detecting forgeries, checking paper authenticity. Perfectly innocent.

Except nothing about Eleanor seemed innocent anymore.

I photographed everything. The receipt for the aging compounds dated back six months—right when Eleanor started working here. Before I'd even arrived.

If she'd been planning this since before we met, how much of what I'd "found" had actually been planted? The torn guest book pages could have been removed to hide her own presence here. The photograph could be doctored. How hard would it be to create "evidence" of a visit I never made?

Storage didn't require a deadbolt that heavy.

I added another note to my file: *Locked room - PRIORITY - need access.*

My cursor blinked at me. I had pages of documentation. Hours of recorded conversations. Surveillance footage from the cameras I'd hidden throughout the estate. But I still didn't have proof. Not the kind that would hold up in court or even in an article. Just a growing certainty that Eleanor was lying about everything, and that the people who'd vanished weren't random victims of Thornewood's isolation.

They were her victims.

I had to prove it.

---

**Saturday, November 16 — Morning**

I was splitting firewood behind the mansion when Roy pulled up in his truck.

The handyman came by every couple of weeks to check the heating system, clear gutters, handle repairs Eleanor couldn't manage alone. He was in his fifties, grizzled, with the weathered look of someone who'd spent his life working outdoors.

He climbed out of the truck, nodded at me. "Morning."

"Hey, Roy."

He pulled a toolbox from the truck bed, then paused. Looked at me for a long moment. Like he was deciding something.

"Can I ask you something?" he said.

"Sure."

Roy glanced toward the cottage. Eleanor's car wasn't there—she'd gone to town for supplies. We were alone.

"You been here what, three, four weeks now?"

"About that, yeah."

"You planning to stay the whole six months?"

I set down the axe. "That's the plan. Why?"

Roy shifted his weight. Uncomfortable. "Look, it's probably nothing. But I feel like I should mention it."

"Mention what?"

"The last person who stayed here. Another guy renting the place, like you. Name was David Park. He left real sudden. Middle of the night. Didn't even take all his stuff."

David Park. I knew that name. He was one of the cases I'd been researching. Disappeared in August 2022. The police report said he'd last been seen near Cascade trails, but no one had connected him to Thornewood.

Until now.

The abandoned campsite I'd found in the woods flashed through my mind. The wallet, the keys, the phone left behind.

"I found an abandoned campsite in the eastern woods," I said. "Tent, sleeping bag, wallet with ID still in it. You know anything about that?"

Roy's face changed. "That was David's. He liked to camp out there sometimes—said the mansion felt too closed in. When he vanished, Helen and I found his stuff a few days later. Figured he'd come back for it." He shook his head. "Never did."

So the campsite had been David Park's. Someone who'd come here to stay, to write, and never left.

"When was this?" I kept my voice calm.

"Two years ago. August 2022, maybe September. Hard to remember exact."

August 2022. Right when David Park vanished.

"Did he say why he was leaving?"

Roy shook his head. "Never saw him. I came by the next morning to fix the cottage door—it sticks in humidity—and the caretaker back then—Helen, not Eleanor—said he'd packed up and left overnight. Got a call about a family emergency, couldn't even wait till morning. Eleanor wasn't here yet. She only started in the spring."

"And you didn't find that strange?"

"Sure I did. But people do weird things. Maybe he got spooked living alone out here." Roy paused. "But there was more."

"What?"

"His room. I went in to check if the door worked now that he wasn't using it. There was blood on the sheets. Not a lot, but enough. Helen said he'd gotten a nosebleed, woke up disoriented, decided to leave."

My blood ran cold. "And you believed her?"

Roy met my eyes. "At the time, yeah. Now?" He shrugged. "I don't know what to think. But I'm telling you because..." He trailed off.

"Because what?"

"Because Eleanor's been different the past couple months. Since you arrived. She's... I don't know how to describe it. More focused. Like she's been anticipating."

"Waiting for what?"

"That's what worries me." Roy picked up his toolbox. "I'm probably just being paranoid. Living alone makes people strange, and this place has a history. But I thought you should know. About David Park. About the blood."

"Roy, if you thought something was wrong, why didn't you call the police?"

"And tell them what? That a guy left in the middle of the night and maybe got a nosebleed? Helen's explanation made sense. Still does, mostly." He started toward the mansion. "I'm just saying... be careful. And maybe don't mention I told you this."

"Why not?"

Roy stopped. Looked back at me. "Because Eleanor's got secrets, Josh. Everyone knows it. And people with secrets don't like it when you start asking questions about what happened to the last person who stayed here."

He walked to the mansion without another word.

I stood there, axe in hand, processing what he'd told me.

David Park had been here. At Thornewood. Had stayed in the mansion, as I was.

And then he'd vanished.

The old caretaker had explained it as a family emergency. A nosebleed. Nothing suspicious.

But David Park was on the missing persons list. Had never been found. And the last place anyone could confirm he'd been was here, at Thornewood Estate—two years before Eleanor even arrived.

I looked toward the cottage. Empty now. But she'd be back soon.

I needed to search David Park's old room. See if there was any evidence left. Any sign of what really happened two years ago.

Because Roy was right about one thing: people with secrets didn't like questions.

And I'd been asking a lot of questions.

---

A sound from downstairs made me freeze. Footsteps. Slow and deliberate, crossing the main floor toward the library.

Eleanor? At four in the morning?

I moved to my window, looked out toward the cottage. Lights off. No smoke from the chimney. She wasn't there.

The footsteps continued. I could track them by sound—across the great room, past the dining room, into the library. Then silence.

I should stay in my room. Lock the door. Wait until morning.

Instead, I grabbed my phone, pulled up the camera app, and headed downstairs.

The mansion was colder at night, the kind of cold that came up through the floors and settled into your bones. I descended the main staircase carefully, avoiding the third step that groaned, keeping to the edges where the wood was more solid.

The library door stood open. Firelight flickered inside—someone had rebuilt the fire.

I stopped in the doorway.

Eleanor stood at the bookshelf, her back to me, reaching toward the upper shelf. She was dressed, not in sleep clothes. Jeans and a sweater and her work boots. Like she'd been awake for hours.

"Couldn't sleep?" I said.

She turned, and for a fraction of a second, an expression flickered across her face. Surprise? Fear? It vanished before I could name it.

"Jesus, Josh. You scared me." Her voice was steady but her hand had moved to her chest, palm flat over her heart. "What are you doing up?"

"I could ask you the same thing."

"Insomnia." She pulled a book from the shelf—*Birds of the Pacific Northwest*. "Thought I'd read for a bit. The cottage was too quiet."

I watched her face. She was lying. I was almost certain. But about what?

"You rebuilt the fire," I said.

"It was dying. Seemed a shame to waste the coals." She moved to the leather sofa near the fireplace, settled into the corner like she owned it. "Want to join me? Or are you going to stand there interrogating me about my reading habits?"

There was challenge in her voice. Amusement, even. Like she knew exactly what I was thinking and found it entertaining.

I should go back upstairs. Maintain distance. Professional boundaries.

I crossed to the sofa and sat.

She smiled. "Can't sleep either?"

"Headache," I said. Which was true. The pain behind my eye had sharpened, a spike of pressure that made me want to close my eyes and press my palms against my skull.

"You get those a lot lately." Not a question. An observation.

How did she know that? I'd been working in the library, alone, when most of them hit. Unless she'd been watching. Or unless she knew exactly when they'd occur.

"You've been watching me?"

"I pay attention." She set the book aside, shifted to face me. "You should see a doctor. Headaches like that—stress or something worse."

Stress. Or chemical withdrawal. The headaches had actually gotten worse since I'd started making my own meals, refusing the coffee she brought. Withdrawal symptoms matched the timeline.

"I'm fine."

"You're not fine. You look exhausted. You're losing weight. And you wander around at night like you can't bear to be still." Her hand reached toward my face, hesitated, then touched my jaw gently. "What are you running from, Josh?"

The question landed like a punch. I pulled back.

"I'm not running from anything."

"Everyone who comes to Thornewood is running from something." Her voice had gone soft. Intimate. "I'm running. You're running. The question is what happens when we stop."

I should press her. Ask what she's running from. Investigative instinct said this was an opening, a moment of vulnerability I could exploit.

But her hand was still near my face, and the firelight softened her green eyes to something almost warm, and the question dissolved into silence.

"Eleanor," I said. Her name was dangerous in my mouth.

She kissed me.

It wasn't like the first kiss had been—tentative, questioning, testing boundaries. This was deliberate. Her hand slid into my hair, pulled me closer, and I stopped thinking in investigator terms entirely.

She tasted like tea and woodsmoke. Her body was warm against mine, and when I pulled her into my lap she came willingly, straddling me, her weight solid and real and grounding.

This was a terrible idea. I was investigating her. She might be a serial killer. Every professional instinct I had screamed that I was compromising everything.

But her mouth was on my neck now, and my hands had found the hem of her sweater, and rational thought was drowning in the feel of her skin.

"Upstairs?" she whispered against my ear.

I should say no. Should maintain objectivity. Should—

"Yes."

---

**Sunday, November 17 — Morning**

I woke alone.

Sunlight streamed through the windows.

I sat up too fast. The room tilted.

I was in my bed. Fully dressed. Jeans still buttoned, boots still on. The last thing I clearly remembered: Eleanor's mouth on mine, her hands in my hair, the sofa in the library.

Had we come upstairs? Had something happened?

My head was splitting open. The pain was blinding, white-hot behind both eyes. I stumbled to the bathroom, fumbled for aspirin, choked down four with water from the tap.

The mirror showed a stranger. Hollow-eyed. Pale. Days of stubble darkening my jaw.

I checked my phone: 11:23 AM. Late morning. Must have fallen asleep after Eleanor left.

I splashed water on my face. The insomnia was getting worse. Or maybe it was the bourbon. The stress of the investigation.

I needed to check the footage.

---

The cameras I'd hidden throughout the estate were motion-activated, saving to a hard drive in my closet. I pulled up the files, sorted by timestamp, found last night's recordings.

Library camera, 4:03 AM: Me entering the frame. Eleanor on the sofa, turning toward me. Our conversation—mouths moving but no audio. Then the kiss.

I fast-forwarded.

4:17 AM: Still on the sofa. Her in my lap. My hands under her sweater.

4:31 AM: Standing. Eleanor leading me toward the door. My hand in hers.

Then the camera angle changed—hallway camera, second floor.

4:38 AM: Us climbing the stairs. She was smiling. I was—

I leaned closer to the screen.

The camera angle was poor, the lighting dim. My face was in shadow, hard to read. I looked tired, maybe. Distracted. The way anyone might look at 4 AM after drinking.

Eleanor glanced back at me, checking if I was following. Then she stepped ahead, leading the way.

The footage continued. We reached my bedroom door. Eleanor spoke—her mouth moved, her eyes watching my face. Then she stepped back, let go of my hand, and walked away down the hall.

And I stood there.

Just stood in the doorway for—I checked the timestamp—twelve minutes. Staring at nothing.

Then I walked into my room and closed the door.

Next camera: interior room camera, hidden in the bookshelf.

4:52 AM: Me entering frame. Moving to the desk. Opening the laptop. Sitting down.

And typing.

For three hours, I typed.

The camera angle caught me from the side, mostly in shadow. Hard to make out details. I sat still, focused on the screen. Typing. But the footage quality was grainy, the lighting poor—I couldn't see my face clearly enough to read my expression.

At 7:43 AM, I stopped. Closed the laptop. Walked to the bed. Lay down fully clothed.

And didn't move for four more hours.

I stared at the footage. Rewound it. Three hours of typing. But what had I been writing? And why couldn't I remember any of it?

I opened my laptop with shaking hands.

The document was titled: **TIMELINE - NOVEMBER 1-7.**

The contents were meticulous. Dates, times, locations. My movements cross-referenced with Eleanor's. Weather conditions. Moon phases. Detailed observations about the estate—measurements of rooms, distances between buildings, sightlines from various windows.

It read like surveillance notes. Like someone documenting a target.

But the handwriting—the typing style, I mean—wasn't mine. Too formal. Too precise. Sentences structured differently than I normally wrote.

I scrolled down.

More notes. About Eleanor specifically. Height, weight, build. Estimated strength. Weaknesses—she favored her left ankle when tired, suggesting an old injury. She locked her cottage door but the windows were old and could be forced. She ran every morning at dawn, always the same route, always alone.

My hands were shaking so badly I could barely control the trackpad.

This wasn't investigation. This was—

The word that came to mind was *targeting.*

I closed the laptop. Pushed it away like it might bite.

Someone had written this. During those hours I couldn't account for, someone had documented Eleanor like prey. Measured her vulnerabilities. Cataloged her routines. Planned—

But who?

Eleanor had access to my laptop. She could have created this file herself, typed it while I slept, planted it to make me think I was losing my mind. The footage showed someone at my desk, but the angle was bad, the lighting worse. Could that have been her? Same build, similar height when seated. She'd have my login password—she'd watched me type it enough times.

Or was it me? Had I really spent three hours writing this and somehow blocked it out completely?

I was going to be sick.

I needed to talk to Eleanor.

---

The cottage was dark when I crossed the grounds. Snow had started falling—the first real snow of the season, fat flakes drifting down through gray afternoon light.

I knocked. No answer.

Tried the door. Locked.

"Eleanor?" I called. "I need to talk to you."

Silence.

I walked around the building, checking windows. All locked. But the back window—the one that looked into her main room—showed a light coming from somewhere. The loft, maybe. The space above her bedroom.

The locked room.

I could force the window. The frames were old, wood swollen with moisture. One good shove and—

What was I doing? Breaking and entering? Violating her privacy?

Finding the truth. Getting answers. That was the justification.

I reached for the window frame, testing it. Started to apply pressure—

"Looking for something?"

I spun around.

Eleanor stood ten feet away, arms crossed, watching me with an expression I couldn't read. Not anger. Not surprise. Something colder. More calculating.

"I—" My mind raced for an explanation. "I was looking for you. You weren't answering."

"So you were going to break into my cottage?"

"The window was already loose. I was just—"

"Don't." She cut me off. "Don't insult me by lying badly, Josh. You're better than that."

We stared at each other across the snowy ground. The cottage light behind her cast her face in shadow, but her posture was clear—ready, coiled, like she was prepared for me to do something violent.

Like she knew what I was.

"What's in the locked room?" I asked. No point pretending anymore.

"Storage."

"Bullshit. Storage doesn't need a deadbolt that heavy. Storage doesn't require you to keep it locked every second you're away from the cottage." I stepped toward her. "What are you hiding, Eleanor?"

"What are you hiding, Josh?"

"I'm not—" I stopped. Started again. "I saw you that first day. From the tower. You were crushing pills in your cottage. Putting them in your coffee."

Her expression didn't change. "And?"

"And I've felt drowsy. After eating meals you've made. After drinking your coffee. The stew two nights ago—I fell asleep for seven hours. Lost the whole evening."

"You're exhausted, Josh. You barely sleep. You work yourself into the ground. Of course you crash when you finally eat a decent meal."

"Is it exhaustion? Or are you drugging me?"

The words hung between us. Snow fell around us, silent and steady.

Eleanor's face went very still. "You think I'm drugging you."

"I think something's not adding up. The drowsiness. The memory gaps. The way you watch me sometimes like you're waiting for something to happen."

"The pills you saw me crushing," she said slowly, carefully, "are pain medication. For migraines. I crush them because they work faster that way."

Her face was unreadable. But her eyes were bright with what might have been tears or fury.

I told Eleanor I needed to drive into town for supplies.

Three days of wondering if Eleanor's migraine explanation was real or the most elegant lie I'd ever been told.

The clinic in Brightwood was forty minutes down the mountain. I'd found it online during one of my late-night research sessions—small, understaffed, the kind of place that wouldn't ask too many questions about why a writer from Portland needed an urgent toxicology screen.

The nurse who drew my blood was efficient and disinterested. "Results will take a few days," she said, wrapping the tourniquet around my arm. "We'll call when they're ready."

"Can you rush it? I'll pay extra."

She looked up at me then, really looked. Saw whatever desperation was written on my face.

"Two days," she said quietly. "That's the best I can do."

I waited. Went through the motions of normal life. Chopped wood with Eleanor. Made coffee. Pretended the test wasn't happening. Pretended I wasn't terrified of what it might show.

"Mr. Caine? This is Brightwood Clinic with your test results."

"Yes." My voice came out rough. "What did you find?"

A pause. Papers rustling. "The toxicology screen came back... complicated. We found trace amounts of sleep aids—melatonin, diphenhydramine. But there's also an anomaly. An organic compound we couldn't fully identify with our standard panel. Could be a medication metabolite, could be herbal, could be environmental. We'd need more specialized testing to say for certain."

My chest tightened. "What kind of compound?"

"That's the thing—it's not on our standard drug screen. The molecular signature suggests it might be plant-based, possibly a natural sedative or anxiolytic. Or it could be completely benign. Your liver function shows slight elevation, which could indicate processing of something unusual, but nothing definitively toxic."

"So someone could be drugging me?"

"I can't rule it out. But I also can't confirm it. The levels of everything we found are low. If someone were drugging you, they'd be doing it very carefully, with substances that metabolize quickly. Or—" she paused, clearly choosing words carefully, "—you could be experiencing symptoms from stress, lack of sleep, and mild over-the-counter medication use."

"But I've been experiencing severe drowsiness. Lost time. I thought—"

"Have you been taking anything for insomnia?" the nurse interrupted gently. "The diphenhydramine levels suggest regular use."

"I..." Had I? I couldn't remember. "Maybe. Sometimes. When it gets bad."

"That would explain it. Combined with stress, irregular sleep patterns, possible depression—those symptoms are consistent with what you're describing. I'd recommend seeing your regular physician for a full evaluation. Maybe a sleep study."

I thanked her. Ended the call. Stared at my phone.

The results proved nothing. Could be drugging. Could be stress. Could be an unidentified plant-based compound Eleanor was slipping into my food. Or could be my own paranoia manifesting as physical symptoms.

The ambiguity was almost worse than confirmation. At least if I knew for certain, I could act. This left me suspended between possibilities, unable to trust my own perceptions.

Was Eleanor drugging me with something sophisticated enough to evade standard testing? Or was I losing my mind?

But also nothing that proved she wasn't. Trace amounts could mean I was taking medication I didn't remember taking. Or it could mean someone was giving me small, careful doses—just enough to make me drowsy, not enough to show up definitively in a blood test days later.

The results proved nothing. Made me trust my own memory even less.

I sat there in the library, phone in hand, and realized the test had accomplished exactly the opposite of what I'd hoped. Instead of answers, I had more questions. Instead of proof, I had ambiguity.

And the drowsiness—the exhaustion that had driven me to get tested in the first place—pressed heavier than ever, like water, like snow, like the weight of all the things I couldn't remember and might never understand.

I looked at my hands. Wondered if they'd taken medication I didn't recall. Wondered what else I was forgetting.

Outside, I heard Eleanor's boots on the porch. Her key in the lock.

I closed my eyes and pretended to be sleeping when she found me on the library couch twenty minutes later. Pretended the test had never happened. Pretended I knew who I was and what I was capable of.

But the envelope with the test results stayed hidden in my jacket pocket, and every time I touched it, the same cold certainty gripped me: something was very, very wrong.

And a blood test couldn't tell me if the danger was Eleanor or if it was me.

---

My head exploded in white light. I was falling. Consciousness slipped away like water through my fingers.

The last thing I thought before the darkness took me:

*What am I?*

Then nothing.

Just the void.

Just the darkness where the rest of my life should have been.

I stood, stiff from sitting too long in the cold. Four hours of sleep a night in a freezing cottage, the constant stress, the anxiety of maintaining the lie—it was taking its toll. I needed to finish this before Josh figured out what I was really doing here.

I climbed down from the loft, careful on the ladder, testing each rung before committing weight. The cottage was dark below, still, the main room exactly as I'd left it. Josh had been here once, early November, a casual visit—but I'd watched him study everything, his eyes cataloging every detail. I'd made sure nothing personal was visible. Nothing that would give me away.

Cold. Fire burned down to ash. I rebuilt it methodically: crumpled newspaper, kindling, split logs arranged in careful architecture. Watched the flame catch and spread. Heat radiating outward, pushing back the December chill, the five AM darkness.

I made fresh coffee. Stood at the kitchen window while it brewed, watching the mansion. Lights off except Josh's bedroom on the second floor—still asleep, still unaware. Peaceful-looking. The kind of face you'd trust.

I wondered what he dreamed about. If the nightmares came for him too. If his sleep was as broken as mine.

The coffee finished. I poured it, black, hot enough to burn. Took it to the window. Watched the mansion. Watched the snow fall. Watched the world turn white and pristine and false, everything ugly buried under beauty's disguise.

My phone sat silent on the counter. No messages. No one checking in. I'd told everyone I needed space, needed time alone to heal. They'd stopped asking months ago.

I was alone with this. Completely alone.

Maybe that was better. No one to see what I was becoming. No one to ask questions I couldn't answer. No one to tell me to stop, to come home, to let it go.

Because I couldn't let it go. Couldn't stop. Not until this was finished.

This was my obsession. This was my purpose. This was the only thing that mattered.

I finished the coffee. Poured a second cup. Stood at the window as the sky gradually lightened, charcoal to slate to pale gray. Snowfall thinning. The mansion emerging from darkness like a ship from fog.

Seven thirty-two AM. I saw movement in Josh's window. Curtain shifting. He was awake.

I set down the coffee. Changed into work clothes—flannel, jeans, boots. Braided my hair back. Checked my reflection in the bathroom mirror.

Smile. Practice the smile. Warm, easy, uncomplicated.

Good enough.

I stepped out into the December morning. Cold bit through my flannel immediately. I'd need a heavier coat soon, the winter parka I'd bought in October—soon the temperature would stay below freezing and the snow would measure in feet instead of inches.

Crossed the hundred yards between cottage and mansion. Snow crunching under my boots. My breath visible, hanging in the air. Peaceful. Beautiful. Deceptive, maybe.

I opened the mansion's back door—always unlocked, as it should be since I worked here—and stepped into the kitchen.

Josh was already there.

Making coffee, back to me, wearing the soft gray henley I liked, the one that clung to his shoulders in a way that made my body respond despite all my better judgment. Despite everything I still didn't understand about him.

He turned when he heard me. Smiled. The charming, slightly crooked smile that transformed his face. Josh's smile, not that other expression I'd glimpsed once—empty and mechanical, like a mask that had slipped. Genuine warmth in his eyes.

"Morning," he said. "I made extra coffee."

"You're a saint." The words came automatically. I poured myself a cup from his pot, added cream, took a sip. "Sleep well?"

"Actually, yeah." He leaned against the counter, studying me. "You?"

"Like the dead," I said, and the words hung in the air between us, heavy with meanings he couldn't hear.

He laughed. Took a sip of his own coffee. "Got plans today?"

"Usual. Check the generator, split some firewood, make sure the pipes are winterized." I met his eyes over the rim of my cup. "You?"

"Research. Writing." He gestured vaguely toward the library. "The usual."

*The usual. Your research. Your writing. Whatever you're really doing.*

"Sounds productive," I said.

We stood in comfortable silence, drinking coffee, and I watched him the way I always watched him now—searching for signs of whatever I'd seen that morning. Any hint of wrongness.

Nothing. Just Josh. Just the man who made me coffee and smiled like he knew me.

The man I wasn't sure I knew at all.

He set down his cup. Crossed the space between us in three steps. Pulled me close, gentle, giving me space to refuse. I didn't refuse. Couldn't make myself. I let him kiss me, let him taste like coffee and mint toothpaste, let his hands find my waist, let my body respond the way it always did despite all the confusion in my head.

"Stay," he whispered against my neck. "For breakfast. We could make pancakes."

*I should say no. I should keep some distance. I should figure out what I'm doing before this goes any further.*

"Pancakes sound good," I said.

He smiled. Kissed me again. Turned to the cupboard to find ingredients, and I watched his back, the slope of his shoulders, the easy way he moved through the kitchen.

Right now, in the December morning light, making pancakes with him, I could almost forget my doubts. Could almost pretend this was normal. Uncomplicated.

Almost.

"Eleanor?" Josh was looking at me, spatula in hand. "You okay? You looked a million miles away."

"Just tired," I said. "Long night."

"You should sleep more."

*I should. But sleep hasn't been coming easily.*

"I'll sleep when this is over," I said, trying to make it sound like a joke.

He laughed. Returned to the pancakes. And I stood in the kitchen of Thornewood Estate, drinking coffee and watching him, the weight of everything I still didn't understand pressing down.

Something was going to break soon. The pressure was building. The careful balance we'd been maintaining—the intimacy mixed with secrets, the connection built on things left unsaid—couldn't hold much longer.

I wasn't ready for it.

But it was coming anyway.

Josh plated the pancakes. Passed me one with butter and syrup. Smiled like this was simple.

It wasn't simple. Not for me.

But I smiled back anyway.

We ate in silence. The pancakes were good, but I barely tasted them. My mind was elsewhere—on all the ways I was losing control, all the feelings I couldn't afford, all the reasons I shouldn't be here doing this.

But I was here. And I couldn't leave. Not yet.

Not until I finished what I came to Thornewood to do. Why I'd trapped us here together.

The snow kept falling outside. Winter was closing in.

And it was going to break. Soon.

The pressure was unmistakable.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 13: THE DISCOVERY
## December 10 — Josh

---

**Earlier that day**

I needed to move my truck. The snow was coming down heavily now, and if I didn't relocate it to the cleared area near the mansion, I'd be snowed in completely by morning.

Eleanor had gone for a walk—I'd watched her head into the woods with her camera, said she wanted to photograph the first snow. She'd be gone at least an hour.

I grabbed my keys and headed out to my truck. I'd been parking near the cottage lately—closer to Eleanor, closer to the supplies she brought me, easier than the long walk from the mansion's circular drive. A small change I'd made without thinking, back when I still trusted her.

As I approached, I noticed Eleanor's car parked beside mine. The trunk was slightly ajar, not fully closed. A corner of something dark was visible.

I shouldn't look. I knew I shouldn't.

But I was an investigator. And she'd been lying about too many things.

I glanced toward the woods. No sign of Eleanor.

I opened her trunk.

The bag was black nylon, unzipped. Inside, supplies:

Rope. Thick, climbing-grade.
Duct tape. Industrial strength.
Plastic sheeting. Heavy-duty, the kind used for construction or...
Zip ties. Multiple packs.
A burner phone. Prepaid, untraceable.
Latex gloves. A whole box.
A folding shovel.

And underneath it all, wrapped in cloth—a handgun. Smith & Wesson 9mm. Loaded.

My hands shook as I pulled out my phone, took photos of everything. Documented it all.

This wasn't hiking gear. This wasn't groundskeeping supplies.

This was a kill kit.

Everything you'd need to subdue someone, transport a body, bury evidence, disappear without a trace.

The rope could restrain. The duct tape could silence. The plastic sheeting could contain blood spatter. The gloves would leave no fingerprints. The burner phone couldn't be traced. The handgun—God, the handgun—that was intent. Preparation. The willingness to kill if necessary.

And the shovel—

I thought about the disturbed earth I'd found on the logging road. The fresh grave.

My heart hammered. I carefully replaced everything exactly as I'd found it, closed the trunk to the same partially-open position.

Backed away from the car.

Eleanor had all of this. Ready. Prepared.

For what? For whom?

For me?

I walked back to the mansion on numb legs, my mind racing.

She'd been planning something. Had the materials ready. Was she planning to kill me? Stage it as an accident in the wilderness? Bury me somewhere on the vast Thornewood property where I'd never be found?

That night at dinner, Eleanor smiled at me across the table. Poured me wine. Asked how my day had been.

"Good," I lied. "Productive."

"I'm glad." She reached across the table, took my hand. "I worry about you, working so hard. You need to take care of yourself."

The same hands that had packed a kill kit in her trunk.

I smiled back. Squeezed her hand.

And wondered which of us would survive the winter.

Later that night, I went to my closet for a sweater. The cold had seeped into the mansion, and I couldn't stop shivering—though maybe that was the adrenaline finally wearing off.

I pulled open the closet door and froze.

There were two pairs of boots on the floor. My hiking boots, the ones I'd brought from Portland—brown leather, well-worn. And next to them, a pair I'd never seen before. Black. Muddy. The same size as mine, but caked with dried earth I didn't recognize.

I crouched down, studied them. The mud was reddish-brown, clay-heavy. Not the black forest soil around the mansion. This was from somewhere else. Somewhere I hadn't been.

Had Eleanor put them here? Was this part of whatever she was planning?

I photographed them but didn't touch them. Added it to my growing list of evidence. Another piece of the puzzle I couldn't yet solve.

---

**3:47 AM**

The library clock reads 3:47 AM when I give up on sleep entirely.

I've been staring at the ceiling for hours, unable to stop thinking about what I'd found in Eleanor's trunk. The kill kit. The supplies. The evidence that she was planning something.

The sound of wind hurling snow against my windows had become words: *Six names. Six attacks. Six locked doors.* The investigation is consuming me. The pattern that keeps almost-but-not-quite emerging. The sense that I'm missing something obvious, something that would make everything click into place if I could just see it.

I pull on jeans and the wool sweater Eleanor gave me—soft, smells faintly of cedar and her shampoo. The house holds cold like a grudge.

The mansion at night transforms. Shadows pool in corners, hardwood groans under my bare feet with intimate familiarity. Third step creaks left, seventh right. Small details I collect, catalog, store away.

Details I've always collected.

The library door is ajar. Must have left it open earlier. I've been distracted lately—the investigation consuming every thought. Yesterday Eleanor had looked at me strangely when I couldn't recall if I'd photographed the east woods yet or not.

"You okay?" she'd asked, hand on my arm, genuine concern in those green eyes that see too much.

"Fine," I'd lied. "Just working. You know how I get."

The fire has burned down to coals. I feed it new logs, watch flames hesitate before committing to consumption.

Twelve, fourteen hours a day in this room. My investigation covers every surface: timeline on the windows in grease pencil, photographs pinned to cork boards, file folders creating archaeology on the table. Eleanor says I'm obsessed. She's not wrong.

Except I'm no closer than I was two months ago.

Six names. Five dead and one survivor who'd never walk again, and the only common thread is the place itself and the gaps in documentation. One body recovered, one witness with no memory. No real evidence except absence, which is the hardest evidence to prove.

I circle the room, trailing fingers along book spines. Two stories of shelves connected by the wrought-iron rolling ladder I've mostly avoided. The books follow some private logic—medical texts next to poetry, history beside botany.

I've photographed every shelf, looking for what I couldn't say.

The ladder beckons.

I've been avoiding the upper shelves. Something about heights in the dark, maybe, or the way the ladder sways slightly, or how the second-floor shelves are deep in shadow even with the fire. But insomnia makes me brave, or stupid, or both.

The ladder rolls smooth, and I climb to the second-story shelves. Up here the books are older, leather bindings cracked, that smell of slow decay. Dust motes swim in moonlight through the snow-covered windows.

*Something about this feels familiar.*

The sensation surfaces without warning, and I grip the ladder hard. Which is absurd—I arrived October 22nd. I have documentation, proof.

But it persists. Just fatigue playing tricks. Tired neurons misfiring.

I shake it off and scan the spines. More poetry here: Plath, Sexton, Dickinson, Yeats. Someone had gothic tastes. Next to them, a collection of true crime—I recognize several I've read, one I wrote.

Nothing unusual. Just books.

I climb down, restless. The investigation is stalled. Six names, six attacks, and I'm no closer to understanding the pattern. Eleanor keeps telling me to rest, to stop pushing so hard, but how can I when the answer seems so close?

My phone buzzes. Text from Eleanor: *You up? Saw light in library.*

I stare at it for a moment before responding: *Can't sleep. Too much coffee.*

Three dots appear, then: *Want company? I'm awake too.*

*Sure. Come over.*

Five minutes later, the library doors open and Eleanor walks in, wearing sweatpants and my old Syracuse hoodie that I lent her weeks ago and haven't gotten back. Hair pulled into a messy bun. She looks soft, sleepy, concerned.

"Josh," she says. "It's almost four in the morning. You okay?"

"Just working." I gesture at my spread of notes and photographs covering the reading table. "Can't turn my brain off."

She moves closer, studying my timeline. The names. The dates. The gaps in the pattern I'm trying to fill.

"You're going to burn yourself out," she says quietly.

"Maybe. Probably." I rub my eyes. "But I feel like I'm missing something obvious. Something that would make it all click into place."

Eleanor is quiet for a moment, looking at my notes. Then she reaches out, hand on my arm. "Come here."

She pulls me away from the table, over to the fireplace where the coals are dying down. Sits on the rug, pats the space next to her. I sink down, suddenly aware of how exhausted I am.

"Tell me about it," she says. "Not the investigation. You. Why this case? Why push yourself this hard?"

I lean back against the stone hearth, the residual warmth against my back. "Because if I don't... who will? These people disappeared and nobody cares. The authorities closed the cases. The families gave up. Someone has to remember them."

"And it has to be you?"

"It's what I do. What I've always done."

Eleanor shifts closer, her shoulder against mine. We sit in comfortable silence for a while, watching the fire die.

"My mother was murdered when I was three," I say finally. Words I rarely share. "I don't remember any of it. Foster care after that, then the Caines adopted me. They were good people. Gave me a real childhood. But I always wondered what happened to her—who did it, why. That's when I started investigating other people's tragedies. Easier than facing my own, I guess."

"I'm sorry."

"It's ancient history now." I close my eyes. "But that's how I learned to investigate. Following stories about missing people, cold cases. Trying to give voice to the dead. I became obsessed with it."

"Did you find anything?"

"No. Nothing that held up. But I learned how to investigate. How to follow a story. How to not let go." I open my eyes, look at her. I can see the concern in her expression, that soft green gaze that sees too much. "That's what scares me. I don't know how to let go. Even when I should."

She takes my hand. Squeezes it. "You'll solve this. But not tonight. Not at four AM when you haven't slept in—how long?"

"Twenty-four hours. Maybe more."

"Come on." She stands, pulls me up. "You're going to sleep. Actually sleep. I'll stay if you want. Just to make sure you don't sneak back down here."

I laugh despite myself. "You'd do that?"

"Someone has to save you from yourself."

She walks me upstairs to my room. In the hallway, gray dawn light is starting to filter through the snow-covered windows. She looks younger in this light. Softer.

"You'll actually sleep?" she asks at my door.

"I'll try."

"Josh."

"I promise."

She nods. Then, unexpectedly, she steps forward and hugs me. Brief, tight, warm. She smells like woodsmoke and cedar soap and something underneath that's purely her.

"You scare me sometimes," she says into my shoulder.

"I scare myself," I admit.

She pulls back, hands on my arms, looking at me seriously. "Get some rest. The investigation will still be there tomorrow."

She leaves, and I hear her footsteps on the stairs, the front door opening and closing, the crunch of her boots on snow as she walks back to the cottage. I watch from my window until I see lights come on, until I know she's safe inside.

I turn back to my room, my mind a chaotic buzz. I should sleep. I promised her I would.

I lie down, still fully clothed, and pull the covers up to my chin. The bed is cold. The room is silent except for the wind. Sleep feels miles away. I close my eyes, trying to force my brain into submission.

Minutes stretch into an hour. I drift in that frustrating space between wakefulness and sleep, a gray twilight of half-formed thoughts and anxieties.

Eventually, exhaustion wins. I sleep.

---

**The next afternoon**

I woke around noon with a clearer head. No drowsiness. No confusion. Just the normal grogginess of sleeping too late after staying up too late.

Eleanor had texted: *Let you sleep. Working on the south fence. I'll bring lunch around 1?*

I stared at the message for a long time before responding: *Already ate. Thanks though.*

It was a lie. My stomach was empty, growling. But I needed to know. Needed to test whether the drowsiness pattern was real or just my paranoid imagination.

An hour later, there was a knock. Eleanor stood in the doorway with a covered dish.

"I brought soup anyway," she said. "Thought you might be hungry. You didn't eat much yesterday."

The smell was incredible—chicken and vegetables, homemade bread. My stomach twisted with hunger.

"That's really thoughtful," I said, not taking the dish. "But I'm actually not hungry. Had a big brunch."

She looked at me strangely. "Josh, your kitchen's been cold all day. I checked the stove when I came in. You haven't cooked."

"Protein bar," I said. "From my stash."

We stared at each other. The dish sat between us like a challenge.

"Are you..." She paused. "Are you avoiding my food?"

"No. I just—I'm not hungry right now."

"Because you think I'm drugging you." Not a question. A statement. Her voice flat.

"I didn't say that."

"You didn't have to." She set the dish on the hallway table, hard enough that the lid rattled. "I told you. The pills you saw were for migraines. My migraines. Not for poisoning you."

"I know. I'm sorry. I'm just..." I stopped. How did I explain paranoia without sounding paranoid?

Eleanor's jaw tightened. "You know what? Fine. Don't eat. Starve if you want. But don't act like I'm the problem when you're the one who can't trust anyone."

She turned and left, boots heavy on the stairs.

I stood there listening to the front door slam, watching through the window as she stormed back to the cottage.

The soup sat on the table, still steaming. I stared at it for ten minutes. Then I took it to the kitchen and poured it down the drain. Felt guilty doing it. Wasteful. Stupid.

But I needed to know.

---

**That evening**

I stayed alert all afternoon. Worked in the library for six straight hours. Read files, cross-referenced timelines, made notes. No drowsiness. No sudden exhaustion. No lost time.

My head stayed clear. My thoughts stayed sharp.

It proved nothing. One afternoon without her food didn't mean anything. Could be coincidence. Could be that I'd happened to crash on the nights I ate her cooking because I was exhausted anyway.

But I documented it in my journal:

*Dec 10 - Refused Eleanor's lunch. Stayed alert all afternoon. No drowsiness. No gaps. Correlation or causation? Can't tell. Need more data.*

*She was angry. Knew I was testing her. Either she's innocent and insulted, or she's guilty and knows I'm onto her.*

*Which one scares me more?*

I closed the journal and looked out at the cottage. Lights off except for one dim glow in the loft. Eleanor was up there in her locked room, doing whatever she did in that space she wouldn't let me see.

And I was here, alone, still hungry, wondering if I'd insulted an innocent woman or narrowly avoided being drugged again.

Either way, I wasn't sure I'd sleep tonight.

---

A floorboard creaks in the hall.

My eyes snap open. I hold my breath, listening. Nothing. Just the house settling. I'm being paranoid.

Then I hear it again. Closer this time. The softest, most deliberate sound of a footstep just outside my door.

My heart hammers against my ribs. I stay perfectly still, forcing my breathing to remain slow and even, faking a sleep I am nowhere near.

The doorknob turns with an agonizing slowness. A sliver of dark hallway appears as the door opens, and a figure slips inside.

Eleanor.

She closes the door without a sound. I watch her through my eyelashes, my body rigid with a fear so cold it feels like ice water in my veins.

She moves to the side of my bed. The moonlight from the window is weak, painting her as a silhouette, all shadows and indistinct edges. She stands there, watching me. I focus on my breathing, in and out, the slow rhythm of a man deep in slumber. Don't move. Don't let her know you're awake.

Then I see it. She's holding something. A dark shape in her right hand, held down at her side. The form is maddeningly ambiguous in the gloom. It could be a book. It could be a phone. It could be the heavy, volcanic rock from the fireplace hearth. It could be a knife.

My mind races. What is she doing? Is she checking on me, as she said she would? Or is she here for another reason?

She takes a half-step closer. I can almost feel the warmth radiating from her body. She lifts the object slightly, and my muscles tense, ready to spring. But she only shifts her grip, and I see the object's profile for a fraction of a second. Long and thin. A metal glint.

A syringe?

No. It couldn't be. My mind is playing tricks on me. It's a pen. A thermometer. Something innocent.

She stands over me for what feels like an eternity. Just watching me breathe. Is she waiting for me to fall deeper into sleep? Or is she admiring her work?

Finally, with the same spectral silence, she turns and glides out of the room, closing the door behind her.

I lie there, frozen, for a full ten minutes, listening to the frantic drumming of my own heart. Was I dreaming? Was it a sleep-induced hallucination, a product of my exhaustion and paranoia?

Or was it real?

The fear is a living thing inside me. I throw off the covers, stumble to the door, and lock it. I drag a heavy armchair in front of it, my hands shaking. It's an irrational, useless gesture, but it's all I can think to do.

Sleep is impossible now. But my body, pushed beyond its limits, finally betrays me. I collapse back into bed, the image of her shadowy form burned into my mind, and fall into a fitful, nightmare-ridden sleep, not from peace, but from pure, debilitating exhaustion.

---

I wake to the sound of a truck engine. Disoriented, I check my phone—2:47 PM. I've slept ten hours, deeper than I have in weeks. No dreams I remember.

Through the window, I see a pickup truck parked near Eleanor's cottage. Someone's here.

I pull on clothes and head downstairs. The cold hits me as I step outside—sharp, clean, the kind of cold that clears the head. Eleanor is near the greenhouse with an older man in work clothes, gesturing at the building.

As I approach, a dog trots out from behind the truck. Medium-sized, brown and white, tail wagging. Then it sees us—both Eleanor and me—and stops.

The dog's ears go back. Tail slows. It stands there, maybe ten feet away, watching both of us with fixed attention. Wary.

"Scout?" The older man—weathered face, carpenter's belt—sounds surprised. "What's wrong, girl?"

The dog doesn't come closer to either of us. Just keeps that steady, unsettling stare, looking between Eleanor and me like it can't decide which one is the problem.

"Sorry about that," the man says. "She's getting old, bit anxious sometimes. Doesn't like certain places or people—can't ever tell what sets her off."

"No worries," I say. Eleanor shifts beside me, and I notice Scout's eyes track her movement.

"Roy, this is Josh," Eleanor says smoothly. "He's renting the mansion for a writing retreat. Josh, Roy Hendricks. He maintains the greenhouse."

We shake hands. "Writer, huh?" Roy says. "What kind of stuff?"

"True crime mostly. Investigative pieces."

"Well, you came to the right place." He grins. "Thornewood's got plenty of history."

The dog—Scout—still hasn't moved. Still watching us both. Not growling, but radiating distrust. Like this whole situation doesn't add up.

"Scout, outside," Roy commands. The dog hesitates, then trots back toward the truck, pausing once to glance back at the two of us with that fixed stare.

Eleanor and Roy talk about the heating system. I watch through the greenhouse glass, see her maintaining the groundskeeper role perfectly. Every gesture, every smile. Scout stays near the truck, occasionally looking toward the greenhouse where we're all standing.

Twenty minutes later, Roy finishes his work and heads out, Scout following at his heels. The dog pauses at the truck, gives one last wary look in our direction before jumping into the bed.

I watch them drive away, gravel crunching under tires.

The silence he leaves is suffocating.

---

Back in the library, I return to my investigation files. The names. The timeline. The pattern I can't quite see.

But today feels different. Clearer. The rest helped.

I spread out my notes again, looking for what I've been missing. The connections between the disappearances. The gaps in the documentation. The reason Thornewood keeps claiming lives.

Eleanor was right—I needed sleep. Needed to step back.

Now I can see with fresh eyes.

And somewhere in these files, in these names and dates and disappeared lives, is the answer.

I have to find it.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 14: VULNERABILITY
## December 12 — Eleanor

---

He's been gone for seven hours.

The headache had been building all day. I dry-swallowed two aspirin and kept working. No time to stop. Not with everything still unfinished.

I watch the empty driveway from my cottage window. Six in the morning, he drove away. Now it's past one, and I don't know where he went. Don't know what he's been doing. Don't know what he's capable of when I'm not watching.

At 13:15, his truck finally returns. I watch him get out. His movements are stiff. There's a new scratch on his knuckles, and his jacket is soiled. The confusion on his face as he checks his phone—he doesn't know where he's been either.

Seven hours. Gone. Just like that.

He looks lost. Frightened. Like a man waking up from a nightmare he can't remember.

I give him twenty minutes to try to pull himself together. Then I walk to the mansion.

He's at the kitchen table, staring at his phone, his expression lost.

"Hey," I say softly.

He jumps. Hadn't heard me come in. "Eleanor. Yeah. I'm... yeah."

"Where'd you go? You were gone all morning."

"I—" He stops. "Town. I went to town for supplies."

"What'd you get?"

The panic in his eyes is instant. "What?"

"What supplies did you get? You've been gone seven hours."

He can't answer. Can't account for the time. I watch him scramble for an explanation, and something tightens in my chest. This is what I needed to see. Him breaking. Vulnerable.

I cross to him. Put my hand on his shoulder. Feel him trembling.

"Josh. Talk to me. What's going on?"

"I don't know." His voice breaks. "Eleanor, I don't know where I was. I don't remember."

Seven hours. Gone. And he has no idea what happened.

"Come with me," I say.

I lead him to the bedroom. He follows without question. Trusts me completely.

He sits on the edge of the bed, looking up at me with those haunted eyes. Waiting for me to tell him everything's okay.

But I can't. Because it's not.

"What happened out there?" I ask. "You look like hell."

He admits it has. Blames stress. Insomnia. Trauma responses from childhood. He's building a wall of explanations, trying to make sense of something that doesn't make sense.

"I feel like I'm losing my grip," he whispers. "Like whole chunks of time just... disappear. And I can't figure out if it's the stress or if someone's doing this to me."

The fear in his voice is real. He's spiraling, looking for someone to anchor him.

And I want to be that person. God, I want to wrap my arms around him and tell him everything will be fine.

But comfort is a luxury neither of us can afford. Not when I'm this close to finishing.

Instead, I kiss him. Because it's easier than answering. Because I don't know what else to do.

He responds with desperate gratitude. We fall back onto the bed, and I try to focus on his body instead of his questions. The fresh scratch on his hand. The new bruise on his ribs. Signs of the missing hours he can't explain.

When we come together, I tell myself I'm helping him. Giving him comfort. But the truth is, I need this too. Need to feel something besides the cold dread that's been growing in my chest.

Afterward, he holds me like I'm the only real thing in his world.

"I need to know what's happening to me," he says. "I need to document it. Cameras. I need to set up cameras."

"Do it," I say. "Find out the truth. And I'll help you figure out what to do." *Let him watch himself. Let him see what I've been seeing. It'll make the end easier.*

"Will you?" His voice is small. Hopeful. "Even if the truth is bad?"

"Yes," I whisper. The lie tastes like ash. I already know exactly what we'll find.

He falls asleep in my arms. I lie there in the darkness, listening to him breathe, wondering what happens next.

**December 12.**

*He lost seven hours today. Came back with scratches and bruises he doesn't remember getting. He's scared. I'm scared too.*

*I told him I'd help him find the truth. I meant it. He's coming undone. The more confused he gets, the easier this becomes.*

*He's almost broken. Soon he'll have nowhere left to run.*

*And then this ends.*

---

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 15: SURVEILLANCE
## December 14-19 — Josh

---

Five days pass in careful distance.

I avoid Eleanor for two full days after the episode in the bedroom—Dec 13 and 14, holed up in the library, nursing my confusion and shame. By the 15th, I'm ready to start looking for answers. By the 17th, I've found the keystroke logger, and everything shifts into high gear.
