"About what you're really doing here."

I had been up in the loft when he knocked. A careless mistake, leaving that notebook in the library. I had to get control back.

The storm had kept us trapped together. Now that closeness was becoming a problem.

He was waiting in the great room, my notebook on the table. Evidence of everything I'd been hiding.

"You left this in the library," he said. His voice was neutral, but the undertone was sharp. "It fell open and I saw... things."

"What things?" I asked, feigning ignorance.

"Notes about me. My routines. You've been tracking my movements, Eleanor. Like I'm a subject."

He was starting to see. Good. Let him wonder what else I knew.

"Yes," I admitted. A direct confirmation might disarm him more than a lie.

"Why?"

This was the moment that mattered. "Because I'm scared," I said. "After what happened here in 2020, I wanted to know who was on the property. If they were... safe." I watched his face. The suspicion warred with his desire to see me as a victim, someone in need of his protection.

"The murder?" Josh's eyes narrowed. "Did you know them? The victim?"

The word landed between us. *Victim.* He was digging closer. I needed to redirect him before he found something I couldn't explain. I kept my face neutral.

"No," I lied smoothly. "But it happened on my property. That makes you my responsibility."

He didn't believe me. "Who was the victim to you, Eleanor?"

"Someone who threatened everything I built here," I whispered. The line was true enough to be convincing, vague enough to be safe. It landed perfectly. His expression softened. He was recalibrating.

"You've been tracking me like I'm dangerous," he said, the accusation now a plea for understanding.

"Haven't you been investigating me?" I returned, turning the focus back on him. The flicker of guilt on his face confirmed my counter-surveillance.

He tried to justify his actions, speaking of his own fears, his own potential for danger. He was spiraling, turning his paranoia inward. Exactly where I needed him. The doubt was doing its work.

"I think you might be right about something being wrong," I said, my voice gentle. "And I think you came here because some part of you knows."

His face went pale. "Knows what?"

"That something happened here."

I wanted to push him further. Make him squirm. "Come with me," I said.

"Where?"

"The tower room."

The highest, most exposed point in the mansion. Nowhere to hide up there.

He followed. In the tower room, he felt something—it showed in his face. Unease he couldn't explain. Something dark stirring beneath the surface.

Then he saw the carving. "M+L 2020. Who's M? And who's L?"

He had no memory of the carving. Didn't remember the tower. That kind of gap was useful to me.

"You think I'm connected to what happened here," he stated.

"I think," I said slowly, "that you need to decide what you believe." I was giving the responsibility back to him. Let his own guilt consume him.

He broke. "I believe I'm dangerous. I believe you should call the police."

He was surrendering to me. I held him, whispered promises. He was mine now. Completely. He promised not to run. He promised to trust me.

At the door to the cottage, he kissed me. Tenderly. He believed I was his savior.

I went to the loft. Sat in the dark and made a decision. The video diary entries where I'd let myself feel things—those were dangerous. A weakness. I deleted them. All of them. I couldn't afford to waver now.

Then I reviewed the footage from the tower room. Watched his face when he saw the carving. The confusion. The fear. Everything I needed.

He was almost broken. But he was also coming apart faster than I'd expected. I couldn't wait much longer. Winter wouldn't last forever. The roads would reopen. The window was closing.

Seven days. I would give it seven days. Then, I would initiate the end game. One way or another.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 17: THE WRITING
## December 21 — Josh

---

I wrote the murder scene at three in the morning on the shortest day of the year.

Couldn't sleep. Eleanor had gone back to the cottage hours ago, citing an early morning errand into town before the Christmas Eve storm. She'd looked exhausted—more than usual. I'd noticed her hands shaking when she put on her coat, noticed she'd winced when I'd hugged her goodbye. Like her head hurt. I'd kissed her goodnight at the door, watched her silhouette disappear across the moonlit grounds, and tried not to think about how normal that was. How domestic. How wrong.

Back in the library, laptop open, I'd intended to work on the podcast. Six episodes behind schedule. Derek had emailed twice this week—increasingly concerned, increasingly direct. *Need something, Josh. Anything. Are you even alive up there?*

Instead, I found myself opening a blank document. No notes. No outline. Just the cursor blinking in the darkness.

And I started writing.

Not about Eleanor. Not about the victims. About a killer. About what it might feel like to take a life. To cross that final line from human to monster.

The words came easier than expected. Years of research paying off, I told myself. All those interviews with convicted murderers finally finding an outlet.

---

By dawn, I had four thousand words. The scene was complete—visceral, disturbing. A killer in an isolated location. The psychology of violence.

I'd researched criminal psychology obsessively over the years—for my podcast, for the books I'd written. Interviews with convicted murderers, psychiatric evaluations, behavioral analysis reports. Every true crime writer accumulated this knowledge. It was part of the job.

The prose flowed more easily than anything I'd written in years. Maybe it was the isolation, stripping away distractions. Maybe it was the setting—gothic mansion in winter, the perfect backdrop for dark fiction.

My mother's murder. The loss that had shaped everything. That was where this intensity was coming from—years of immersion in violence, finally distilled into fiction.

The prose was good—better than anything I'd written in years. Clean, precise, unflinching. It read like reportage.

Too precise? Or just finally using my research properly?

It was research—it had to be. Years of studying crime scenes, reading autopsy reports, interviewing killers. The knowledge had accumulated, synthesized, emerged as fiction. This is what writers did. They inhabited terrible spaces so readers didn't have to.

But my hands were shaking as I typed the email to Derek.

---

**TO:** DerekHayes@TrueCrimeWeekly.com
**FROM:** JoshCaine@ProtonMail.com
**SUBJECT:** Pages (finally)
**DATE:** December 21, 5:47 AM

Derek—

Alive. Barely. The isolation up here is... intense. Good for writing. Bad for sanity.

Attached: opening chapter of the book. Psychological thriller. Man investigating murders, starts to question his own reality. All the darkness.

Let me know what you think. Need objective eyes on this.

—J

**[ATTACHMENT: TheThornewoodProject_Ch1.docx]**

---

I hit send before I could reconsider. Closed the laptop. Went upstairs and showered under scalding water, trying to wash away the sense that I'd just exposed a truth I should have kept hidden.

The library scene. Why a library? Why winter? Why candlelight and perfume and those specific details?

Maybe because I was IN a library. In winter. With candles and Eleanor's perfume lingering. Maybe my unconscious mind was pulling from my current environment, weaving my present circumstances into fiction. That's what creative minds did—they blended observation with imagination.

Or maybe it was older, deeper. Childhood trauma manifesting in specific, recurring imagery.

I didn't know.

But the details were right. Were true.

They came from somewhere real, even if I couldn't name the source.

---

Eleanor returned mid-morning with groceries and a small spruce tree tied to the roof of her car. Christmas. Right. The calendar said December 21. Three days until Christmas Eve.

"Thought we could decorate tonight," she called from the kitchen, unloading bags. "Make it festive. Unless you're Scrooge."

I should have been charming. Should have played along. Instead I stood in the kitchen doorway, watching her move through the space with easy familiarity, and asked: "The woman who was killed here. The 2020 victim. How did she die?"

Eleanor's hands stilled on a bag of coffee beans. Didn't turn around. "Why are you asking?"

"Professional curiosity. You said she was murdered here. I'm a crime writer. It's what I think about."

"It's Christmas week, Josh."

"I know. But I can't stop thinking about it. About her. About what happened in this house." I paused. "My mother died when I was three. I don't remember her, but it shaped everything. It's why I write about this stuff—trying to understand loss I was too young to comprehend."

She turned slowly. Her expression was careful. Guarded. "Strangulation. Manual. No weapon. Just... hands."

My mouth went dry. "Where?"

"Why does it matter?"

"Context. Setting. I think about these things."

"You think about murders at Christmas."

"I think about murders all the time. You know that." I crossed to the table, sat, tried to appear casual. "Where was her body found?"

Eleanor studied me for a long moment. "In the woods. Past the grounds, off the old logging trail. Partially buried. Animals had..." She stopped. "Does that satisfy your professional curiosity?"

In the woods. Partially buried.

The details were grim. Real. Not the sanitized version you got in news articles.

I thought about the fiction I'd been writing—dark psychological thriller, a killer's mindset. Nothing like this specific case. Different victim, different circumstances. But still, hearing about an actual murder at a place where I was writing about fictional ones was strange. Like the setting was bleeding into my work, or vice versa.

I shook off the unease. Occupational hazard of being a true crime writer.

"Josh? You look pale."

"I'm fine." But I wasn't. "What time of day? When she died?"

Eleanor paused—just a beat too long, eyes dropping to the coffee mug in her hands. "Evening. Sometime between nine and midnight. Coroner couldn't be more specific." Her voice sharpened. "Why?"

"Just establishing timeline." The lie came automatically. "Did she know her killer?"

"They think so. No signs of forced entry. She let them in. Probably knew them." Eleanor moved closer. "Are you writing about her? About the victim? Is that what this is?"

"No. I just—I need to understand what happened here. If I'm living in the house where someone was murdered—"

"You said you weren't afraid."

"I'm not afraid. I'm curious. There's a difference."

She didn't look convinced. But she let it drop, returning to the groceries. "Help me with the tree?"

We spent the afternoon decorating. She'd bought lights, ornaments, tinsel. We strung them on the small spruce in the great room while Christmas music played from her phone. It should have been cozy. Romantic. Instead it was performance. Like we were both pretending at normalcy while circling the truth neither of us wanted to name.

She laughed at my terrible wrapping skills—I was attempting to wrap her gift, a vintage edition of *Rebecca* I'd found in the library. She told stories about Christmases past, carefully vague about family. I told her about foster care Christmases—some kind, some lonely, all temporary.

And beneath it all, I kept thinking about the woman who'd died here. About Eleanor, who'd found out about the murder and chosen to stay anyway. About what kind of person did that—worked at a place where someone had been killed.

What kind of person was I, for finding that intriguing rather than horrifying?

---

Derek's response arrived at eleven PM, just as Eleanor and I were finishing dinner.

My laptop sat on the library desk. I'd been avoiding it all day. But when I heard the notification chime, I excused myself—"Just need to check email, work thing"—and opened the message.

---

**TO:** JoshCaine@ProtonMail.com
**FROM:** DerekHayes@TrueCrimeWeekly.com
**SUBJECT:** RE: Pages (finally)
**DATE:** December 22, 11:03 PM

Josh—

Read your chapter. Twice.

This is really strong work. The sensory details are vivid—almost uncomfortably so. You've always had a knack for immersive writing, but this feels particularly intense. The violence feels visceral in a way that made me need to put it down and come back to it.

Reading it made me uncomfortable, which I think is the point. You've captured something dark here.

But I have to ask—are you okay up there? The isolation, the subject matter you're working with... it's heavy, even for you. Just want to make sure you're taking care of yourself while you dig into this material.

Maybe take a break? Get outside, talk to someone? I know you like to immerse yourself in cases, but this is grim stuff to be living with alone.

Strong chapter though. Really strong. Just... be careful with how deep you go.

—Derek

---

I read the email three times. Each time, my hands shook harder.

Derek was right. The chapter was intense. Too visceral. The violence had poured out of me in a way that was... different. Not like crafting a scene, but like remembering one.

I hadn't interviewed anyone about the mechanics of violence. Hadn't reviewed case files for this particular scene. I'd written it. Let it flow from somewhere deep.

But how? When? I'd never killed anyone. Never witnessed a murder—my mother was killed when I was three, and I had no memory of it at all. Just a blank where that day should be.

So where had these details come from? The precise timing. The physical progression. The way consciousness fades. The precision felt too effortless for pure imagination.

Maybe I'd absorbed it from all the research. Years of reading case files, interviewing survivors, studying forensic reports. Maybe my subconscious had assembled a composite from hundreds of sources.

That explanation made sense. That explanation was safe.

But it still seemed wrong. Like I was rationalizing instead of reasoning. Like I was refusing to look at the truth.

Or.

Or I'd been there. Done it. Remembered it.

The thought was absurd. Impossible. I was investigating Eleanor. That's why I was here. That's the only reason.

My phone buzzed. Text from Eleanor: *Coming back to mansion. Left sweater. Saw your light. You okay?*

I texted back: *Fine. Working. See you tomorrow.*

Closed the laptop before she could arrive and ask questions.

But the document remained. And Derek's concern. And the unsettling question: where had those details come from?

---

Christmas Eve arrived with snow. Heavy, silent, isolating. The roads would be impassable by evening. Eleanor and I were truly alone now—no possibility of visitors, no escape if things went wrong.

We spent the day in careful domesticity. Made breakfast together. Watched the snow fall. Avoided talking about anything real.

But the document haunted me. And Derek's email. And the terrible, growing certainty that I knew things I shouldn't know.

Finally, over dinner—roast chicken Eleanor had prepared, wine from the cellar—I couldn't hold it anymore.

"I need to tell you the truth."

She looked up from her plate. "Okay."

"I wrote a scene. A murder scene. For the book I'm supposedly working on."

"Okay," she said again, carefully.

"It was detailed. Too detailed. My editor noticed. Asked me how I knew the physical reality of it so well."

Eleanor set down her fork. "What did you tell him?"

"That I researched it. But Eleanor, I didn't. I wrote it. Like I was remembering." I met her eyes. "I grew up around violence. Witnessed things as a kid that... those memories don't go away. They surface when I write. That's all Derek is seeing—trauma memory, not experience."

"You lost your mother young. You told me that. Childhood trauma—"

"I know. That's what I told myself. But then you described the victim today. And I'd written scenes like it. Bodies in woods. Partially buried. Generic crime scene details, but..." I paused. "It seemed like coincidence. Or not."

The silence stretched. Snow ticked against the windows.

"You're a crime writer," Eleanor said finally. Her voice was careful. "You write about this stuff all the time. Bodies get buried in woods. That's not prophecy, it's pattern recognition."

"Maybe. Or maybe you've been feeding me details. During our conversations. When I've been drinking. You describe things, and I absorb them without realizing, and then they surface when I write."

Eleanor stood abruptly. Crossed to the window, back to me. When she spoke, her voice was strange. "That's not what's happening here."

"Isn't it? You just admitted you're 'interested in true crime.' That you read the autopsy report. You know all these details. And you've had weeks to slip them into conversations with me."

"You're deflecting—"

"I'm thinking clearly for the first time since I got here. The episodes, the stress—Eleanor, I told you about those in confidence. And ever since, you've been planting suggestions. Making me doubt myself. Classic gaslighting."

"Josh, listen to yourself—"

"No, you listen. I came here to investigate a pattern of disappearances. And the groundskeeper who conveniently shows up after each one? She has detailed knowledge of every crime. She has access to the victims' last locations. She's been watching me, manipulating me—"

Eleanor crossed back to the table. Sat. Looked at me with an expression I couldn't read—grief, fear, anger, resignation. "Show me the document. The one you wrote."

"Why?"

"Because I need to see what you think you wrote."

I retrieved my laptop. Opened the file. Turned the screen toward her.

She read in silence. Two minutes. Three. Her face didn't change, but her hands gripped the table edge, knuckles white.

"This is disturbing," she said finally. Her voice had gone strange, hollow.

"It's fiction. For the book."

"It's a strangulation murder. In winter. In an isolated house." She looked up at me. "Like what happened here."

My blood turned to ice. "Generic crime scene details. Thousands of murders happen that way. It's not—I'm a crime writer. I write about this stuff."

"I've read about the case too. I was interested in true crime, in what happened here. And Josh, what you wrote..." She hesitated. "Why this scenario? Why winter, why strangulation, why an isolated estate? That's a lot of coincidences."

"Because you told me. Maybe not directly. But you've been describing the case, asking leading questions, watching how I respond. You even admitted you've been watching me on surveillance cameras. How many conversations did I forget? How many times did you talk about these details while I was half-asleep or drinking?"

"That's not—" Her voice hardened. "You know because you were there. Because you never stopped circling that night. And now, four years later, you came back. To what—relive it? Cover your tracks?"

"No. I came here to investigate you."

"Fine. Then investigate this: You wrote about her death. Strangulation. Winter. Isolated location. That's the same basic scenario as what happened here."

I stood, chair scraping. "Because I'm living in the house where it happened. I'm a crime writer working on a book about murders. Of course I'd write a scene that parallels the case. That's not prophecy, that's pattern recognition."

"You're exhausted. Disoriented. You admitted you don't always know where the time goes. How can you be sure of anything?"

"I'm sure about you. You know what does make someone suspicious? Having detailed knowledge of every disappearance on this property. Being the common denominator across years of vanishings. Having people's movements tracked, their habits memorized—"

"I researched the case!"

"Or you were there." I met her eyes. "You're the one who's read the autopsy reports. You're the one who knows all the details. Not me. I wrote a fictional scene, and you're the one getting upset about it. Why? What do you know that I don't?"

"Are you serious right now?" Eleanor stood too. We faced each other across the table, the laptop screen glowing between us. "You wrote something incredibly detailed and disturbing and you're trying to frame me?"

"I'm not trying to frame anyone. I'm following the evidence. And the evidence all points to you."

She stared at me. Something flashed across her face—fury, or maybe fear. When she spoke, her voice was deadly calm. "You really believe that."

"I believe what the facts tell me. You have means, opportunity, and detailed knowledge. You've been manipulating me since I arrived. And now you're trying to make me think I'm the one responsible? That's textbook DARVO—Deny, Attack, Reverse Victim and Offender."

"You have no idea what you're talking about."

"Don't I? Let me guess—next you'll tell me you have evidence against me. Documents. Footage. Some carefully constructed narrative that makes me look guilty."

Her expression didn't change. But the silence told me everything.

"I need to leave." The words came out strangled. "I need to go. Get away from here before—"

"Before what? Before I expose you?" Eleanor's laugh was bitter. "The roads are snowed in, Josh. We're trapped here together."

"Stop trying to flip this around. You're the one with all the answers. You're the one who's been surveilling me, collecting 'evidence,' building a case. And I found your trunk—the gun, the rope, the duct tape. A kill kit, Eleanor. You're the one who came prepared for violence, not me. That's what you do, isn't it? You create suspects. Plant evidence. Make people doubt themselves."

"I plant evidence? You're the one who showed up here with a forged book, planted newspaper clippings, staging your entire investigation—"

"Because I was investigating you!"

"Then explain the document." Her voice went cold. "Explain how you write scenes that parallel what happened here and expect me to believe it's coincidence."

"You claim it's accurate. But I only have your word for that. You could be lying. You've been lying about everything else."

We stood in silence, the snow falling outside, the Christmas tree lights blinking in the next room. The careful domesticity we'd built shattered completely. Pretense over. Cards on the table.

Eleanor spoke first. "I've been watching you. Tracking your every move. The surveillance footage—I've seen all of it. And Josh, you have episodes. Times when you're not quite yourself. When you move differently, look different."

"Watching me? Surveillance?" My mind spun. "The cameras. You've been using them to—"

"To catch you in the act. Yes. I installed cameras everywhere. And I've recorded your nighttime walks. Your library visits at three AM. The way you sometimes seem confused about where you are."

"You've been spying on me this whole time. Building a case. That's what this is—you've been setting me up. Making me look unstable, dangerous. Getting footage of me looking disoriented so you can claim I'm having 'episodes.'"

"I've been watching you." Her eyes flashed. "Just like you've been watching me. We're both liars, Josh. Both pretending. Both circling the same truth."

"What truth?"

"That I know exactly what you are. And what you deserve."

The words should have landed like physical blows. But instead, they clarified everything.

"You're right," I said. "One of us is a killer. And I know which one."

"Josh—"

"Get out." My voice was steady now. "Go back to the cottage. I'm done playing your games."

"You think I'm leaving you alone in this house so you can destroy evidence? Run? I don't think so."

"I'm not destroying anything. I'm calling the police. First thing tomorrow when the storm clears, I'm driving to town and telling them everything. About you. About the surveillance. About how you've been gaslighting me for weeks."

She stared at me. Something flickered across her face—calculation. "I have everything. Your fingerprints in places you don't remember going. Footage of things you don't remember doing. Go to the police. See whose side they take."

"Then show it to them. Let's see how your carefully constructed frame job holds up under real scrutiny."

For a long moment, we faced each other across the kitchen table. Then Eleanor gathered her coat, her boots.

"Keep the car keys," I said. "Keep the gun if it makes you feel safer. I'm not going anywhere tonight. But tomorrow, this ends."

She paused at the door. "You're making a mistake."

"The only mistake I made was trusting you."

Then she was gone. The door closing. Her footsteps crunching through snow toward the cottage. Lights appearing in her windows moments later.

I stood alone in the silent mansion with my laptop and the document I'd written. My hands were shaking—not from fear, but from anger.

She'd almost had me. Almost made me doubt myself, question my own memory, accept her gaslighting as truth.

But Derek's concern echoed back with new meaning. The intensity of what I'd written. The visceral details.

The answer was obvious: Eleanor had fed me those details. Weeks of careful conversation, strategic questions, planting information. And now she was trying to use my own fiction as evidence against me.

It was brilliant. Sociopathic, but brilliant.

Tomorrow, I'd prove it.

---

I didn't sleep Christmas Eve.

Instead, I sat in the library—the same library where my fictional killer had strangled his victim—and read the document again. And again. Looking for any detail that could have come from research, from imagination, from anything except experience.

But Eleanor was right. It matched. The small details no outsider would know. Lavender perfume. Bitten tongue. The specific location. The timing.

And the psychology. The internal monologue I'd given the killer:

*He felt nothing. That was the surprise. He'd expected guilt, horror, humanity. Instead: calm. Completion. Like finishing a task that needed finishing. Her death wasn't personal. Just necessary. Just what happened when a man stopped caring about consequences.*

That phrase. The strange detachment, the sense of something mechanical about it. I'd written it before Eleanor started planting suggestions. Before Derek questioned me.

Because she'd made me know. She'd planted the word in my subconscious, fed me the details through drugged conversations I barely remembered. This was what gaslighting looked like—making the victim write their own confession script.

At dawn on Christmas, I opened a new email to Derek. Typed and deleted a dozen times. Finally settled on:

---

**TO:** DerekHayes@TrueCrimeWeekly.com
**FROM:** JoshCaine@ProtonMail.com
**SUBJECT:** The document
**DATE:** December 25, 6:23 AM

Derek—

You asked how I knew the details. I don't have an answer that makes sense.

But I think I know what's happening to me. The exhaustion. The strange moments. The confusion. Eleanor is drugging me.

I know how that sounds. Paranoid. Conspiracy thinking. But it fits everything. The coffee she brings every morning. The dinners we share. The wine we drink together. The gaps in my day always happen after being with her.

She's been making me think I've been here before. Making me write things that sound like confession. Planting false memories, false evidence. Muddy boots in my closet I never bought. Journal entries that look like my handwriting but can't be. Torn pages from a guest book that I suspect once held my name—pages conveniently destroyed before I could verify anything.

She's trying to make me think I'm dangerous. That I've done terrible things. She's gaslighting me, and I'm losing track of what's real.

I'm at Thornewood Estate in Oregon. Coordinates: [attached]. If you don't hear from me again, if something happens, tell the police to look here. Tell them to ask about the disappearances. Tell them Eleanor Moore has been drugging the person investigating her.

Tell them I think she's going to kill me and make it look like suicide. Or make it look like I'm the killer.

—Josh

---

I hit send. Then immediately regretted it. But it was done. Sent into the digital void. Accusation or truth—I didn't know which anymore.

Outside, fresh snow covered everything. Beautiful. Pristine. Deadly.

The cottage lights were still on. Eleanor awake too, probably. Both of us unable to sleep, both processing the same danger from different sides.

I'd come here to investigate her. To expose a killer. To be the hero in my own story.

Instead, she'd turned the tables. Made me the suspect. Made me doubt my own mind.

The Christmas tree lights blinked in the next room, cheerful and oblivious. I sat in the dawn light and wondered:

How far would she go to protect her secrets?

What else had she made me believe that wasn't real?

How many false memories had she planted?

And most terrifying: what happens when you can't trust your own mind? When the investigator discovers he's being investigated—and framed?

Eleanor knew I was onto her. She'd been watching, recording, building her frame-up against ME. Waiting for me to confront her. Or waiting for the perfect moment to finish this.

We were both hunting. Both liars. Both circling the same terrible truth from different sides.

And Christmas morning at Thornewood Estate, isolated by snow and darkness and the weight of her manipulation, one truth was finally clear:

I didn't come here to catch a killer.

I came here because someone had to expose the truth. Someone had to stop whatever Eleanor was planning.

---

# CHAPTER 18: DESPERATION
## December 26 — Eleanor

---

I dropped the coffee mug.

Nerves. My hands were shaking and the handle slipped. The mug hit the floor, shattered. Coffee spread across the tile in a dark pool.

Josh wasn't here yet. Good. I had maybe twenty minutes before he'd arrive for this confrontation I knew was coming.

I bent to pick up the pieces, focusing on making my hand work. Fingers. Close. Grip. Lift.

The weakness passed. Thirty seconds, maybe less. Stress. Exhaustion. Not enough sleep, too much coffee, too many nights in the loft.

I cleaned up the mess, hands still shaking from the adrenaline. Threw the broken pieces in the trash. Made more coffee carefully, fingers still tingling from the surge of stress.

I splashed water on my face, gripped the counter until my hands stopped shaking. Needed to stay sharp. Needed my hands steady for what was coming. Josh had found the cameras. Found everything. This was the moment I'd been preparing for.

Checked my watch: 11:47 AM. The pressure was building. I couldn't keep this performance going much longer.

I had to end this. Now. Before Josh figured out what I was really doing here.

Before I couldn't finish what I started.

---

He found all of them.

Deep winter already, and the isolation was showing cracks. All three cameras. Every single one I'd placed. He'd found everything I'd been using to watch him. To see what he'd do when he thought no one was looking. Laid out on the library table like an accusation. Numbered. Catalogued. Organized in his careful, meticulous way. It was terrifying to see it all laid out like that.

I stood in the doorway at noon on the day after Christmas, staring at the display. He'd been methodical—mapped each camera's location on a hand-drawn floor plan, documented their angles and coverage, traced their wireless signals back to my laptop. The totality of my surveillance. Proof of my deception.

He sat behind the table, backlit by gray winter light. His expression was unreadable.

"You've been watching everything," he said. Not a question. Statement of fact.

"Yes."

"For how long?"

"Since you arrived in October."

"Earlier." He tapped one of the cameras—the library unit I'd installed in April, months before he came. "Battery dates confirm installation last spring. You were watching the house before I got here."

"Yes."

"Waiting for me specifically."

I didn't answer. Didn't need to. He'd already figured it out.

Josh stood slowly. Walked around the table toward me. I held my ground, hand in my coat pocket where the taser was. Fully charged. Ready.

He stopped five feet away. Close enough to read the rage in his eyes. The betrayal. The terrible hurt underneath.

"Who are you?" His voice was quiet. Controlled. More dangerous than shouting.

"You know who I am."

"No. I know who you pretended to be. Groundskeeper. Lover. Ally. Someone helping me figure out my memory problems, the missing hours, my potential for violence." He laughed, bitter. "But that was all performance, wasn't it? You've been watching me. Surveilling me."

I didn't answer. Couldn't.

"Why?" he demanded. "Why install cameras before I even arrived? Why the performance? What the hell are you hiding?"

This was it. The moment I'd been working toward. The confrontation I'd rehearsed a thousand times in my head. But I couldn't tell him. Not yet. Not until he was ready to hear it.

"You don't get to interrogate me," I said instead. "Not when you've been lying from the beginning. You didn't come here to write a book. You came here to investigate me for murders I didn't commit."

He stared at me. "I did my research—"

"You stalked me," I interrupted. "Background checks. Surveillance equipment. Building a case. You came here convinced I was dangerous."

"You are dangerous."

# CHAPTER 19: THE LOFT

Medical articles about rohypnol, scopolamine, benzodiazepines. Detailed notes on dosing, onset times, duration of effects. How long substances remained detectable in blood, urine, hair samples.

She'd been studying how to drug someone without detection.

Further down: psychology articles about trauma responses, memory formation, stress disorders. Highlighted sections about how exhaustion could create disorientation and confusion. How paranoia could be induced through gaslighting.

Was she drugging me? Or making me think I was being drugged?

Both possibilities were terrifying.

I found another folder: "February 2020." Clicked it open.

Photos. The 2020 victim. Young, blonde, bright smile. Dozens of photos I'd seen before in my research.

But these were different. Personal photos. Not from news articles or police reports. Photos taken here, at Thornewood. The victim standing by the lake. Sitting in the library. Walking the grounds.

Eleanor's photos. She'd been here in 2020. Had known the victim. Despite claiming she'd only started working at Thornewood six months ago.

She'd lied. About everything.

My heart was hammering. I kept clicking through files.

A document titled "Timeline—Joshua Caine." Opened it.

She'd been stalking me. Every movement, every conversation, every vulnerable moment captured with chilling precision. Dates, times, notes. A hunter's log that went back months.

*October 22: He's here. Joshua Caine. Claims he's writing a true crime book. Playing the friendly writer perfectly. They always do, at first.*

*November 3: He's been asking questions. Fixated on what happened in 2020. More than curiosity—he's digging. Let him dig. He won't find what he's looking for.*

*November 15: He's watching me now. Found evidence he's been checking my background. Thinks he's clever. Doesn't matter. Everything is going according to plan.*

*December 7: He's falling apart. Exhausted. Paranoid. Jumping at shadows. Won't be long now.*

She'd been watching me like prey. Documenting my every weakness. Waiting for the right moment to strike.

And she'd been here in 2020. Had known the victim. Had lied about her entire employment timeline.

What else had she lied about?

I copied everything to an encrypted drive. Backed it up twice. This was evidence—evidence of Eleanor's deception, her surveillance, her connection to the 2020 murder.

The mansion creaked around me. 4:47 AM. Almost dawn. Eleanor would be awake soon.

I needed to get out. Now. Before she realized I'd accessed her files. Before she escalated whatever plan she'd been executing.

But the roads were snowed in. Had been for three days. No way down the mountain until the plows came through.

Trapped. Isolated. With a woman who'd been systematically lying to me for months. Who'd known the 2020 victim. Who'd been documenting my every move like I was some kind of experiment.

I closed the laptop.

I had maybe twelve hours until nightfall. Twelve hours to gather enough evidence to take to the police when the roads cleared.

Twelve hours to figure out what Eleanor Moore was really hiding in that locked room.

Twelve hours to survive.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 20: PREPARATION
## December 27, 3:47 AM — Eleanor

---

I couldn't sleep.

3:47 AM. I'd been lying in bed for two hours, staring at the cottage ceiling, listening to the grandfather clock downstairs mark every passing minute. Counting down. Always counting down.

I couldn't stop thinking. Too many sleepless nights. Too much time alone with my thoughts.

I didn't know how much longer I could keep this up. The waiting. The watching. The pretending to be someone I wasn't.

And I still hadn't finished what I came here to do.

I gave up on sleep and climbed the ladder to the loft. I stepped onto the loft floor and crossed to the locked door, the one that led to my evidence room. I pulled the key from the chain around my neck. The lock turned with a familiar click.

I flipped the light switch. Fluorescent bulbs flickered to life, washing everything in cold white light.

My room. The one I kept locked even though I lived alone.

Everything from my old life was here. The things I'd brought with me. The memories I couldn't let go of. Six months of living in this loft room, trying to make sense of things.

I stood in the center and looked at what I'd become. What I'd sacrificed to get here. My real life on pause. My friendships abandoned. Everything I was narrowed down to obsession and questions I couldn't answer.

Today might be the day I finally told Josh the truth. Or tomorrow. Soon.

I'd been trying to figure out how to do it. How to finish what I'd started. Because despite everything—despite my reasons for coming here, despite what I'd done to get this far—part of me wanted to stop. To leave him intact. To walk away before I destroyed something I couldn't rebuild.

But I hadn't come this far to stop. I'd come too far for that.

I had to do it before I lost my nerve completely.

---

I looked at the photograph pinned to the wall.

The one I'd been staring at for months. The reason I was here. The reason I'd left everything behind to take a groundskeeper job at an isolated estate in the middle of nowhere.

He thinks he's safe here. Thinks no one's watching. He's wrong.

I'd been trying to finish this ever since. Trying to get what I came for. Trying to figure out if it would ever be enough.

And now I was here. Living in the same place where it happened. Walking the same grounds. Sleeping with someone I had no business getting close to—not when I was still hiding so much from him.

"I don't know what I'm doing anymore," I whispered to the empty room. "I don't know if any of this is worth it."

But I was too far in now. Too committed. No turning back.

---

I sat down and opened my notebook.

All the things I'd noticed over the months. The way Josh sometimes seemed confused about time. The gaps in his memory. The way he'd mention not remembering parts of his day. The paranoia about being drugged.

It could mean everything. Or it could mean nothing—stress, insomnia, the isolation getting to him like it was getting to me.

I'd been obsessing over it. Trying to reconcile what I suspected with what I saw. Trying to understand how the gaps in his memory fit into the larger picture I'd been building.

That was the hardest part. His complete, genuine kindness. The way he'd sat across from me last night at dinner, talking about his book, asking about my day, looking at me like I mattered.

Like he had no idea what I was capable of.

Like I wasn't the woman hiding the truth from him every single day.

He seemed harmless. Someone dealing with stress and insomnia and the weight of isolation. Someone whose vulnerabilities made him easy to read.

But I couldn't ignore the gaps. The nights he couldn't account for. The mornings he woke confused, with mud on his boots and no memory of going outside.

What if the person sitting across from me at dinner wasn't the whole truth?

---

I went through what I'd gathered.

Everything I'd collected over the months. All the pieces I'd been trying to fit together. Questions I'd been asking myself over and over.

I'd gone over it so many times. Looking for the weakness. The detail that would crack him open. The one piece I could hold up to his face and watch him crumble.

---

I could stop now.

The thought kept circling, insistent as the silence. I could walk away. Let the past stay buried. Let the questions remain unanswered. Let myself have this—whatever fragile thing exists between us, this man who makes me feel alive for the first time in years.

He's kind to me. He listens. When he touches me, he's gentle—reverent, even. Like I'm precious instead of broken. Like I matter to him beyond what I represent.

I could let it go.

But then I look at the photograph. The one I've been carrying for two years. The face that won't let me stop.

I pull out the notebook. The one I've filled with everything I know about him. My hands shake as I flip to the entry from February 2020—the one that started it all. The detail that first made me certain.

*Charming. Attentive. Makes you feel like you're the only person in the world. And then the mask slips.*

Every time. The same careful warmth, the same slow tightening of the trap.

I close the notebook. Press it against my chest.

No. I can't let this go. I can't let what he makes me feel erase what I've been building. I didn't come this far to leave empty-handed.

Even if getting them destroys me.

Especially because it's destroying me.

The cost has been so much higher than I expected. I thought I could keep my distance. Thought I could watch him, learn him, take what I needed without letting him under my skin.

But I didn't account for his vulnerability. His genuine fear of himself. The way he'd confessed his own suspicions to me, opened himself up without knowing what he was giving me.

I didn't account for falling for him.

And now I'm here—sleeping with him, letting him inside my body while I search for what I need, while my own heart betrays me with real feelings I have no right to have.

What kind of person does that? What kind of person becomes this?

The person I used to be would be horrified. Not just at the methods—the lies, the manipulation, the using of my body as bait—but at the transformation. That woman is gone. What's left is someone I barely recognize.

But I'm so close. So close to finishing this. To taking what I came for. To watching his face when I finally show him what I know.

I can keep this up a few more weeks. I have to.

I pick up my phone. No new messages from Josh. He's probably asleep in the mansion, unaware that I'm sitting here in the locked room, surrounded by everything I've gathered about what he might have done, wrestling with feelings I shouldn't have.

I could walk over there right now. Climb into his bed. Let him hold me. Pretend for one more night that we're just two lonely people who found each other instead of hunter and prey circling toward an inevitable confrontation.

But I stay in the loft. I stay with the obsession. With the photograph. With the need that's been eating me alive for two years. The hunger that drove me here. The thing inside me that won't stop until this is done.

"I'm not stopping," I whisper to the photograph. "Not for him. Not for anyone. No matter what it takes."

But the photograph doesn't care what I feel. Doesn't care that I'm tired. Doesn't let me stop.

Tomorrow I have to be strong again. Have to keep watching. Have to remember that every touch, every kiss, every moment of intimacy is means to an end, not connection.

Have to forget that when he looks at me, I see someone who could have been right for me in another life. Another timeline. Another world where neither of us was broken.

But this is the world we have. And in this world, he's dangerous and I can't keep hiding forever and the only way forward is through him, no matter how much it costs us both.

I close the notebook. Put the photograph back on the wall. Turn off the loft light and climb down the ladder.

Just a few more days of pretending we could be real.

---

But what if I wasn't wrong?

Josh's paranoia was escalating. His confusion was real. Either someone truly was gaslighting him, planting evidence...

Or he was deeply broken.

And if it was the second option... what did that mean?

I needed to use what I knew before it was too late. Before whatever was breaking inside him made him unpredictable.

---

I checked the time. 4:33 AM.

Josh would be awake soon. He'd been having worse insomnia lately—more nightmares, more paranoia about being drugged, more confusion. He was spiraling, and I needed to act before he fell apart completely.

I didn't know what he would do when I showed him. Whether he'd fight. Whether he'd run.

But I had to move. Had to finish this before I lost my chance.

The weight of everything was getting heavier. The pressure of secrets, the exhaustion of pretending. Something had to give.

I couldn't keep hiding the truth from him. From myself.

Not while I could still think clearly. Not while there was still time to fix this.

---

I couldn't keep still. Couldn't stop thinking about what I was about to do. What he'd believe about me when it was over.

Josh seemed kind. Broken, like me. Easy to get close to.

How could I fall for someone like him? How could I let myself feel anything but cold focus when I knew what I'd come here to do to him?

---

Back in the loft. One last time.

I arranged everything on the floor. The timeline I'd been building. The questions I'd been asking. The notebook. The photo.

And at the center of everything: him. Every pattern. Every gap. Every lie he didn't know he was telling.

I sat on the floor surrounded by months of obsession. Everything I'd sacrificed to be here. Everything I'd given up to corner him.

Now it was time to use it.

And maybe—maybe—he'd react in a way that made this easier. That confirmed what I already believed. That let me stop second-guessing and finally act.

Or maybe he'd make it harder. Maybe he'd look at me with those confused, trusting eyes and I'd have to do it anyway.

I stared at the photo until my vision blurred.

Part of me had wanted it to be easy. A clean break. One decisive moment where I held up what I knew and watched him shatter.

But Josh—the man I'd gotten to know over the past two months—made it so easy to forget. He was kind in ways that disarmed me. Attentive in ways that made me careless. He brought me coffee without being asked, noticed when I was hurting, read passages from his manuscript with the vulnerability of someone who had nothing to hide.

And he trusted me. Completely. Believed every lie I told. Let me close enough to see every crack, every weakness, every opening I could use.

This Josh made it easy to hesitate.

But what if there was more? What if the memory gaps, the confusion, the paranoia—what if it all pointed to a truth I didn't want to see?

I didn't have answers. Just questions. Just suspicions that terrified me.

Today I would ask him. Show him what I'd found. See how he reacted.

And maybe—maybe—I was wrong about all of it. Maybe my obsession had made me paranoid, desperate to find patterns where none existed.

But if I wasn't wrong... then everything I'd felt for him was just another lie I'd told myself. Another way I'd let him get too close.

And that meant the man sleeping in the mansion—the man I'd let inside me, inside my life—was something I couldn't afford to care about anymore.

I didn't have a plan beyond today. Beyond the confrontation. Beyond finally ending this.

After that... I didn't know.

I'd show him what I'd found. Lay it all out. Watch him squirm.

But I couldn't keep living like this. Suspended between what I wanted and what I'd come here to take.

---

I pressed my palms against the cold floor, steadying myself.

Not yet. Not today. I needed today to be clear-headed. Needed to be able to think, to talk, to finally confront him.

I stood slowly, legs stiff from sitting on the cold floor for hours. The ache receded, leaving only a faint echo.

I had maybe twelve hours before the stress became overwhelming. Before I lost my nerve completely.

Twelve hours to find my courage.

I checked my phone. 5:04 AM. Still dark outside. Josh would wake around 7 AM, same as always. I'd wait until 9 AM—give him time to wake up, have coffee, be ready for a conversation.

Then I'd ask him to come to the cottage.

I'd show him what I'd been hiding in the locked room.

And finally—finally—I'd know for certain. One way or the other.

And maybe—maybe—I'd be wrong. Maybe he'd have answers that made sense. Maybe I was connecting dots that didn't exist, creating conspiracy theories out of obsession and isolation and fear.

Maybe.

Or maybe I was still lying to myself. Still letting the man I'd spent the past two months living with—sleeping with, talking with, wanting despite myself—make me soft.

But either way, today was the day I'd know.

I turned off the lights. Locked both doors behind me. Climbed back down to my cottage.

Outside, the first gray light of dawn was breaking over the mountains. December 27, 2024. The day I'd finally stop hiding.

The day everything would finally end.

One way or another.


---

# CHAPTER 21: BLANK SPACES
## January - February 2025 — Josh

---

**January 8**

The photo was creased from years in my wallet. Edges worn soft. A woman and a toddler, both squinting in bright sunlight. She was laughing at something outside the frame. I was maybe two, reaching for her face with chubby fingers.

"This is all I have from before," I said, handing it to Eleanor. "Me and my mom. Someone took it—I don't know who. Social services found it in my stuff when I went into foster care."

We were in the library, fire crackling, snow falling outside. One of those January nights when the isolation was almost comfortable. When I could almost forget why we were both really here.

Eleanor took the photo carefully. Studied it.

Something happened to her face. A flicker—so brief I almost missed it. Her eyes went distant for a moment. Her breath caught.

"She's beautiful," Eleanor said quietly. Her voice sounded strange. Thick.

"You okay?"

"Fine." She handed it back, not quite meeting my eyes. "Sorry. Just... that's a lot to carry around. Not knowing what happened to her."

"I know she was murdered. That's all anyone would tell me. Case went cold. Files sealed because I was a minor." I tucked the photo back in my wallet. "I used to think if I could find out what happened—who did it, where, why—maybe I'd finally feel like a complete person. Like there wasn't this huge piece of myself missing."

Eleanor was very still. "Did you ever try to find out?"

"For years. Hit dead ends every time. Eventually I stopped. Figured some mysteries aren't meant to be solved."

She turned toward the fire. The light made shadows dance across her face, hiding her expression.

"Sometimes the answers find you," she said. "Whether you want them to or not."

I didn't know what she meant. At the time, I thought she was being philosophical.

---

**February 4**

I woke in my bed with no memory of how I got there.

Last thing I remembered was dinner with Eleanor. Wine. Conversation. Her hand on mine across the table. Then... nothing. A gap. Hours of darkness.

My head pounded. My throat was raw, like I'd been screaming.

The clock read 6:47 AM. Sunrise was a gray line on the horizon.

Something was wrong. My body told me—muscles aching, hands sore. Like I'd been exerting myself. Hard.

I pulled back the covers. Looked at my hands.

Scratches. Fresh ones. Thin red lines across my knuckles, dirt under my fingernails.

What had I done?

I got dressed. Went to find Eleanor.

She was in the cottage kitchen. Standing at the sink, her back to me when I knocked and entered.

"Morning," I said. "I... I don't remember coming home last night. What happened?"

She turned around.

Bruise on her arm. Dark purple, the size of a handprint, visible below the sleeve of her sweater.

"Eleanor—"

"It's nothing." She tugged the sleeve down. "I fell. Slipped on ice outside."

"That looks like—"

"I said it's nothing." Her voice was sharp. Final.

She wouldn't look at me. Moved around the kitchen with careful, deliberate motions, keeping distance between us.

"Please," I said. "Tell me what happened. I don't remember anything after dinner. If I—if I did something—"

She finally met my eyes. Her expression was unreadable. Fear? No—colder than fear.

"You don't remember?"

"No. Nothing. Eleanor, please—"

"Then there's nothing to tell." She handed me a cup of coffee. "Drink this. You look terrible."

The coffee was hot and bitter. I drank it and watched her watching me.

She knew. What I'd done in those missing hours—she knew.

And she wasn't going to tell me.

I kept thinking about the way Eleanor had looked at my mother's photo. That moment of stillness when I'd shown it to her. The way she'd handed it back without comment, her expression carefully neutral.

What had she seen? What had she recognized?

Or was I imagining things again? Finding patterns in nothing, the way I always did. The way this place made me do.

M+L. The initials circled back to me late at night when I couldn't sleep. What if M was Eleanor's real initial? What if Moore was an alias, and her actual name started with M? What if L was someone she'd brought here before me—another guest, another target?

The thought made my skin crawl. M+L. Malcolm and Laura. Max and Louise. Mystery and Lie.

I was spiraling. I knew that. But knowing didn't stop the spiral.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 22: CERTAINTY
## January - February 2025 — Eleanor

---

**January 8**

The photo hit me harder than I expected.

Something about that photo made my chest tight. The woman holding the toddler. The rawness of it—him as a child, reaching for someone who would be taken from him. Another weapon he didn't know he was giving me.

"She's beautiful," I said. My voice came out wrong—thick, unsteady. I handed the photo back and smoothed my expression. Sympathetic. The performance he expected.

"That's a lot to carry around," I said. "Not knowing what happened to her."

But inside, my mind was racing. His mother was murdered. Case never solved. Files sealed. And now he'd handed me another piece of his past without even realizing it.

Later, after he went to bed, I sat by the fire and let myself think.

The vulnerability of that moment. Him showing me his one keepsake. Trusting me with something precious.

He had no idea what I was planning. No idea why I was really here.

And now I understood him differently. A new piece of the puzzle. The reason he kept coming back to this place.

I went to the loft. Looked at the boxes. The files. Everything I'd been collecting.

The woman in his photo. Something about her unsettled me. The vulnerability of it—him as a toddler, reaching for a woman he'd never see again. It stirred something dark. Something I could use.

His past. Whatever happened to drive him here. Whatever wound kept pulling him back to this godforsaken place.

Now Josh was circling Thornewood like a moth to flame. Something broken inside him that he couldn't name, couldn't escape. And I was losing my chance to prove what that brokenness had made him do.

And when the time came, I'd finally use everything I'd collected against him.

---

**February 4**

He doesn't remember.

Good.

I watched him at breakfast, searching my face for answers. The bruise on my arm throbbed under my sleeve. He kept glancing at it, trying to piece together what happened. What he did.

Three in the morning. He picked my lock in under a minute—I heard the soft click from my bedroom, the door swinging open. He walked in like he owned the place. But something was off. His eyes were glassy, unfocused. He looked right through me—like a sleepwalker who doesn't know where he is.

He grabbed my arm first—hard, wrenching, fingers digging in like a vise. Then he slammed me against the wall. His hands moved to my throat—not squeezing, not yet, but positioned. Ready.

And then he stopped. Blinked. Looked confused. Walked out without a word. Back to the mansion. Back to bed.

I sat on my kitchen floor for an hour afterward, shaking. Checking that my throat wasn't bruised. That I could still breathe. That I was still alive.

Now he was here, asking what happened, and I wasn't going to tell him.

"It's nothing," I said. "I fell. Slipped on ice outside."

But he won't forget. He'll see that bruise every time he looks at me. He'll wonder what else he's capable of. What else he might have done that he doesn't remember.

The doubt will eat at him. And when it does, everything else will start to unravel.

This is how it begins.

Now I was certain. Whatever I'd seen that night wasn't a one-time thing.

Part of me had wondered if maybe the violence was over. If whatever drove those outbursts had burned itself out. If he'd somehow stopped.

But I'd seen it with my own eyes. Those glassy, unseeing eyes. Those hands around my throat. Whatever was happening to him—it was still there. Still dangerous. Still capable of anything.

Now I had to survive long enough to finish this.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 23: TWELVE HOURS
## February 28, 5:17 AM — Josh

---

I barricaded the mansion door at 5:17 AM, late February darkness still thick outside.

Heavy oak desk from the study. Two chairs from the dining room. The antique coat rack. Anything I could move quietly, I stacked against the main entrance. The wood scraped against wood, but I didn't care anymore about noise. About waking anyone. There was no one to wake except me.

The back door I locked and wedged with a broom handle jammed under the knob. Kitchen door—same. Both would hold against a casual push. Both would give me warning if she tried to force entry.

I worked methodically. Checking each window on the ground floor. Testing each lock. My hands shook the entire time—adrenaline, I insisted, not fear—but I kept moving. Kept planning. Because stopping meant thinking, and thinking meant confronting what I'd seen on that footage.

My body. My face. Moving through the mansion like a machine.

Not me. Never me. What she'd made me into.

By the time dawn broke fully—gray February light filtering through snow-heavy clouds—I'd fortified every ground-floor entrance. The mansion was my stronghold now. She was outside, in the cottage. And I had twelve hours. Maybe less.

Twelve hours to figure out what she'd been making me do during those controlled states.

Twelve hours to gather enough evidence to survive this.

Twelve hours before the roads cleared and I could run.

---

I returned to the library. Set the laptop on the desk. But I didn't review the footage again. Not yet. First, I needed to think. To organize. To be the investigator I'd trained myself to be.

I opened a fresh document. Titled it: EVIDENCE OF MANIPULATION.

Then I started writing.

---

**THE CASE AGAINST ELEANOR MOORE**
**Compiled by Josh Caine**
**February 28, 2025 - 6:03 AM**

**Overview:**
Eleanor Moore (alias: groundskeeper) has been conducting a systematic campaign of chemical manipulation, gaslighting, and evidence planting for months. The following impossibilities can only be explained by her active involvement in framing me for crimes I did not commit.

---

**IMPOSSIBILITY #1: The Muddy Boots**

**Evidence:** Boots found in my closet with mud composition matching burial sites. Wear patterns allegedly matching my gait.

**Why It's Impossible:** I never bought these boots. I only noticed them when Eleanor asked why I had two pairs of the same style. They appeared in my closet without explanation.

**Eleanor's Method:** She purchased identical boots in my size. Wore them herself to create the wear patterns and mud traces. Then planted them in my closet while I was drugged. Or she drugged me and made me wear them during controlled states, directing me to walk specific routes to create the "evidence."

**Conclusion:** The boots are planted evidence designed to link me to burial sites.

---

**IMPOSSIBILITY #2: Memory Inconsistencies**

**Evidence:** I have conflicting memories about basic purchases. Journal entry from October mentions buying a vintage watch at a Portland antique shop. But I don't remember buying it. Don't remember the shop. The watch on my wrist feels unfamiliar.

**Why It's Strange:** How do you forget buying a watch? Why would I journal about it if it wasn't real?

**Possible Explanations:**
1. Eleanor forged the journal entry, planted the watch, trying to make me doubt my own timeline
2. I bought it but was already experiencing memory issues even before arriving at Thornewood
3. It's a normal purchase I forgot because I was stressed about the trip

**Conclusion:** Inconclusive. Could be stress-induced forgetfulness, could be another planted doubt. I can't rule out either possibility.

---

**IMPOSSIBILITY #3: The Surveillance Footage Gaps**

**Evidence:** My own surveillance system shows mysterious gaps during "episodes." Cameras disabled, then re-enabled. Footage showing me moving with mechanical precision.

**Why It's Impossible:** I set up that system. I encrypted it. I'm the only one with access.

**Eleanor's Method:** She hacked my system remotely. She's been in the mansion for months, had plenty of time to install backdoors, keyloggers, or physical access to my laptop. She can view my feeds, delete footage, even add footage. The "mechanical" movements I see could be deepfaked—AI-generated video of me doing things I never did.

**Alternative:** She drugs me with something that makes me compliant, then directs me to disable my own cameras, making it look like I'm hiding something when I'm actually following her commands.

**Conclusion:** My surveillance system has been compromised. The footage proves her sophistication, not my guilt.

---

**IMPOSSIBILITY #4: The Guest Book Signature**

**Evidence:** The guest book contains my handwriting under a fake name, dated February 2020. Handwriting analysis would likely confirm it's mine.

**Why It's Impossible:** I wasn't here in February 2020. I don't remember signing any guest book. I've never heard the name Marcus Webb.

**Eleanor's Method:** Handwriting can be forged with enough practice. She's had access to my notes, my journal, dozens of samples. Or she obtained a document I signed during a drugged state and transferred it somehow. Or the signature isn't actually mine—it needs to look close enough to raise suspicion.

**Conclusion:** Forged evidence or drugged-state manipulation. The signature proves nothing except her sophistication.

---

**IMPOSSIBILITY #5: The 2020 Disappearance Timeline**

**Evidence:** A young woman disappeared from Thornewood in early 2020, during the exact months the guest book shows I allegedly worked here.

**Why It's Impossible:** I wasn't here. Someone else killed her. Someone else was here that winter. The police had other suspects. I wasn't the only person with access.

**Eleanor's Connection:** What if Eleanor killed the victim herself? What if she's the one who was here in 2020, and this whole thing—the surveillance, the frame job—is her covering her tracks by pinning it on me? That would explain the obsession. The elaborate setup. The months of planning.

This isn't about solving a random cold case. This is personal for her. She's framing me because she needs someone to take the fall for what she did.

**Conclusion:** Eleanor is the real killer. That's why she's here. That's why she's doing this.

---

**IMPOSSIBILITY #6: The Locked Room in Her Cottage**

**Evidence:** Eleanor has a locked room in the loft of her cottage that she's never let me into. When asked about it, she deflects.

**Why It's Suspicious:** What's she hiding?

**Likely Contents:**
- Surveillance equipment to monitor me
- Chemical compounds she's been using for the drugging
- Forged documents, planted evidence she hasn't deployed yet
- Research on me—my background, my writing, my patterns
- Evidence of her real identity

**Conclusion:** The locked room is her command center. Where she plans my destruction.

---

I sat back from the laptop. Read through the list twice.

Six impossibilities. Six pieces of "evidence" that proved my guilt.

Except none of them proved anything except Eleanor's sophistication.

She'd thought of everything. Guest book, boots, jacket, witnesses, timeline. She'd even compromised my own surveillance system. Turned my investigative tools against me.

The only thing she hadn't counted on was me seeing the footage and interpreting it correctly. Realizing it wasn't episodes or mental illness or guilt.

It was her.

It had always been her.

I saved the document. Encrypted it. Backed it up to three separate drives the same way I'd backed up the surveillance footage.

If I didn't make it out of Thornewood—at least there would be evidence of what she'd done.

---

I checked the time. 8:12 AM.

Dawn fully established now. Gray light filtering through the library windows. Snow still falling lightly.

I needed food. Water. But going to the kitchen meant leaving the library, meant moving through the mansion, meant vulnerability.

I compromised. Filled two water bottles from the library bathroom sink—checked the tap first, let it run for three minutes to flush any potential airborne drugs from the pipes. Probably paranoid. Probably ineffective. But I couldn't drink anything I hadn't personally vetted.

No food. I could survive on water for twelve hours.

I returned to the library. Locked the door from inside. Wedged a chair under the handle for extra security.

Then I pulled up the surveillance footage again. Not to review the episodes. To study Eleanor.

I switched through camera angles. Cottage exterior. Path between buildings. Mansion perimeter.

She was awake. Smoke rose from the cottage chimney. Lights on in the windows.

But she hadn't emerged yet. Hadn't tried to approach.

Either she didn't know I'd seen the footage. Or she was planning her next move.

I watched. Waited. Clock ticking toward 8 AM.

My phone buzzed.

**Eleanor:** *Morning. Coffee?*

My stomach clenched. So casual. So normal. Like she wasn't a predator and I wasn't prey.

I stared at the message. Typed and deleted three responses.

Finally: *Busy. Maybe later.*

Three dots appeared immediately. Eleanor typing.

**Eleanor:** *Everything okay?*

No. Everything was not okay. Everything was the furthest possible thing from okay.

**Me:** *Fine. Just working.*

**Eleanor:** *On the book?*

Right. The book. The perfect cover story I'd maintained for months.

**Me:** *Yeah.*

**Eleanor:** *I'll leave you alone then. Let me know if you need anything.*

I didn't respond. Turned the phone face-down on the desk.

She knew. Of course she knew. She'd been monitoring me for months. She probably had the cottage rigged with listening devices, cameras I hadn't found yet. She knew I'd barricaded the doors. Knew I was scared.

This text exchange was theater. Maintaining the fiction. Giving me enough rope to hang myself.

I pulled up the perimeter cameras again.

At 8:47 AM, the cottage door opened.

Eleanor emerged. Wearing her usual winter gear—heavy coat, boots, gloves. Carrying a thermos, it looked like from this distance.

She started walking toward the mansion.

My heart rate spiked.

She was coming here. Now. In broad daylight.

Bringing what? Coffee? Poisoned coffee? Or establishing that everything was normal, nothing to see, checking on poor paranoid Josh?

I watched her approach on camera. Steady walk. No hurry. Casual.

She reached the front door. I heard her try the handle.

Rattle. Resistance. The barricade holding.

Then her voice, muffled through oak and furniture: "Josh? Are you in there?"

I didn't move. Didn't breathe. Pressed myself against the library wall, out of sight of the windows.

"Josh, I know you're in there. Your truck is here. Please. I just want to talk."

Talk. Right. Talk while whatever chemical she'd spiked the coffee with worked its way into my bloodstream.

"The roads are clearing," she called. "Detective Williams texted. They'll be here by tonight. I know you're scared. I know you found something. But Josh—please. Let me in. Let me explain."

Explain what? How she'd been drugging me? How she'd forged the guest book? How she'd turned me into her puppet?

"I'm not going to hurt you," she said quietly. "I promise. I just... I need to show you the truth. It will help you understand. Please."

I stayed frozen. Silent. Every instinct screaming not to engage, not to reveal my position, not to give her any advantage.

After a long moment—thirty seconds, a minute, longer—I heard her footsteps retreating from the door.

But not far.

I shifted to the window. Careful. Staying out of sight. Peered through a gap in the curtain.

She was sitting on the front steps. Back against the door. Thermos beside her. Phone in hand.

Settling in. Waiting.

Trying to outlast me.

---

At 9:30, she was still there.

At 10:15, she stood. Stretched. Walked around the perimeter of the mansion. Testing windows. Checking the back door. Assessing my defenses.

At 10:47, she returned to the cottage.

I watched her go. Tracked her path on camera. She went inside. Closed the door.

Twenty minutes later, she emerged again. This time carrying nothing. Just walking back toward the mansion.

What was she doing? Wearing me down? Establishing a pattern?

She reached the front door again. Knocked. "Josh. Please. We need to talk before the police arrive."

Silence from me.

"I know what you're thinking," she said. Her voice was different now. More strained. "I know what you think you saw on that footage. But you're wrong. You're interpreting it wrong."

Was I? Or was this more gaslighting?

"What you're experiencing is real," she continued. "The episodes are real. The lost time is real. But the cause isn't what you think."

My breath caught. Was she about to confess? Admit to the drugging?

"It's not me," she said. "I'm not doing this to you. I'm not drugging you or controlling you or whatever you think. What's happening is... it's complicated. And I can't explain it through a locked door."

Liar. Every word calculated. Every pause strategic.

"There's evidence in my cottage," she said. "In the locked room. Truth. Everything you need to understand what's really happening. But you have to let me in. You have to trust me. One last time."

Trust her. The woman who'd been systematically destroying my life for months.

Not a chance.

"Josh, please. The police will be here in eight hours. Eight hours for you to understand the truth before they take you away. Don't waste it hiding."

I heard her move. Sit down again. Back against the door.

"I'll wait," she said quietly. "I'll wait as long as it takes."

---

11:23 AM.

She'd been out there for over two hours now. Alternating between sitting, walking the perimeter, trying to talk to me through the door.

I'd stayed in the library. Silent. Watching her on camera. Refusing to engage.

I couldn't do this for eight more hours. Couldn't hide and wait for the police to arrive.

Because what would I tell them? A woman has been drugging me? Based on what proof? Surveillance footage that showed me moving mechanically—which she could easily claim was sleepwalking or stress-induced behavior?

I needed more than footage. I needed the chemicals themselves. The delivery system. The locked room in her cottage.

But Eleanor was between me and the cottage. Watching. Waiting.

And I was trapped inside.

---

At noon, she tried again.

"Josh." Knocking on the door. Gentle. Persistent. "Please. Let's talk about this."

I'd retreated to the study. Small room off the main foyer with a view of the front entrance through the window. Close enough to hear her approach. Far enough to barricade if needed.

I'd grabbed the fireplace poker from the library on my way. Heavy iron. Comforting weight.

"I know you saw the footage," Eleanor called. "I know you're trying to make sense of it. And I know what you must be thinking."

That you've been drugging me. That you've been controlling me. That you're dangerous.

Yeah. I'm thinking that.

"But Josh—what if you're wrong? What if there's another explanation?"

There isn't. I've been through every possibility. The list proves it. Six impossibilities that can only be explained by her manipulation.

"What if the control isn't coming from outside?" Her voice was quieter now. Closer to the door. "What if it's inside you?"

I pressed against the wall. Out of sight of the window. Listening.

"I can't explain it through a locked door," she said. "But I can show you. If you let me in. If you trust me one last time."

Trust. The word was almost funny.

"There's evidence in my cottage," she continued. "In the locked room. Truth. Everything you need to understand what's really happening."

The locked room. Exactly where I need to get.

"Josh, please. The police will be here in seven hours. Seven hours for you to understand before they take you away. Don't waste it hiding from me."

Take me away. Interesting choice of words.

Because she knows—when the police arrive, I'll tell them everything. About the drugging. The manipulation. The evidence planting. And her elaborate frame job will collapse.

Unless she stops me first.

I heard her move. Footsteps retreating from the door. Walking away.

I shifted to the window. Peered through a gap in the curtain.

She was walking back to the cottage. Shoulders slumped. Defeated.

Or pretending to be.

I watched her disappear inside.

---

1:34 PM.

Eleanor hadn't returned. The cottage chimney showed smoke. Lights on. She was home. But not attempting contact.

Regrouping. Planning her next move.

I reviewed my options.

The encrypted drive I'd made in December—the one with copies of Eleanor's files—was gone. I'd hidden it in my laptop bag, but when I'd checked this morning, the pocket was empty. She must have found it. Of course she had. She had cameras everywhere, probably watched me break into her cottage in real-time. She'd had weeks to search my things while I slept, retrieve her files, destroy the evidence of her surveillance.

Which meant the only proof of what she'd been doing was still in that locked room.

**Option A:** Stay barricaded until police arrive (6.5 hours).
- Pros: Safe. Can't be drugged if I don't interact with her.
- Cons: No evidence. She took back the drive. Police might believe her story over mine.

**Option B:** Confront her. Demand access to the locked room.
- Pros: Get the evidence I need. Prove the drugging.
- Cons: Puts me in direct contact. Vulnerable to chemical attack or physical violence.

**Option C:** Break into the cottage while she's distracted. Get into the locked room myself.
- Pros: Get evidence without confrontation. I've been in there once—I know what to look for.
- Cons: Breaking and entering. Could contaminate evidence legally. Might encounter booby traps.

None good. All risky.

But doing nothing meant letting her control the narrative when police arrived.

I needed that locked room.

---

At 2:18 PM, my phone buzzed.

**Eleanor:** *I'm going to try one more time. Please read this.*

Three dots. She was typing a response.

Then a wall of text appeared:

**Eleanor:** *Josh, I know you think I've been drugging you. I know you think I'm framing you. I know you've built an entire theory about how I forged evidence and manipulated everything. And I understand why you'd think that. It makes sense from your perspective. But you're wrong. Not about everything being strange - you're right about that. But you're wrong about who's responsible. It's not me doing this to you. I'm not your enemy. I have information. Things I've found. Things in the locked room that will explain everything. I'm terrified of you right now. Terrified OF you and FOR you. But I'm also the only person who understands what's happening. Please. The police are coming. Before they get here, before this gets worse, let me show you what I know. You deserve to see it. You deserve to understand. Please, Josh. Let me in. Trust me one more time.
-E*

I stared at the message.

"Things in the locked room." "Information that will explain everything." "You deserve to understand."

No.

No, that was exactly what she'd want me to believe. Draw me into the cottage, show me her fabricated evidence, make me think I'm insane. Make me doubt my own sanity. Classic manipulation.

I deleted the message.

Turned the phone face-down.

Didn't respond.

---

3:47 PM.

The sun was starting its descent. February afternoon. Light already fading.

A little over four hours until police arrived.

Eleanor hadn't tried again. Hadn't called. Hadn't texted. Hadn't approached the mansion.

Silence.

Which was somehow worse than the attempts to reach me.

What was she doing in there?

I pulled up the cottage exterior camera. Zoomed as tight as possible.

Curtains drawn. No movement visible. Just smoke from the chimney.

Waiting. Like me.

Both of us trapped in our respective strongholds. Counting hours.

---

At 5:03 PM, full dark now, movement on the perimeter cameras.

I switched to cottage exterior view.

Eleanor's door opening. She stepped out. No coat. No boots. Just standing on her porch, looking toward the mansion.

Looking at me.

Even though she couldn't possibly see me through the mansion windows at this distance in the dark.

She stood there for a long moment. Breath visible in the cold air. Then she went back inside.

What was that? A signal? A threat? Just checking if I was still here?

I pulled up the mansion exterior cameras. Checked every angle. No movement. No one approaching.

But the sense of being watched intensified.

---

5:47 PM.

I was in the study now. The small room off the foyer with the best view of the front entrance.

The fireplace poker leaned against the wall beside me. Heavy iron. Solid. Real.

I'd moved it here an hour ago. Just in case.

Just in case she tried to come in. Just in case I needed to defend myself. Just in case this ended badly.

The poker's weight was comforting. A reminder that I wasn't helpless. That I could fight back if needed.

I pulled up the surveillance feeds one more time. Cottage. Path. Mansion perimeter.

And froze.

Eleanor was walking toward the mansion.

Not hurrying. Not creeping. Just walking steadily through the darkness. Hands in pockets. No weapon visible. No threat posture.

Just approaching.

My heart hammered.

This was it. The confrontation I'd been dreading and preparing for all day.

I grabbed the poker. Moved to the window. Stayed out of sight but watched her approach.

She reached the front steps. Climbed them slowly. Stood at the door.

Knocked. Gentle. Three taps.

"Josh." Her voice muffled through the oak and barricade. "I know you're in there. I know you're scared. I know you think I'm the enemy."

I didn't respond. Gripped the poker tighter.

"I'm not armed," she said. "I'm not here to hurt you. I just want to talk. Please."

Silence from me.

"The police called," she continued. "They'll be here in two hours. Maybe less. Roads are clearing faster than expected. They're coming to take statements. And Josh—when they get here, when you tell them your theory about drugging and framing and all of it—they're not going to believe you."

My jaw clenched. Of course they wouldn't. She'd spent months laying groundwork.
