"But there's still time," Eleanor said. "Two hours for you to see the truth. To understand what's really happening before they take you away."

Take me away. Interesting phrasing.

"The locked room in my cottage," she said. "Everything is in there. Evidence. Truth. Answers. I'll show you. All of it. No more hiding. No more deflecting. You want to know what's in that room? I'll open it. Right now. Tonight. Before the police arrive."

I pressed against the wall. This was a trap. Had to be.

"But Josh—you have to leave the mansion. You have to come to the cottage. And you have to trust me. One last time."

Trust her. The woman who'd been destroying my life for months.

"I know you won't," she said quietly. "I know you can't. Not after everything. But I'm asking anyway. Because in two hours, it'll be too late. The police will arrive. They'll take you. And you'll never know the truth."

She was quiet for a moment.

"I'll be in the cottage," she said finally. "Waiting. If you change your mind. If you want answers. The door will be unlocked. You can bring the poker if it makes you feel safer. You can keep your distance. But please. Come see what I've been building. What I've found. What you need to understand."

Footsteps retreating. Fading.

I shifted to the window. Watched her walk back down the path toward the cottage. She didn't look back.

Didn't run. Didn't rush. Just walked slowly through the snow.

Like she had all the time in the world.

Like she knew I'd come.

---

I stood in the study for ten minutes after she left. Poker in hand. Mind racing.

Two hours until police arrived.

Two hours to decide.

Stay barricaded. Present my case when they got here. Risk them believing her over me.

Or go to the cottage. See the locked room. Get the evidence I needed.

Walk straight into whatever trap she'd prepared.

I pulled up the surveillance feeds. Cottage windows glowing. Smoke from chimney. Eleanor's silhouette moving past a window.

Waiting.

The locked room. That's what this had always been about. That's where the proof was. Drugs. Forged documents. Her real identity. Everything I needed to expose her.

But getting there meant leaving my stronghold. Meant going to her territory. Meant making myself vulnerable.

Unless I was smart about it.

I reviewed the mansion cameras one last time. Made sure everything was recording. Set the system to upload to cloud storage every five minutes. If I didn't come back, at least there would be evidence of where I'd gone.

Then I grabbed the poker.

Pulled on my coat.

Checked that my phone was fully charged. Emergency contacts ready.

And unlocked the front door.

---

The walk to the cottage was like walking to my execution.

A hundred yards through darkness and snow. Exposed. Vulnerable. The poker gripped in my right hand. Phone in my left pocket, 911 ready to dial.

Every step crunched too loud. Every shadow could hide a threat. Every window in the cottage could have Eleanor watching, waiting, planning.

But I kept walking.

Because I needed to know. Needed to see that locked room. Needed proof.

Even if it killed me.

I reached the cottage door. Put my hand on the knob.

Unlocked. Just like she'd said.

I pushed it open. Slowly.

Warm air. Fireplace burning. The familiar small space. Kitchen table. Two chairs. Everything exactly as I'd seen it dozens of times before.

Eleanor sat at the table. Hands folded. No weapon. No threat. Just watching me with those sad, tired eyes.

"You came," she said softly.

I stayed in the doorway. Poker ready. "Show me the room."

"Josh—"

"No talking. No explaining. Just show me the fucking room."

She stood slowly. Hands visible. Non-threatening.

"Upstairs," she said. "Follow me."

She walked to the ladder. Started climbing.

I followed. Keeping distance. Poker between us.

We reached the loft. Small bedroom. Bathroom. Storage.

And the locked door.

Eleanor pulled out a key. Her hand shook slightly as she unlocked it.

"Before you see this," she said quietly, "you need to understand. I didn't want it to be true. I spent two years hoping I was wrong."

"Open it."

She turned the key. Pushed the door open.

Stepped aside.

"This is everything," she said. "Everything I've found. Everything I've documented since I started looking."

I looked past her into the room.

And my world shattered.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 24: THE BARRICADE
## February 28 — Eleanor

---

He'd seen the footage.

I knew it the moment I woke at 6 AM and checked my phone. No good morning text. No response to yesterday's messages. Just silence.

Josh always texted in the morning. Always. Even on days when we were both busy, when we barely saw each other, he'd send something. *Coffee?* or *Morning* or *Sleep okay?*

Nothing today.

I pulled up the cottage surveillance feeds on my laptop. The cameras I'd hidden months ago. The ones Josh didn't know about.

Mansion exterior: No movement. Curtains drawn. Front door—

I zoomed in.

Furniture stacked against it. Visible through the narrow window. Chairs. Desk. A barricade.

My stomach dropped.

He'd seen the footage. And he'd interpreted it exactly wrong.

---

I got dressed. Made coffee I couldn't drink. Paced the cottage kitchen trying to figure out what to do.

The plan had been to show him the evidence room today. To sit him down, gently, and walk him through what I'd found. Everything I'd been holding back. Everything I needed him to see.

I'd rehearsed it a hundred times. How to say it. How to help him understand without destroying him completely.

But he'd found his own surveillance footage first. Seen himself during those episodes. And his investigator's brain had done what investigator brains do: constructed a theory to explain the evidence.

The wrong theory.

He thought I was drugging him. Controlling him. Framing him.

He thought I was the monster.

I checked the feeds again. No movement from the mansion. All curtains drawn. No signs of life except smoke from one chimney.

He was in there. Barricaded. Terrified. Armed, probably.

Waiting for me to make a move.

Or waiting for the police to arrive so he could tell them his theory. About drugging and manipulation and evidence planting.

And when he did, when he told Detective Williams that I'd been keeping information from him, that I had a locked room he wasn't allowed to see, that I'd been lying about who I was—

Everything I'd come here for would be lost. The truth would be buried under his paranoia. And Josh would never get the answers he needed.

I had to reach him. Had to show him the truth. Before the police arrived.

Before it was too late.

---

At 8:47 AM, I tried the direct approach.

Walked to the mansion. Thermos of coffee in hand. Casual. Non-threatening.

Just Eleanor checking on Josh. Nothing unusual.

I knocked on the front door. "Josh? Are you in there?"

Silence.

"Josh, I know you're in there. Your truck is here. Please. I just want to talk."

No response. But he was listening. His breathing was almost audible on the other side of all that furniture.

"The roads are clearing," I called. "Detective Williams texted. They'll be here by tonight. I know you're scared. I know you found something. But Josh—please. Let me in. Let me explain."

The police part was a stretch. Williams had checked in yesterday, asked if we needed anything. I'd told him the roads were still too bad. But Josh didn't need to know that.

More silence.

"I'm not going to hurt you," I said quietly. "I promise. I just... I need to show you the truth. It will help you understand. Please."

I waited. Thirty seconds. A minute.

Nothing.

Finally, I heard movement. Footsteps retreating. Moving away from the door.

He wasn't going to let me in.

I sat on the front steps. Back against the door. Set the thermos beside me. Pulled out my phone.

And waited.

---

At 9:30, I was still there.

The February cold seeping through my coat. My hands numb even inside gloves. But I didn't move.

He had cameras. He was watching. I wanted him to see that I wasn't going anywhere. That I was patient. That I wasn't a threat.

That I cared.

At 10:15, I stood. Stretched. Walked the perimeter of the mansion slowly. Not testing defenses. Just... present. Visible. Showing him I wasn't hiding.

Every window was covered. Every door secured. He'd done thorough work.

Smart. Methodical. Exactly what I'd expect from an investigator who thought he was under attack.

At 10:47, I walked back to the cottage. Made more coffee. Checked my laptop.

Mansion feeds showed no movement. Just smoke from the chimney. Curtains still drawn.

Standoff.

---

At noon, I tried again.

Knocked on the door. Gentle. Patient. "Josh. Please. Let's talk about this."

Silence.

"I know you saw the footage," I said. "I know you're trying to make sense of it. And I know what you must be thinking."

That I've been drugging you. That I'm dangerous. That you need to survive until help arrives.

"But Josh—what if you're wrong? What if there's another explanation?"

I pressed my hand against the door. Wishing I could reach through it. Touch him. Make him understand.

"What if the control isn't coming from outside?" I said quietly. "What if it's inside you?"

No answer.

"I can't explain it through a locked door," I said. "But I can show you. If you let me in. If you trust me one last time."

I heard movement. Close to the door.

He was listening.

"There's something in my cottage," I continued. "In the locked room. Evidence. Truth. Everything you need to understand what's really happening."

The locked room. The thing he'd been curious about for months. The one place I'd never let him see.

"Josh, please. The police will be here in seven hours. Seven hours for you to understand the truth before they take you away. Don't waste it hiding from me."

Silence again. Then footsteps. Retreating.

I sat down. Back against the door.

"I'll wait," I said quietly. "I'll wait as long as it takes."

---

1:34 PM.

I'd gone back to the cottage an hour ago. Too cold to sit outside anymore. My fingers wouldn't work properly. My fingers especially—the cold making them stiff and clumsy, or maybe stress, exhaustion, the weight of everything finally breaking me down.

I sat at the kitchen table. Laptop open. Watching the mansion feeds.

No movement. No change.

I thought about calling Williams. Asking him to come early. To bring negotiators or mental health professionals or someone who could talk Josh down.

But that would mean admitting the situation was out of control. Would mean revealing why I'm really here before I was ready. Would mean Josh learning the truth with cops present, in custody, his whole world collapsing in the worst possible way.

No. This had to be private. Personal. Just him and me.

The way it should have been from the beginning.

I pulled up my phone. Started typing a text. Deleted it. Tried again.

How do you explain what I've discovered in a text message? How do you tell someone the truth about what they've done when they have no memory of it?

I tried anyway.

---

At 2:18 PM, I sent it.

A long message. Maybe too long. Trying to explain what I'd discovered. Why I'd been watching him. What I needed him to understand.

Telling him I wasn't his enemy. That I was trying to help. That the memory gaps he experienced weren't what he thought. That the locked room would explain everything.

Asking him to trust me.

I hit send. Watched the message deliver.

Three dots appeared immediately. Josh typing.

Then disappeared.

He'd read it. Started to respond. Stopped.

I waited. Ten minutes. Twenty.

No reply.

He'd deleted whatever he'd been typing. Dismissed my message. Maybe thought it was more gaslighting. More manipulation.

I set the phone down. Stared at the ceiling.

Seven hours. Maybe less. Then Williams would arrive with backup and this would all be over.

Josh would be arrested. Would go to trial. Would spend the rest of his life facing what the evidence showed—without remembering any of it.

And I'd have failed. Failed to help him. Failed to give him understanding before the end.

Failed everyone.

---

At 3:47 PM, I made a decision.

I couldn't reach him through words. Couldn't convince him through texts or door conversations or waiting patiently.

He needed to see. Needed evidence that couldn't be disputed or dismissed or rationalized away.

The locked room. That was the only thing that could reach him now.

But he wouldn't come to the cottage. Wouldn't leave his stronghold.

Unless I gave him a reason.

I checked the time. 3:47 PM. Police would arrive around 8 PM, Williams had said. A little over four hours.

Four hours to convince Josh to walk into what he thought was a trap.

Four hours to save him.

---

At 5:03 PM, I stepped onto the cottage porch.

No coat. No preparation. Just stepped outside into the cold and looked toward the mansion.

Josh wasn't visible. But he had cameras. He was watching.

I stood there. Breath visible. Letting him see I was alone. Unarmed. Just a woman standing in the cold.

Then I went back inside.

A signal. A message. I'm here. I'm not hiding. I'm waiting for you.

---

At 5:47 PM, I walked to the mansion.

This time I had a plan. A script. Words I'd been rehearsing for the past hour.

I climbed the front steps slowly. Knocked. Three gentle taps.

"Josh." Voice calm. Steady. "I know you're in there. I know you're scared. I know you think I'm the enemy."

I waited. No response. But he was there. Close. On the other side of the barricade.

"I'm not armed," I said. "I'm not here to hurt you. I just want to talk. Please."

Silence.

"The police called," I continued. "They'll be here in two hours. Maybe less. Roads are clearing faster than expected. They're coming to take statements. And Josh—when they get here, when you tell them your theory about drugging and framing and all of it—they're not going to believe you."

It was a lie. Williams had texted to check on us, asked if we needed anything. I'd told him we were fine, roads still too bad for a visit. He'd said he'd try again in a few days when the weather broke.

But Josh didn't need to know that. He needed pressure. A deadline. A reason to stop hiding and finally face me.

"But there's still time," I said. "Two hours for you to see the truth. To understand what's really happening before they take you away."

I leaned my forehead against the door. Closed my eyes.

"The locked room in my cottage. Everything is in there. Evidence. Truth. Answers. I'll show you. All of it. No more hiding. No more deflecting. You want to know what's in that room? I'll open it. Right now. Tonight. Before the police arrive."

My voice cracked. I steadied it.

"But Josh—you have to leave the mansion. You have to come to the cottage. And you have to trust me. One last time."

I knew what I was asking. Knew it was impossible. But I asked anyway.

"I know you won't," I said quietly. "I know you can't. Not after everything. But I'm asking anyway. Because in two hours, it'll be too late. The police will arrive. They'll take you. And you'll never know the truth."

I stepped back from the door.

"I'll be in the cottage," I said. "Waiting. If you change your mind. If you want answers. The door will be unlocked. You can bring the poker if it makes you feel safer. You can keep your distance. But please. Come see what I've been building. What I've found. What you need to understand."

I walked away. Didn't look back. Didn't rush.

Just walked slowly through the snow. Giving him time to watch. To decide. To think.

---

I sat in the cottage for forty-seven minutes.

Watching the laptop. Mansion exterior cameras. Waiting to see if the door would open.

Knowing it probably wouldn't.

Knowing Josh would stay barricaded. Stay convinced I was the threat. Stay trapped in his paranoid theory until police arrived and took him away still believing he was the victim.

At 6:34 PM, movement on the camera.

The front door of the mansion opening.

Josh stepping out. Coat on. Something in his hand—the poker, I realized. He'd armed himself.

He looked at the cottage for a long moment. Then started walking.

Slowly. Carefully. Like approaching a minefield.

But walking.

My heart hammered. This was it. The moment I'd been working toward for two years. The moment where he'd finally see. Finally understand.

Or the moment where everything fell apart.

I stood. Moved to the kitchen table. Sat down. Hands folded. Trying to look non-threatening. Calm. Safe.

The cottage door opened.

Josh stood in the doorway. Poker ready. Eyes scanning for threats. Finding none.

Finding only me.

"You came," I said softly.

He stayed in the doorway. Weapon between us. "Show me the room."

"Josh—"

"No talking. No explaining. Just show me the fucking room."

His voice was flat. Dead. The voice of someone pushed past breaking.

I stood slowly. Hands visible.

"Upstairs," I said. "Follow me."

---

We climbed in silence.

Me first. Josh behind. The poker's presence like a third person on the stairs.

I reached the loft. The locked door.

My hands shook as I pulled out the key. This was it. Five years of searching. The better part of a year here at Thornewood. All the evidence I'd gathered. All the truth I'd been holding back.

All about to be laid bare.

"Before you see this," I said quietly, "you need to understand. I didn't want it to be true. I spent two years hoping I was wrong."

"Open it."

I turned the key. Pushed the door open.

Stepped aside.

"This is everything," I said. "Everything I've found. Everything I've been documenting for two years."

Josh stepped forward. Looked into the room.

And I watched his face as the truth destroyed him.

The walls covered in photographs and timelines and maps. Documents and dates and connections spanning years. Evidence of things he didn't remember. Places he'd been. People he'd met.

All connected. All proven. All undeniable.

His knees buckled. The poker clattered to the floor.

"What..." His voice failed.

I caught him before he fell. Helped him to the floor. Sat beside him.

And waited. Knowing that tomorrow, everything would change. That the evidence room would show him what I couldn't say. That he would finally understand what he was.

What we both were now.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 25: THE BOXES
## February 28 — Josh

---

"Did I kill them?"

The question hung in the air between us.

Eleanor's hand on my shoulder. Steady. Grounding.

"Yes," she whispered. "I think so. Yes."

The poker lay on the floor where I'd dropped it. Forgotten. Useless. What good was a weapon against the truth?

I couldn't move. Couldn't speak. Couldn't process what I was seeing.

The walls.

Every surface covered with photos, timeline, maps, documents—string connecting points across years and cities and faces.

Six faces.

Six people staring at me from the walls.

And my face among them. Dozens of photos of me. Surveillance shots. Candid moments. Images I didn't remember being taken.

All connected.

All proven.

All impossible to deny.

"I need..." My voice cracked. "I need to see. All of it. Every piece."

Eleanor helped me to my feet. Guided me further into the room.

"Take your time," she said quietly. "I'll explain anything you want. Answer any question. Just... take your time."

She paused. Something shifted in her expression.

"Before we go through everything," Eleanor said, "there's something you should know. Three days ago, I mailed a package to your agent. Rachel Winters. Everything you're about to see—copies of all of it. She has instructions: if she doesn't hear from me by March 20th, she opens the package and contacts the FBI."

My stomach dropped. "You—"

"Insurance," Eleanor said flatly. "In case something goes wrong tonight."

There was no escape. Even if I ran. Even if I hurt her. Rachel would open that package. The truth would come out.

"So whatever happens in this room," Eleanor continued, "it's already over. One way or another, the world is going to know."

---

The evidence board takes up the entire north wall.

Photographs. Documents. Timeline. Map. String connecting locations to dates to faces to—

To me.

My face is everywhere.

Center of the board: a photo of me. Professional headshot from my podcast website. But around it, radiating outward like a spider web, are dozens more. Surveillance photos. Me at the grocery store. Me at the gas station. Me arriving at Thornewood. Me carrying firewood. Me in the library. Me everywhere, always watched, always documented.

Surrounding my face: six other faces.

Six victims.

Six names written in precise block letters beneath each photo.

SARAH MARTINEZ. KEVIN CHEN (SURVIVED). LILY ASHFORD. JENNIFER WALLACE. ROBERT FISCHER. DAVID PARK.

I step closer. Can't stop myself. Can't look away.

Each victim has their own section. Photo at center. Around it: missing person reports. News clippings. Timeline of last known movements. And beneath each timeline, highlighted in yellow: a date.

Below each date: my location on that date. Documented. Proven.

Sarah Martinez: Portland, October 2019. I was in Portland, October 2019.

Lily Ashford: Thornewood, February 2020. I was at Thornewood, February 2020.

Jennifer Wallace: Portland, May 2020. I was in Portland, May 2020.

I wasn't at Thornewood that winter.

I've never been to Thornewood before October 2024.

"Yes, you were."

Eleanor's voice. I realize I said it aloud.

She crosses to a file cabinet. Pulls out a folder. Hands it to me.

Inside: employment records. Contract between Marcus Webb and Thornewood Estate Property Management. Dates: February 8-22, 2020. Winter caretaker position. Signed in my handwriting—but not my name.

A photocopy of the estate guest book. A signature that looks like mine: "Marcus Webb, February 8, 2020."

Photos. Security camera footage stills. A man who looks exactly like me carrying boxes into the mansion. February 8, 2020. Timestamp in the corner. A note in Eleanor's handwriting: *Police pulled these during investigation. Standard procedure for any property with surveillance. Got copies through PI contacts.*

"That's not possible." My hands are shaking. The folder nearly slips. "I wasn't—I would remember—"

"Would you?"

Eleanor's question hangs in the air. She doesn't wait for an answer. Just turns back to the evidence board.

"Look at it," she says quietly. "Really look."

The folder slips from my hands. Papers scatter across the floor. Employment records. Security stills. Proof that I was here, at Thornewood, in early 2020.

Proof that I don't remember.

"This isn't real." I'm backing away. Toward the door. Toward escape. "You fabricated this. You're trying to—"

"To what?" Eleanor's voice is sharp. "To frame you? To make you believe something that isn't true? Why would I do that, Josh?"

"Because you're—" I stop. Can't finish.

"Because I'm what? The killer? The monster you came here to investigate?"

She gestures to the evidence board. To my face surrounded by victims. To the timeline that matches my movements perfectly. To the documentation that spans years.

"Look at it. Really look. This isn't fabrication. This is investigation. Two years of tracking you. Documenting you. Learning your patterns."

I force myself to look. To really look.

And that's when something clicks. Something familiar about the names.

"Wait." My voice sounds distant. "Sarah Martinez. Jennifer Wallace. Robert Fischer. David Park. Kevin Chen. I... I was researching those names. The people who disappeared. I thought they were connected to Thornewood. To you."

Eleanor's expression shifts. Something that might be pity.

"You were tracking yourself," she says quietly. "Your subconscious was trying to tell you something. The disappearances you were investigating—" She moves to the evidence board, points to the photos. "Four of them were your victims. Sarah, Jennifer, Robert, David. Kevin Chen was number five—he survived. You made a connection, saw the pattern. You just didn't realize you were the pattern."

My legs won't hold me. I sit down hard.

"The fifth person on your list," Eleanor continues, pulling up a file on her laptop. "The hiker who disappeared in 2018. That one wasn't you. Wrong timeframe, wrong pattern. Your research was mostly correct, Josh. You just drew the wrong conclusion about who the killer was."

"I was hunting myself," I whisper.

"You were documenting yourself. Your fugue state left breadcrumbs—false names, employment records, surveillance footage. And conscious Josh followed them without knowing what they meant. You built a case against a serial killer. You just didn't know you were looking in a mirror."

The timeline is meticulous. My movements plotted in blue. Disappearances plotted in red. Every time they intersect, someone goes missing.

Six intersections.

Six victims.

And beneath the timeline, a separate board: my life. My apartment leases. My job contracts. My travel receipts. My social media posts. Everything public about me, organized chronologically.

And the gaps. Spaces where I should have been posting, working, existing—but wasn't. Spaces of three to five days where I effectively disappeared.

Always before a move. Always before a new city. Always before reinventing myself somewhere else.

I thought those gaps were transition periods. Packing. Travel. Exhaustion.

But Eleanor's timeline shows what really happened in those gaps.

People died.

"No." My voice sounds distant. Like it's coming from underwater. "I'm not—I would never—"

"You don't remember." Eleanor moves to stand beside me. Looks at her own evidence board. "That's the cruelest part. The Josh standing here right now genuinely didn't know. Genuinely believed he was investigating me. Genuinely thought he was the good guy."

She turns to face me.

"But there's another Josh. One that emerges during fugue states. One that travels. One that takes jobs in isolated locations. One that identifies vulnerable people. And one that kills them."

"Stop." I press my hands over my ears. Childish. Futile. "Stop saying that."

"You've been dissociating for over a decade. The fugue states last days. Sometimes weeks. During those periods, you have no memory of your conscious life. You become someone else entirely. Someone cold. Methodical. Dangerous."

"You're lying."

"Am I?" She pulls out another folder. "These are your journals. The ones I found when I searched your room while you were in town. The ones you write during fugue states."

She opens one. The handwriting is mine—but not. Similar enough to be recognizable. Different enough to be disturbing. More mechanical. Less personality. Like someone copying my hand without understanding the intention behind it.

The entries are clinical. Observational. Describing locations. Describing people. Describing opportunities.

Describing victims.

"You scout," Eleanor says quietly. "During the fugue states, you identify isolated jobs. Positions where you'll be alone with someone vulnerable. Caretaker. Maintenance worker. Remote positions. Then you get hired using false references—which you create during the fugue state—and you disappear. Conscious Josh wakes up somewhere new with a convenient story about needing a fresh start."

"And the killings—"

"Happen during the fugue states. You don't remember them. Never have. Probably never will. The amnesia is complete."

I'm sitting now. Don't remember sitting. The floor is hard beneath me. Evidence papers scattered around me like accusatory leaves.

"The keystroke logger," I said suddenly. "On my laptop. I found monitoring software I didn't remember installing."

Eleanor tilted her head. "I didn't install it."

"Then who—" But I already knew. The answer was the same answer to everything. "I did. Didn't I? Some part of me was trying to watch myself."

"It would fit the pattern," Eleanor said quietly. "One part of you documenting. Another part trying to catch the documentation. You've been at war with yourself for years, Josh. You just didn't know it."

I stared at my hands. The hands that had typed those notes. The hands that had installed software to spy on myself. The hands that had—

I couldn't finish the thought.

"How long have you known?"

"That you're the killer? Since April 2023. That you'd be coming back to Thornewood? Since August 2024."

"Coming back—"

"You killed someone here. February 2020. During your fugue state employment. Then conscious Josh woke up, panicked by the missing time, and fled. Made up a story about a family emergency. Left. Forgot everything."

She crouches beside me. Her face is inches from mine.

"But I never forgot. Because the person you killed—" She stops. Stands abruptly. "I need you to understand what you are before I tell you why this matters to me personally."

"Who was she?" I ask. "The woman I killed here. Who was she?"

Eleanor's jaw tightens. "Someone who mattered. Someone who deserved better than dying alone and confused in a library at twenty-two years old. Someone who believed people could be good."

The way she says it. The way her voice cracks on "twenty-two years old."

This isn't professional. This is personal.

"Eleanor—"

"Tomorrow." She cuts me off. Her eyes are too bright. Too close to breaking. "Tomorrow I'll tell you everything. Who she was. Why I'm here. What you took from me. But tonight—" Her voice finally breaks. "Tonight I can't. I can't say her name to your face. Not yet."

I want to push. Want to demand answers. But looking at Eleanor—at the way she's holding herself together with visible effort, at the tears she's fighting back—I can't.

"Okay," I whisper. "Tomorrow."

"Tomorrow you'll understand why I did all of this. Why I came here. Why I let you touch me. Why I've spent two years of my life hunting you." She turns away. "And then you'll understand exactly how much you've taken from the world.

"Oh god." I'm going to be sick. "Eleanor, I didn't—I don't remember—"

"I know." Her voice is flat. "That's what makes this so much worse. You don't remember her. Don't remember her face, her voice, her final moments. She died confused and terrified by someone she trusted, and you can't even give her the dignity of being remembered."

"Show me." The words come out desperate. "The proof. I need to see proof it was me."

Eleanor's expression shifts. Something complex passes across her face. Pain. Reluctance. Determination.

"Are you sure? Once you see it, you can never unknow it. Never unsee what you're capable of."

"I need to know." I'm standing now. Facing her. "If I did this—if I attacked six people, killed five of them—I need to see it. Need to understand. Need to face it."

Eleanor studies me for a long moment.

"Not tonight," she says finally. "Tonight you've seen enough. Tonight you need to process that you've been to Thornewood before. That you have fugue states. That your whole life is built on gaps you can't remember. That I'm not the monster you came here to investigate."

"But tomorrow—"

"Tomorrow, if you still want to see, I'll show you everything. The footage. The evidence. All of it." She pauses. "But fair warning: it will destroy you. The Josh standing here will not survive watching what fugue-Josh is capable of."

I nod. Because she's right. Because I already feel like I'm fracturing. Because everything I thought I knew about myself is wrong.

"What happens after?" I ask. "After you show me. After I see. What happens then?"

Eleanor's eyes are dry. Too dry. Like she's cried all the tears she has and this is what's left.

"Then I call the police. Then you confess. Then you spend the rest of your life in prison where you can't hurt anyone else."

"And you get justice for whoever she was. Whoever I killed here."

"Yes."

"Will it be enough?" I don't know why I'm asking. Don't know what I want the answer to be. "After everything you've done. Everything you've sacrificed to catch me. Will justice be enough?"

Eleanor looks at me for a long time. Then she looks away.

"I don't know," she whispers. "I spent two years believing it would be. But now that we're here... I don't know if anything will ever be enough."

Silence falls. Heavy. Complete.

I look at the evidence board one more time. My face. Six victims. The undeniable pattern.

"I'm a serial killer," I say. Testing the words. Seeing if they fit.

They fit.

They fit perfectly.

"Yes."

"And I didn't know."

"No."

"And tomorrow you're going to show me proof."

"If you can handle it."

Can I? Can I watch myself kill someone? Can I see the monster that lives inside me and emerges when I'm not looking?

I have to.

Because these people deserve to be remembered. Because whoever Eleanor lost deserves justice. Because Eleanor deserves the truth she's sacrificed everything to uncover.

And because I need to know what I really am.

"Tomorrow," I say. "Show me tomorrow. Show me everything."

Eleanor nods. "Tomorrow. 9 AM. Come to the cottage. Be prepared. Be really prepared, Josh. What I'm going to show you... there's no coming back from it."

"I understand."

I move toward the ladder. Need to leave. Need to process. Need to figure out how to survive the next twelve hours knowing what I know now.

At the top of the ladder, I pause. Look back.

Eleanor is standing by the evidence board. Staring at one photo in particular—the woman from Thornewood, five years ago. The one she won't name yet. Her hand hovers near the photo but doesn't touch it. Her shoulders are rigid. Her breathing is careful.

She's holding herself together through sheer force of will.

"Eleanor," I say.

She doesn't turn.

"The months we've been together. Was any of it real?"

Long silence.

Then: "Get out, Josh. Come back tomorrow at 9. We'll talk about what was real after you've seen what you are."

I climb down. Cross the cottage. Step out into the freezing February night.

Behind me, I hear a sound that might be a sob. Might be Eleanor finally breaking. Might be the sound of two years of investigation catching up with her all at once.

But I don't go back. Can't go back.

I walk to the mansion. To the library where, apparently, I killed someone. Someone who mattered to Eleanor. Someone whose identity I still don't know. Four years ago in a fugue state I can't remember.

I sit in the chair by the window. Watch the cottage. See Eleanor's silhouette move through the rooms. See the loft light stay on all night.

I don't sleep.

How could I sleep knowing what I know now?

Knowing that I'm not the investigator. I'm the investigated.

Not the hero. The monster.

Not the truth-seeker. The truth.

And tomorrow, Eleanor is going to show me exactly what that truth looks like.

Tomorrow, I'm going to watch myself kill.

Tomorrow, everything I think I am will end.

I sit in the darkness. Count the hours. Wait for dawn.

Wait for the footage that will destroy me.

Wait for the moment when I can no longer deny what I am.

A serial killer who doesn't remember his crimes.

A monster who thinks he's human.

Something broken wrapped in a man-shaped shell.

Tomorrow, I'll see the truth.

Tonight, I have twelve more hours of being Josh.

Twelve more hours of almost-innocent.

Twelve more hours before I have to face what I've done to Eleanor. To the person I killed here. To six victims whose names I don't even remember.

Twelve hours.

Then the footage.

Then the truth.

Then everything ends.

I close my eyes. Open them. The cottage light is still on. Eleanor is still awake. Still processing. Still preparing for tomorrow.

Tomorrow.

When she'll show me what I am.

When I'll have to watch myself kill.

When the last pieces of my identity shatter beyond repair.

I'm ready.

I'm not ready.

But tomorrow comes anyway.

---

# CHAPTER 26: THE REVELATION
## March 1, 2:00 AM — Eleanor

---

The cottage is cold tonight. I let the fire die hours ago. Don't have the energy to rebuild it. Don't want the warmth. The cold feels right. Appropriate.

It's 2 AM. In seven hours, I'll show Josh the evidence. In seven hours, I'll watch him see what he is. In seven hours, everything I've worked toward for five years will finally happen.

So why do I feel like I'm about to make the biggest mistake of my life?

I sit at my desk in the loft, surrounded by everything I've collected. Two years of planning. Two years of active surveillance. Five months of living a lie so complete I sometimes forgot it was a lie.

The timeline is spread out in front of me.

I trace the line from her last journal entry to the estimated time of death. I pull the journal close, read the final entry one more time:

*"February 17, 2020. Marcus has been here over a week now. We've become a good team—he handles the heavy lifting, I handle the planning. He's quiet lately though. Something changed. The way he looks at me sometimes... through me, almost. Like he's seeing someone else. Asked me again today if anyone knows I'm here, if Eleanor ever visits. Weird questions. But he's been nothing but helpful. Probably just cabin fever—we've both been isolated too long. Tomorrow I'm showing him the eastern trails before he leaves. Contract ends soon. I'll miss having someone to talk to."*

She'd trusted him. Thought he was nice. Had no idea that "Marcus"—Josh in his fugue state—would strangle her the next day.

Four days. She was missing for four days before her body was found. Four days I didn't know she was gone because we'd fought. Because I'd told her coming to Thornewood was a stupid idea. Because I'd said "Fine, go get yourself killed in the middle of nowhere, see if I care."

Those were the last words I said to my sister. *See if I care.*

And then she was gone.

I press my palms against my eyes. No tears left. Haven't cried in months. The grief has gone cold, calcified into cold purpose.

Tomorrow I'll show Josh the footage. The security camera that caught everything. The four minutes that destroyed my world.

I've watched it hundreds of times. Memorized every second. The way she fought. The way she scratched his arms, trying to defend herself. The way he stood there afterward, so calm, checking her pulse to make sure the job was complete.

The way he looked at the camera when he realized he'd been recorded.

That look. Not panic. Not fear. Just... calculation. Problem-solving. *How do I fix this?*

He broke the camera. Ripped it off the wall. But the footage had already backed up to the central server. He left two days later. Disappeared. Became someone else.

Became Josh again. The Josh who didn't remember. Who had no idea what he'd done.

I pull out the letter I wrote earlier tonight. The one I'll leave for him if everything goes wrong tomorrow. If the fugue state emerges. If he runs. If I don't survive showing him the truth.

*Josh—*

*If you're reading this, I'm probably dead. Or you are. Or we both are. Doesn't matter which at this point.*

*You killed my sister. February 18, 2020. Thornewood Estate. Manual strangulation. Four minutes from start to finish. I have the security footage. I've watched you do it two hundred and thirty-seven times.*

*Her name was Lily. She was 22. She came here to work as winter caretaker because she wanted adventure, wanted isolation, wanted to prove she could handle herself in the wilderness. She thought you were nice. Wrote in her journal about "the sweet writer staying in the mansion." Thought you were harmless.*

*She was wrong.*

*I've spent five years trying to understand how someone can attack six people—kill five of them—and not remember a single victim. Five years grieving. Two years hunting. Four months trying to reconcile the man who killed my sister with the man I fell in love with.*

*Yes, I fell in love with you. I didn't mean to. Didn't want to. Tried not to. But somewhere between October and now, I stopped being able to see you as my sister's killer. Started seeing you as Josh. As the broken man trying to piece together his own fractured mind. As someone worth saving.*

*I hate myself for that. Hate that I betrayed Lily by caring about you. Hate that I used intimacy as an investigative tool and ended up caught in my own trap. Hate that I'm dying and running out of time and all I want is more time with the man who murdered my sister.*

*If you're reading this, the confrontation went wrong. Maybe the other you emerged. Maybe I pushed too hard. Maybe you couldn't handle the truth and it broke you completely.*

*Or maybe I'm the one who broke. Maybe I couldn't go through with it. Couldn't show you what you are. Couldn't destroy you the way you destroyed Lily.*

*I don't know which would be worse.*

*All I know is that I tried. I did everything I could to get justice for Lily. And if I failed, I'm sorry. If I succeeded and you're in custody and I'm dead, I'm sorry for that too. Sorry I couldn't tell you the truth face to face. Sorry I couldn't help you. Sorry I fell in love with you when I should have pulled the trigger.*

*There's a gun in the locked drawer of my desk—a .38 revolver, not the 9mm you found in my trunk. I bought the revolver two years ago. Told myself it was for protection. But I think we both know what it was really for.*

*The one in my car was for you. The one in my desk was for me. I never used either. Couldn't decide if that makes me a coward or worse.*

*Goodbye, Josh. I wish we'd met under different circumstances. I wish you were the person you think you are. I wish Lily was still alive. I wish a lot of things.*

*But wishing doesn't change what happened. Doesn't bring anyone back. Doesn't make either of us whole.*

*—Eleanor*

I fold the letter. Seal it in an envelope. Write his name on the front. Leave it on the desk where he'll find it if he comes looking.

Then I turn to my laptop. Open the security footage one more time. Watch my sister die again.

This time, I don't cry. Don't flinch. Just watch with the clinical detachment I've learned to cultivate. Counting the seconds. Noting the details. Preparing to narrate this horror to the man who caused it.

Four minutes. That's all it took to end Lily's life. Four minutes that have defined the last five years of mine.

Tomorrow, Josh will watch these four minutes. Will see what he's capable of. Will have to reconcile the monster on screen with the man he believes himself to be.

I don't know what that will do to him. Don't know if his mind can survive it. Don't know if mine can either.

But I'm out of time. The headaches are worse. Vision blurring more often. I have maybe six weeks before I can't function anymore. Before I lose what's left of my mind.

Three weeks to get justice for Lily. Three weeks to make Josh understand what he is. Three weeks to decide if I'm going to report him or mercy-kill him or walk away and let the disease take me before I have to make that choice.

The supplies in my trunk—the rope, the plastic sheeting, the shovel, the handgun—I'd packed them for all three possibilities. Evidence collection. Self-defense. Or worse, if justice through the system failed. I still don't know which version of me would have won if it came to that.

I close the laptop. Look at Lily's photo on my wall. The one I stare at every morning when I wake up and every night before I sleep.

"Tomorrow," I tell her. "Tomorrow he'll know. Tomorrow I'll make him see what he did to you. And then... I don't know. But you'll have your answer. You'll know I tried."

She smiles back at me. Frozen at 22. Forever young. Forever trusting. Forever dead.

I climb down from the loft. Lie in my cold bed. Stare at the ceiling.

In seven hours, I'll show Josh the footage.

In seven hours, I'll destroy him.

In seven hours, I'll get justice or revenge or whatever this hollow feeling is supposed to be.

I don't sleep. Just lie there counting the minutes. Watching the sky lighten through the window. Waiting for 9 AM.

Waiting for the moment everything ends.

When dawn comes, I'm ready.

As ready as I'll ever be.

I dress carefully. Practical clothes. Layer a cardigan over my shirt—easy to remove if I need to show him the medical port in my chest. Proof that I'm dying. Proof that I'm telling the truth about everything.

I make coffee but don't drink it. My stomach can't handle anything right now.

I walk to the cottage door. Look back at the loft one more time. At Lily's photo. At the letter on my desk. At five years of my life spread across cork boards and file boxes.

"I love you," I tell her. "I'm sorry it took so long. I'm sorry I fell in love with him. I'm sorry for everything. But today, you get justice. Today, he learns the truth."

Then I open the door and step outside.

The mansion looms ahead. Josh will be waking soon. Getting ready. Preparing himself for whatever he thinks is coming.

He has no idea.

I cross the grounds. The morning air is sharp and cold. My breath fogs. My hands shake.

I unlock the cottage evidence room one last time. Make sure everything is ready. Laptop charged. Timeline organized. Documents in order. Lily's journal open to the last entry.

Then I sit. And I wait.

For Josh to arrive.

For the confrontation I've been building toward for two years.

For the moment when I finally tell him what he is.

I'm ready.

I'm not ready.

But 9 AM comes anyway.

---

He arrives at exactly 9 AM.

I watch from the cottage window as Josh walks from the mansion, crossing the hundred yards of snow-patched ground in the early morning light. He's wearing the same clothes as yesterday—jeans, flannel shirt, winter coat—but he looks like he's aged ten years overnight. Pale. Exhausted. Moving like each step requires conscious decision.

He didn't run.

Part of me expected it. Expected to wake up and find him gone, disappeared into the mountains, fugue state taking over to protect him from the truth I'm about to show. But he's here. He kept his promise.

I don't know if that makes this easier or harder.

The knock on the cottage door is soft. Tentative. Not the knock of someone who thinks they deserve to be let in.

I open it.

"Eleanor." His voice is rough. Like he's been crying. Like he hasn't slept. Both are probably true.

"Come in."

He steps inside. Stops just past the threshold, hands in his pockets, not looking at me directly. There's a careful distance between us now. The intimacy of four months burned away by yesterday's revelations, leaving only the investigator and the suspect.

I close the door. Lock it. The sound of the deadbolt makes Josh flinch.

"I'm not going to run," he says quietly. "You don't need to—"

"It's not to keep you in. It's to make sure we're not interrupted." I move past him to the loft ladder. "Come up."

---

The evidence room looks different in daylight. Clinical. Official. The walls covered with timeline documentation, photographs, forensic notes. Two years of investigation laid bare in a twelve-by-twelve room.

Josh stands in the center, slowly turning, taking it all in. The photos of him—surveillance shots from the last two years, showing his movements across different cities. The timeline showing his relocations matched against missing persons reports. The victim photographs, six people, ages ranging from early twenties to mid-fifties.

All dead.

"This is..." He stops. Starts again. "How long did this take?"

"Two years of active investigation. Longer to plan."

He reaches toward one of the photographs. Pulls his hand back without touching it. Like he's afraid the evidence might burn him.

"I did all of this."

It's not a question. But I answer anyway.

"Yes."

A memory surfaces unbidden: Roy's dog Scout, that day in the yard, looking between the two of us like she couldn't decide which one was the threat. Maybe she'd known. Animals sense things. Maybe she'd smelled the killer on him—and the hunter on me.

He turns to look at me. Really look at me. And I see the moment he understands the full scope of what I've done. What I've sacrificed. What I've endured to stand here with him in this room full of proof.

"You've been investigating me the entire time we've been..." He can't finish.

"Yes."

"Everything was—"

"Part of the investigation. Yes." The lie comes easily now. Has to be a lie, because the truth—that some of it was real, that I forgot sometimes, that I let myself feel things I shouldn't—that truth serves no purpose except to destroy me more completely than I'm already destroyed.

Josh looks away. Jaw tight. Hands clenched at his sides.

"What happens now?"

I cross to the desk in the corner. The laptop is already set up, screen dark, waiting. Two chairs positioned facing it. I've been preparing for this moment since October. Since before October. Since the day I realized I could find him. Could trap him. Could make him watch.

"Now I show you who you are."

---

Josh sits in the chair I indicate. Doesn't argue. Doesn't resist. Just sits, hands folded in his lap, spine rigid, eyes on the blank screen.

I sit beside him. Close enough that our shoulders almost touch. Close enough to see every micro-expression, every reaction, every moment of realization.

My hand hovers over the trackpad.

"Before I show you this," I say carefully, "I need you to understand. This is not edited. This is not fabricated. This is footage from Thornewood Estate's security system. The cameras you were so interested in. The blind spots you were worried about."

"There aren't any blind spots," Josh whispers.

"No. There aren't."

I click play.

---

The video opens on the library. Black and white, high angle, timestamp in the corner: **February 18, 2020, 8:43 PM**.

The library looks the same as it does now. Same bookshelves, same leather furniture, same fireplace. But the fire is out. Cold. The room is empty.

Josh is staring at the screen, barely breathing.

Then the door opens.

A young woman enters frame. Blonde, early twenties, wearing jeans and a green fleece jacket. She's carrying a book, reading as she walks, smiling at something on the page. She crosses to the couch, sits down, curls her feet under her.

Completely at ease. Completely safe.

"Who is she?" Josh asks. His voice is barely audible.

I don't answer. Just watch the screen.

**8:43 PM.** The woman reads. Turns a page. Glances up at something off-camera—maybe a noise from elsewhere in the mansion. Returns to her book.

**8:44 PM.** The library door opens again.

A man enters.

Josh makes a sound. Not quite a word. More like a wounded animal.

Because the man on the screen is him.

Same face. Same build. Same copper hair, though slightly longer than he wears it now. Same eyes. But everything is different. The way he moves. Fluid. Purposeful. Predatory.

The man—Josh but not Josh—approaches the woman. She looks up. Smiles. Says something—no audio, just the image of her lips moving. A greeting. A question about his day.

The man smiles back.

Then his hands are on her throat.

---

It happens fast. Faster than I expected the first time I watched this. The woman doesn't have time to scream. Barely has time to understand what's happening before she's fighting, hands clawing at his wrists, legs kicking, book falling to the floor.

**8:44 PM.** She's still fighting. Scratches his hands, his forearms. Gets one leg up, kicks his shin hard enough that he staggers. But he doesn't let go. Doesn't loosen his grip.

**8:45 PM.** Her movements are getting weaker. More erratic. Desperate.

**8:47 PM.** She stops moving.

**8:47 PM.** The man—Josh—finally releases her. Steps back. Looks down at what he's done.

The woman is sprawled on the floor between the couch and the coffee table. Still. One arm outstretched toward the door she'll never reach. Eyes open, staring at nothing.

Dead.

It took minutes. Too many minutes.

---

Beside me, Josh is making sounds. Not words. Just sounds—gasps, chokes, animal noises of horror and denial. His hands are gripping the arms of the chair so hard his knuckles are white.

But he doesn't look away. I thought he might. Thought he'd close his eyes, turn his head, refuse to see. But he's watching. Forcing himself to watch every second.

On screen, the man who is Josh stands over the body for a long moment. Then he crouches down. Checks for pulse at the neck—the neck he'd strangled. Methodical. Clinical. Making sure the job is complete.

Then he stands. Looks directly at the camera.

And I see it. The moment he realizes he's being recorded. The moment fugue-state-Josh understands that this murder has been documented.

His face doesn't change. No panic. No fear. Just calculation. He walks toward the camera—toward us—and reaches up.

The screen goes black.

"He broke the camera," I say quietly. "Ripped it off the wall. But the footage had already been backed up to the security system's central server in the basement. He didn't know that. Or maybe he did and thought he could erase it later. But he never got the chance. He left Thornewood three days later."

I close the laptop.

The room is silent except for Josh's breathing. Fast. Shallow. On the edge of hyperventilating.

"That's not me," he finally says. "That can't be—"

"It's you. Look." I reopen the laptop, rewind the footage to a clearer frame. Pause it. "The face is grainy—I know what police said, I know forensics couldn't enhance it. But I've studied your face for two years, Josh. The way you hold your shoulders. The angle of your jaw. How you tilt your head when you're focused. Things cameras can't sharpen but obsession can learn." I point at the frozen image. "That's you. February 18, 2020. Thornewood Estate. That's you."

He's shaking. Whole body trembling like he's going into shock.

"I don't remember. Eleanor, I swear to god, I don't remember any of this. I don't remember being here. I don't remember her. I don't—"

"I know."

"Then how can you—how can you show me this and—" His voice breaks. "That's murder. That's me murdering someone. How is that possible if I don't remember?"

"Dissociative state," I say. Using the clinical language. The investigator's voice. It's the only way I can get through this. "You have a dissociative disorder, Josh. When you're under extreme stress, you dissociate. Your consciousness... splits. One part of you goes away, and another part comes forward. A part that doesn't have your memories, your moral code, your sense of self. And when you come back, you don't remember what happened while you were gone."

"That's not—that's not real. That's not a real thing."

"It is. It's rare, but it's documented. Traumatic dissociative states triggered by stress. You experienced severe childhood trauma—witnessing your mother's murder when you were three years old. Your brain developed dissociative coping mechanisms. And sometimes those mechanisms... fracture. Split off into something separate. Something that can do things you would never do. Things you can't remember doing."

Josh is staring at me. Eyes wide. Pupils dilated. In full shock.

"How many?" he whispers.

I could lie. Could make it easier. Could tell him it was this once, this one woman, this one inexplicable moment.

But he deserves the truth. They all deserve the truth.

"Six confirmed victims. Possibly more. The pattern goes back to late 2019."

"Why—" He chokes on the word, tries again. "Why didn't the police find me? If you have all this evidence—"

"I went to the police. Twice." My voice is flat. Controlled. "First in 2022, then again in 2023. The first detective looked at my 'evidence'—a podcaster's travel schedule, handwriting samples I'd compared myself, a theory about dissociative states—and told me I sounded like a grief-stricken woman watching too much true crime." I laugh bitterly. "The second one was kinder. Took notes. Said he'd look into it. Never called back.

"So I built the case myself. Your podcast gave me more than you know." I pull up three audio clips on the laptop, each bookmarked and labeled. "Episode forty-seven. You describe the hiking trail outside Bend where Sarah Martinez's body was found. You mention the switchback near the creek crossing—the one the police never released to the public. In Lily's journal, she wrote about that same trail three days before she disappeared. You couldn't have known that detail unless you were there." I play the second clip. "Episode sixty-two. You say the caretaker cottage at Thornewood had a 'green door with a brass knocker.' The door was repainted brown in 2018. The brass knocker was removed after the murder. You described the door as it looked in February 2020." Third clip. "Episode seventy-one. You use the phrase 'the way the light falls through those library windows in winter.' Present tense. Like you're remembering it. Like you've seen it."

Josh is shaking his head. "Those are coincidences. Research details. I read articles, I study crime scenes from public records—"

"You also attacked me." The words stop him cold. "February 4th. Three AM. You broke into my cottage during a fugue state. You put your hands on my throat, Josh. I fought you off—barely. You left bruises on my arms that took two weeks to fade. And the next morning, you had no memory of it. None. You asked me about the bruise on my arm like a concerned boyfriend. That's when I knew for certain. Not from podcast slips or travel records or handwriting analysis. From your hands on my neck in the dark."

I gesture at the frozen image on the laptop. "They had security footage of Marcus Webb. But Marcus Webb is a ghost—no birth certificate, no criminal record, no military service, nothing in any database. Facial recognition came back empty because the original camera was 720p at best, corrupted by a power surge, timestamp scrambled. The county forensics lab said the file was too degraded for reliable enhancement. And Josh Caine, charming true crime podcaster, didn't exactly match the grainy figure on a four-year-old security tape. You grew a beard. Cut your hair differently. Put on muscle. The composite sketch witnesses gave made you look ten years older and fifty pounds heavier." I shake my head. "Without clear footage or a database match, it was just a grieving sister with a conspiracy theory. People see what they expect to see. No one expected a writer to be a killer."

---

He breaks.

Completely. Utterly. Breaks apart like a dam finally giving way and finally can't hold anymore.

He's on the floor. Didn't realize he'd moved. Curled up on the floor of my evidence room surrounded by photographs of his victims, sobbing so hard he can't breathe. Hands over his face like he's trying to disappear. Like if he presses hard enough he can erase himself.

I watch him. Should feel victorious. Should feel justice being served. Should feel anything other than this hollow ache.

But all I feel is tired.

I crouch beside him. Don't touch him. Just close enough to speak.

"Josh. Listen to me. You need to breathe. You're hyperventilating. You need to—"

"I killed her." The words come out mangled, broken by sobs. "I killed her. I watched myself kill her. How is that possible? How do I not remember? How do I—"

"Josh. Breathe."

"I can't. I can't breathe. I can't—" He's gasping now. Panic attack layered on top of shock. "I'm a murderer. I'm a serial killer. Five people are dead because of me. Five people. And I don't remember any of them. How do I not remember?"

"Because you weren't there when it happened. Not the you that's here right now. That's what dissociation means. The conscious Josh—the one who writes the podcast, who reads poetry, who thinks he's investigating me—you weren't present for the murders. Another part of you was. A part you don't have access to."

"Then how do I know it won't happen again?" He looks up at me. Face destroyed. Tears streaming. "How do I know I won't hurt you? That I won't—Eleanor, what if I—"

"You won't."

"How do you know? How can you possibly—"

"Because I've been living with you for four months. Because I've watched you every single day and every single night. Because I know the triggers. I know the warning signs. And I know you're not going to hurt me."

It's not quite true. I don't know that. Can't know that with certainty. But he needs to hear it. Needs to believe he's still capable of control.

"I don't want to be this," he whispers. "I don't want to be a murderer."

"I know."

"There's no coming back from this. No fixing this. No—" He presses his hands against his eyes. "Everyone I've ever known. Everyone I've cared about. They'll know. They'll see this footage and know what I am."

"Yes."

"My mother was murdered and I thought that was the worst thing. Thought I'd survived the worst thing. Thought I was broken but healing. But I'm not healing. I'm a killer. I'm the thing I've been afraid of my entire life. The thing I've been writing about, investigating, trying to understand—it was me. It was always me."

I don't contradict him. Don't offer false comfort. Because he's right. He's exactly right.

"I can't live with this," he says. So quiet I almost don't hear it. "Eleanor, I can't carry this. I can't wake up every day knowing what I am. Knowing I killed five people. Knowing I might kill more. I can't—"

"Yes, you can."

"No." He's shaking his head. "No. I should—the mountains. I could walk into the mountains and keep walking until—"

"No." I say it sharply. Firm. "You don't get that option. You don't get to escape this by dying. You're going to live with it. You're going to confess to the police. You're going to stand trial. You're going to spend the rest of your life in prison knowing what you did. That's justice. That's what you owe those six victims."

"I don't remember them."

"Then you're going to learn about them. Every one of them. Their names, their lives, their families, what you took from the world when you took them. You're going to carry that. For the rest of your life."

He looks at me. Something like hope—desperate, terrible hope—in his eyes.

"Will you..." He swallows. "Will you tell me about her? The woman in the video? Who was she?"

This is the moment. The moment I should tell him. Should reveal everything. Should say *her name was Lily and you killed her and you need to know who she was to me*.

But I don't.

Because there's one more night. One more conversation. One more revelation.

"Not yet," I say. "There's more you need to know. More I need to show you. But not tonight. You can't handle any more tonight."

"Eleanor—"

"Tomorrow. The library. 8 AM. I'll tell you everything. I'll tell you who she was. Who they all were. And then we're calling the police."

Josh nods slowly. Doesn't argue. Just accepts it.

"Can I..." His voice breaks. "Can I watch it again?"

"What?"

"The footage. I need to see it again. I need to—I need to understand. I need to see myself doing it. See the moment. See if there's any warning. Any sign that I'm about to—"

"No." I close the laptop. "You've seen enough."

"But—"

"Josh. You can't torture yourself into understanding this. You can't watch yourself kill someone over and over until it makes sense. It's never going to make sense. It's going to destroy you. And I need you functional enough to confess tomorrow. So no. You can't watch it again."

He slumps. Defeated. Broken.

"I loved you," he whispers. "These four months. I loved you. And I can't even—that's contaminated now. Everything is contaminated. How can I have loved you while being a murderer? How can both things be true?"

I don't answer. Because I don't have an answer. Because I've been asking myself the same thing in reverse: how can I have felt anything for him while knowing what he was?

"Go back to the mansion," I say. "Try to sleep. Tomorrow we finish this."

He stands slowly. Unsteady. Like gravity is different now. Like the world is a different shape than it was an hour ago.

At the loft door, he stops.

"Thank you," he says.

"For what?"

"For not killing me. You could have. Should have, probably. But you didn't. You did all this—" He gestures at the evidence room. "—to make sure I'd face justice. The right way. The legal way. Even though it would have been easier to end it. So thank you. For caring about justice more than revenge."

"Josh. Wait."

He stops at the loft door.

I reach into the desk drawer. Pull out the prescription bottle. Orange plastic, white label. I hold it out to him.

He takes it. Reads the label. His lips move silently: *Temozolomide. Eleanor Ashford. 250mg.*

"Ashford," he says. Something changing in his face. "Not Moore."

"Not Moore."

He stares at the bottle. Turns it over. Reads the prescribing information on the back. I can see the moment the word registers—*glioblastoma*—because his hands go still.

"Eleanor. What is this?"

I pull down the collar of my sweater. Show him the medical port implanted beneath my collarbone. The small raised disc under the skin, the faint bruising around the access site where the last infusion went in.

"Brain cancer," I say. "Glioblastoma multiforme. Stage four. Diagnosed eighteen months ago. The tremors, the headaches, the balance problems—you noticed all of it. You just didn't know what you were seeing."

He stares at the port. At the bottle. At me.

"How long?"

"Months. Maybe less. The temozolomide is buying time, not curing anything. Nothing cures this."

The silence stretches between us. Two people destroying each other with truth.

"That's why you rushed this," he says slowly. "That's why you came here. Why you did everything alone instead of waiting for the police to listen."

"I wasn't going to die without finishing this. Without making sure someone answered for what happened to—" I stop myself. Tomorrow. That's for tomorrow. "Without making sure there was justice."

He sets the bottle on the desk. Gently. Like it's something fragile.

"I'm sorry," he says. And the terrible thing is, he means it. A serial killer apologizing to the woman who trapped him, because she's dying.

"Don't. Don't be sorry for me. Be sorry for the people you killed."

He nods. Doesn't argue. Just accepts it.

He climbs down the ladder. Crosses the cottage main room. Goes out the door.

I watch him from the loft window. Watch him walk back to the mansion in the morning light. Still moving like every step is conscious effort. Still not running. Still keeping his promise to stay. To face this.

I should feel satisfied. Should feel like the trap worked. Like justice is being served. Like I'm winning.

Instead I feel hollow.

Because tomorrow I have to tell him about Lily.

Tomorrow I have to watch him break all over again when he realizes the woman in the video—the woman he watched himself kill—was my sister.

Tomorrow I have to reveal that every time we touched, every time we made love, every intimate moment between us was me betraying my sister with the man who strangled her.

Tomorrow I tell him about Lily.

But tonight, I have to live with everything else.

I close the evidence room. Lock it. Go downstairs to the cottage main room.

The space feels different now. Like the air shifted. Like the investigation has moved past the point of no return. Like we're in freefall now, and all that's left is to hit the ground.

I make coffee I don't drink. Sit at the table. Stare at the locked door.

And wait for tomorrow.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 27: THE PHOTOGRAPH
## March 2 — Josh

---

I arrive at the library at 5 AM. Three hours early. Eleanor said 8 AM, but waiting in the mansion was impossible.

Didn't sleep. Couldn't. Spent the entire night in my bedroom staring at the ceiling, replaying the footage over and over in my mind. Watching myself walk into frame. Watching myself approach her. Watching myself kill.

A woman I don't remember.

A woman named Lily Ashford. A name I read on Eleanor's wall but can't connect to the motionless figure on that screen.

The library feels different this morning. Colder. Like the temperature dropped ten degrees overnight. Or maybe it's me. Maybe I've been cold since yesterday. Since I watched myself become a murderer.

Eleanor is already there when I arrive. Sitting in one of the leather chairs by the fireplace. No fire burning. Just cold ashes from whenever it was last used. She looks like she hasn't slept either. Dark circles under her eyes. Hair pulled back in a severe ponytail. Wearing the same clothes as yesterday—jeans, thermal shirt, fleece jacket. Like she's been up all night too. Like neither of us could rest knowing what's coming this morning.

There are two mugs of coffee on the table between the chairs. Both steaming. Both untouched. Eleanor gestures to the chair across from her.

I sit.

We don't speak for a long moment. The silence is heavy. Final. Like the last silence before execution.

Finally, Eleanor breaks it.

"There's one more thing you need to know."

Her voice is steady. Clinical. The investigator's voice. But I can hear the tremor underneath. The crack in the foundation.

I almost laugh. It comes out as a sob.

"More?" My voice sounds like someone else's. Someone who's been screaming. "How can there possibly be more than watching myself kill someone?"

"This is the worst part." Now she does look at me, and I wish she hadn't. Her eyes are completely dry. Burned clean of tears. "The reason I did all of this. The reason I trapped you here. The reason I've endured four months of..."

She doesn't finish. Can't finish.

I try to count my breaths. Try to find something to anchor to. My therapist—god, my therapist who I lied to for years, who I thought was helping me cope with witnessing my mother's murder when really she was treating the wrong patient entirely—she always said to breathe through the panic. To find five things I can see, four I can touch, three I can hear.

Five things I can see: Eleanor's hands, perfectly still. The cold fireplace. The morning light through tall windows. My own hands, killer's hands. The steaming mugs neither of us can drink.

Four things I can touch: The leather chair beneath me. The cold air of the library. The clothing on my body, every fiber contaminated now. The knowledge sitting in my chest like a stone.

Three things I can hear: My breathing. Eleanor's breathing. The silence between them, vast and unbridgeable.

Two things I can—

Eleanor stands abruptly. Crosses to one of the bookshelves. Reaches behind a row of books—a hiding place, planned in advance—and pulls out something small.

She returns to stand in front of me. Doesn't sit back down. Just stands there, holding something I can't quite see yet.

A photograph. Five by seven, in a simple wooden frame.

She holds it out. Doesn't say anything.

I take it. My hands are shaking.

The photograph shows a young woman, early twenties, outdoors somewhere. Mountains in the background—these mountains, maybe, or ones like them. She's blonde, shoulder-length hair catching sunlight. Blue eyes. Her smile reaches all the way to those eyes, unguarded and genuine. She's wearing a green fleece jacket and has a backpack strap visible over one shoulder. The photo has that candid quality—someone caught mid-laugh, unaware they're being captured, completely themselves.

She looks happy. She looks alive. She looks like someone I would have liked, in another life. In a life where I was human.

"Who is this?"

Eleanor's jaw tightens. I watch her gather herself, watch her pull on that clinical investigator's voice like armor.

"Her name was Lily. Lily Grace Ashford." A pause. "She was twenty-two years old."

The way she says *was*. Past tense. Past tense for someone young enough they should still be using present tense for decades.

I look at the photo again. At Lily Grace Ashford, twenty-two years old, forever frozen in this moment of unguarded joy.

The silence stretches. Eleanor is waiting for me to understand.

And then I do.

"Your sister," I whisper.

"Yes."

The photograph weighs nothing. It weighs everything. I can't look away from it. Can't look away from those blue eyes, that smile, that aliveness that is somehow also pastness.

"Your sister was one of the—" I can't finish. Can't say *victims*. Can't reduce this laughing young woman to that word.

Eleanor sits down. Not in the chair across from me, but on the coffee table between us, close enough that our knees are almost touching. Close enough that I can see the fine control she's exerting over every muscle in her face.

"February 2020," she says. Each word precisely placed, like she's building something that might collapse if she misses a syllable. "She took a winter caretaker position at Thornewood. It was her first time doing something like that. First time alone in the mountains. She was finishing her environmental science degree before applying to law school. Environmental law. She wanted to save the forests. The job posting mentioned maintaining hiking trails and studying the local ecosystem—perfect for her thesis research. She thought she'd found the ideal place to work on her applications while immersed in the wilderness she loved."

She pauses. Breathes. Continues.

"She called me the day she died. Told me about the deer she'd seen that morning. Five of them, she said, right by the cottage at dawn. About her plans for spring—she was going to hike the Pacific Crest Trail. About the book she was reading. About—"

Her voice catches. The first crack in the armor.

"—about the other winter caretaker who'd been helping with the heavy work. About how nice it was to have someone to talk to. She said his name was Marcus Webb. That he'd been there about ten days. That he was kind. Helpful."

No.

No.

"His name was Josh."

The photograph slips from my hands. Falls to the floor between us. Face-up. Lily Grace Ashford still smiling, now from the hardwood, now from beneath my feet, now from where I can't escape her.

"Eleanor, no. No, I—"

"You." Her voice is iron now. "You were there. You worked with her. You talked to her. You made her feel safe."

"I don't remember." It sounds like the excuse it is. "Eleanor, I swear to god, I don't remember her. I don't remember being there. I don't—"

"I know." She stands. Moves to the kitchen counter. Returns with a journal. Pale blue cover, worn at the edges. "I know you don't remember. That's part of what makes it unforgivable."

She opens the journal. Finds a page—she knows exactly which page, must have read it ten thousand times. Reads aloud, and her voice is steady again, steady and deadly:

"'February 8, 2020: New winter caretaker arrived today. Name's Marcus Webb. Super nice. Helped me move firewood for like three hours without complaining, even though he just got here. Says he's a writer working on a book. True crime stuff. Kind of intense subject matter but he talks about it respectfully, like he actually cares about the victims. Estate management hired him for the heavy maintenance work through February. Nice to finally have someone to talk to up here. Winter's been lonelier than I expected.'"

She flips forward several pages. Finds another entry. Her voice doesn't waver:

"'February 18, 2020, morning: Marcus is leaving today. Contract ends tomorrow but he said he's heading out early. We finished the greenhouse winterization yesterday. He was quiet at breakfast. Asked me again about Eleanor—whether she visits, whether anyone else knows I'm here. Something felt off but he's been nothing but helpful these past ten days. Maybe I'm reading too much into it. He probably wants to get home before the weather turns.'"

She closes the journal. Looks at me.

"That was the morning of February 18. That evening, she was dead. No more entries."

I know what she's going to say.

"There isn't one."

The cottage is too small. The air is too thin. I can't get enough oxygen. Can't think. Can't process.

"The journal," I hear myself say. "The one you showed me. The handwriting that was..."

"Yours." Eleanor sets Lily's journal down. Picks up another one—and I recognize this one. Green cover. Water-stained. The one she found in the tower room, the one she showed me yesterday, the handwriting that was mine but not mine.

The water damage—I suddenly understand. The art restoration supplies I'd found on her desk. The UV light, the chemicals for treating paper. She wasn't forging documents. She was recovering Lily's journals. Salvaging the only record of her sister's final days from years of water damage.

"February 2020. You were here. You lived here. You worked with my sister for ten days before you killed her."

She produces a second paper. Yellowed, torn carefully along one edge. I recognize the format—another page from the guest book. But not the one I found in the library. This one is different.

She points to the signature.

*Marcus Webb, February 8, 2020*

My handwriting. But a name I've never heard before. The signature style is unmistakably mine—same pressure, same slant, same way the letters connect. But the name is wrong.

"You signed the guest book when you arrived," Eleanor says. "Used the name from your fugue state. Marcus Webb. A complete fiction. But your handwriting gave you away. I had a PI pull samples from your apartment, your manuscript notes—compared them myself. Police had the guest book signature, but they were matching it against 'Marcus Webb' databases that returned nothing. I was matching it against you."

"And this time—Joshua Michaels on the rental application?" She almost laughs. "I knew that was fake before you even arrived. A true crime podcaster using a pseudonym to investigate a cold case at the exact location where his handwriting matched an unsolved murder signature?" She shakes her head. "You practically announced yourself."

She pauses. "The carving in the tower room. M+L 2020. You carved that. Marcus and Lily. Like you were claiming her."

I pick up the photograph from the floor. Force myself to look at it again. At Lily. At this person who wrote about me in her journal. Who thought I was *super nice*. Who was glad to have someone to talk to.

I try to remember her. Try to pull anything from that void where 2020 should be. Try to see her face as something more than this photograph. Try to hear her voice, remember a conversation, remember carrying firewood, remember *anything*.

Nothing.

Nothing except the growing horror of understanding what that absence means.

"She liked me," I whisper.

"Yes."

"She trusted me."

"Yes."

"And three days later, I..." I can't say it. Can't put it into words.

Eleanor does it for me. Eleanor, who has had to carry these words for almost five years.

"You strangled her. In the library. Where we—"

She doesn't finish. Doesn't need to.

The library. Where we had sex against the bookshelves. Where we did it on the leather couch. Where Eleanor was touching her sister's killer on her sister's grave and I had no idea. No idea what I was making her endure.

"Oh god." I'm on my feet, stumbling away from her, toward the door, anywhere, but there's nowhere to go. Nowhere that changes what I am. "Every time we... in that room..."

"I know."

"You were sleeping with your sister's killer."

"I know."

The memories come flooding back. Unbidden. Undeniable.

The kitchen. Her hands in my hair. The way she'd gasped my name.

And now I knew: every touch had been her touching her sister's killer. Every kiss had been her kissing the man who strangled Lily in that library. Every time she'd climaxed in my arms—every time she'd called out my name—she'd known. Known what I was. Known what my hands had done to the person she loved most.

The library. The leather couch. Her beneath me, back arching, saying my name like it mattered.

But to her it meant monster. Murderer. The man who made her sister's last moments a nightmare.

"The way you looked at me after," I whisper. "That expression I thought was tenderness..."

"Was revulsion," Eleanor finishes. Her voice is flat. Matter-of-fact. "Self-loathing. Horror at what I was doing. What I was letting you do to me."
