I remember now. The nights she cried in my arms and I thought it was because she was moved by our connection. The way her whole body would shake afterward. The distant look in her eyes that I'd mistaken for contentment.

She'd been enduring. Sacrificing herself. Destroying herself piece by piece every time she let me touch her.

"You said my name," I hear myself say. "You said you wanted me."

"I did want you. That was the worst part." Her eyes are dry. Too dry. Like she's cried all the tears she has. "I hated myself for wanting you. Hated that my body responded. Hated that some part of me—the part that didn't know better, that couldn't help it—wanted the man who killed my sister."

I think about every intimate moment. Every time I thought we were connecting. Every time I thought maybe I was capable of love after all.

And she was there, sleeping with her sister's murderer. Hating herself. Dying inside. All while I remained oblivious.

I run to the bathroom and vomit until there's nothing left.

I turn back to her. She hasn't moved from the coffee table. Still holding that terrible collected calm.

"Eleanor, I'm—"

"Don't." The word cracks like a whip. "Don't you dare apologize. There is no apology for this. There is no sorry big enough for this. There are no words in any language that make this anything other than what it is."

I collapse back onto the couch. Put my head in my hands. Those hands. Those hands that—

"Why?" It comes out broken. "Why didn't you kill me? You could have. First day I was here, first night I was asleep, you could have come up to the mansion and I would never have known. Why this? Why all of this?"

Silence. When I look up, Eleanor is staring at Lily's photograph, now back in her hands.

"Because Lily believed in justice," she says finally. "In the system. In doing things right. She wanted to be a lawyer. Environmental law. She believed people could be better than their worst moments. She believed in rehabilitation. In understanding root causes. In..." Her voice breaks again. "In seeing the humanity even in people who do terrible things."

She pauses. "But I prepared for other outcomes. The supplies you found in my trunk—the rope, the plastic sheeting, the shovel, the gun—I packed them for evidence collection. Self-defense. Or worse, if the system failed and you tried to run." Her voice is flat. Clinical. "I could have buried you in these woods and no one would have ever known. I thought about it. Every day for the first month. Every time you touched me, I imagined my hands around your throat instead."

"The disturbed earth," I say. "On the logging road. The fresh grave I found."

"A contingency." Her voice doesn't waver. "I dug it the second month, when I was still planning to kill you. Scouted the location, prepared the site, made sure I had an exit strategy. Then I filled it back in when I realized I couldn't do it. Couldn't become what you are." She meets my eyes. "You found it because I wanted you to wonder. Wanted you to feel hunted the way Lily must have felt."

She sets the photo down on the table between us. Carefully. Reverently.

"And because I needed to know."

"Know what?"

"If you remembered. If you felt anything. If there was..." She stops. Starts again. "If the person I—"

She can't finish. Won't finish.

I finish for her.

"If there was any good in me."

"Yes."

The word is barely audible.

"And?" I have to know. Have to hear her say it. "Is there?"

Eleanor looks at me for a long moment. Really looks at me. And I see it in her eyes—the one thing I've never seen before: genuine uncertainty. Genuine not-knowing.

"I don't know," she whispers. "That's the worst part. After all of this. After everything. I still don't know."

She stands. Moves to the evidence room ladder again. Returns with more papers. Spreads them on the coffee table. Timeline. Meticulously constructed. Dates and events and movements tracked across almost five years.

"Let me show you what I do know," she says, and the clinical voice is back. The investigator. The professional. The only way she can survive this conversation. "Let me show you what you did."

**FEBRUARY 8, 2020:** Marcus Webb arrives at Thornewood Estate. Hired through third-party management company for winter maintenance work. Clean background check under the alias (of course). Resume shows three previous similar positions. References check out (of course). Signed guest book as Marcus Webb. Assigned bedroom in mansion—second floor, east side.

**FEBRUARY 8-18, 2020:** Marcus Webb and Lily work together. Daily tasks logged in Lily's journal: firewood splitting, roof snow removal, fence repair, greenhouse winterization prep. Lily's entries describe Marcus as helpful, knowledgeable, good company. No red flags. No warnings. No sense of danger.

**FEBRUARY 18, 2020, morning:** Lily's final journal entry. Marcus leaving early. "Something felt off" but "he's been nothing but helpful these past ten days."

**FEBRUARY 18, 2020, evening:** Lily strangled in mansion library. Time of death: approximately 8:47 PM. Four minutes from first contact to death.

**FEBRUARY 19, 2020:** No entry. No calls. No texts.

**FEBRUARY 19-21, 2020:** Radio silence from both caretakers. Management company notes later this was normal—winter caretakers often went days without external communication.

**FEBRUARY 21, 2020:** Marcus Webb sends email to management company: family emergency, have to leave immediately, apologies for short notice. Never responds to follow-up questions. Never requests final paycheck.

**FEBRUARY 22-MAY 2020:** Thornewood empty. Lily listed as missing. Search conducted. No body found initially.

**MAY 2020:** Spring caretaker discovers remains in woods, quarter-mile from mansion. Animals had... Eleanor doesn't say what animals had done. Doesn't need to.

Eleanor points to another document. Medical examiner's report. Cause of death: asphyxiation, manual strangulation. Time of death estimated February 18, based on decomposition, weather patterns, insect evidence.

"Four minutes," Eleanor says quietly. "That's what the ME estimated. Four minutes from initial pressure to death. She fought. There were defensive wounds. Skin under her fingernails. Scratches and bruises on her attacker's hands and forearms."

She shows me another document. Photos from a social media account. Josh Caine, late winter 2020. In several photos from after he left Thornewood—there are visible scratches on his hands and forearms.

The caption on one: "Battle scars from brush clearing yesterday! Blackberries fight back! 😅"

My stomach turns. I make it to the bathroom just in time. Vomit until there's nothing left. Vomit until I'm dry-heaving, until my ribs hurt, until the physical pain is almost enough to distract from the other kind.

When I come back out, Eleanor hasn't moved. Still sitting by the timeline. Still in that clinical space.

"I'm sorry," I start.

"I told you not to apologize."

"I wasn't apologizing." I sit back down. Carefully. Like sudden movements might shatter everything. "I'm sorry you had to see that. The... me being sick."

"I've seen worse. I watched my sister's killer fuck me and pretend to care."

The words are designed to hurt. They succeed.

I look at the timeline again. At the gap between February 21 and May. At all those weeks of her being gone, being missing, being *somewhere* while I was somewhere else living my life like nothing had happened.

"When did you know?" I ask. "When did you figure out it was me?"

Eleanor pulls out another set of documents. This investigation is her own. Starts in 2020, immediately after Lily's body was found. Eleanor, grief-stricken, takes leave from her job (I didn't even know what her job was, I realize—didn't know anything real about her). Gets PI license. Starts investigating cold.

Timeline of her investigation:

**2020-2021:** Interviews management company, reviews hiring records, traces Marcus Webb's background. Finds references were real but minimal. Finds previous positions existed but were short-term. Finds a pattern of a man who moves every few months, never stays anywhere long, never forms deep connections.

**2021-2022:** Eleanor learns digital forensics. Starts tracking online presence. Finds podcast. Finds social media. Finds Joshua Caine.

"You weren't hard to find," she says. "You have an online presence. The podcast. Twitter. Instagram. You post pictures of yourself. You exist publicly. You don't even try to hide."

Because I don't know, I think. Because the part of me that does it has no idea the other part exists.

**2022-2023:** Eleanor watches. Studies. Learns everything about me. My patterns. My interests. My relationships (or lack thereof). My movements.

And then she sees it. Sees the pattern I couldn't see because I can't remember the gaps.

"You move," she says. "Every eighteen to twenty-four months, you move. New city. New apartment. New job or unemployment spell or 'remote work opportunity.' And every time you move, there's a gap. Three to five days where you're not posting online. Not responding to messages. Where you effectively disappear."

She shows me the pattern. Mapped out. My movements cross-referenced with missing persons cases.

Six cities. Six gaps. Six victims.

"Thornewood wasn't the first," I say.

"No. But it was the first one where you stayed in one place long enough to leave a trail I could follow. The first one where there was video evidence of you arriving and leaving. The first one where your handwriting was in a guest book."

**APRIL 2024:** Eleanor's breakthrough. Connects me to Thornewood specifically. Confirms Joshua C. and Josh Caine are the same person through handwriting analysis, photo comparison, digital forensics.

The police had failed for specific reasons. The security camera footage was grainy, low-resolution—the kind of system installed in the 1990s and never upgraded. It captured a bearded man arriving and leaving, but the face was pixelated, the lighting poor. They'd run it through facial recognition and gotten hundreds of partial matches, none conclusive. The handwriting sample from the guest book was signed "Marcus Webb"—a name that returned nothing in any database. No driver's license. No social security number. No digital footprint. The police were looking for a ghost who didn't exist, running traditional investigative methods against a phantom.

Eleanor was looking for patterns. For someone who vanished and reappeared. For the gaps between identities.

Besides, I looked different in 2020. The footage showed a man with a beard, longer hair, twenty pounds heavier. My podcast headshots were clean-shaven, styled, professional. The police had no reason to connect a Portland-based true crime podcaster to blurry footage of a bearded transient three hundred miles away. Eleanor recognized me because she'd been studying my face for two years, watching for the structure beneath the surface—the set of the eyes, the line of the jaw, the way the hairline curved. Things that didn't change with weight or grooming.

**APRIL-SEPTEMBER 2024:** Eleanor's plan. Apply for groundskeeper position at Thornewood (estate still unsold, still needing maintenance). Get hired. Set the trap.

"How did you know I'd come here?" I ask. "You took this job months before I booked the rental."

"I didn't know for certain. But I knew the pattern." Eleanor's voice is clinical, detached. "You always circle back to places connected to trauma. Something draws you here—I understood the pattern before I understood why. And in May, your podcast started asking listeners about 'forgotten estates with dark histories in the Pacific Northwest.' You were researching Thornewood without even knowing why. I saw the episode. Recognized the obsession. I had the groundskeeper position secured within two weeks." She pauses. "If you hadn't come this year, I would have waited. I had time. Or I thought I did."

"I thought you'd remember," she says. "When you first arrived. When you walked into the library, into the bedroom you'd used before. I thought the memories would click. Some flicker of recognition."

"But I didn't."

"No. You looked at everything like it was new. Like you'd never been here. And I realized... you really don't know. When you do it, you're not you. And when you're you, you have no idea what you've done."

The dissociative disorder. Dr. Morrison tried to tell me. Tried to get me to consider it as more than memory gaps from trauma. But I dismissed it. Thought I was stressed. Dealing with my mother's death in my own way.

"How did you stand it?" I ask. "How did you... how did you touch me? How did you let me touch you?"

Eleanor's face changes. Something breaks through the clinical mask. Something raw and terrible and honest.

"I don't know," she whispers. "I would tell myself it was for the investigation. That I needed to keep you close. That I needed to test you, see if intimacy would trigger a memory, a confession, something. I would tell myself I was being professional. Strategic. That it was all part of the plan."

She stops. Swallows.

"But the truth is... there were moments when I forgot. When I was with you and I forgot what you were. Forgot what you'd done. And I hated myself for it. Hated that I could feel anything for you except rage. Hated that some part of me wanted you to be innocent. Wanted the investigation to be wrong. Wanted you to be Josh, the writer with the sad eyes who made me laugh."

A single tear runs down her face. Just one. Like her body can only spare one.

"And then I would remember Lily. And I would feel sick. And I would go back to the cottage and look at her picture and promise her I wouldn't forget again. That I wouldn't let you be human to me. That I would see you for what you are."

"What am I?" I ask. I need to hear her say it.

"I don't know." The tear is gone. The dry-eyed mask is back. "That's what I've been trying to figure out for months. Are you Josh or are you the Killer? Are you both? Are you neither? Is there a person in there worth saving or are you broken? Broken beyond repair?"

I think about Dr. Morrison. About all the therapy. About the tools I've developed for managing stress, for staying present, for being functional. About how I thought I was healing from losing my mother—murdered when I was three, a trauma I can't even remember—when really I was what? Managing symptoms? Keeping the monster asleep?

"What about the camera?" I ask suddenly. "Why was I so obsessed with the cameras?"

Eleanor almost smiles. It's the saddest expression I've ever seen.

"I think you know," she says. "Some part of you knows you lose time. Knows there are gaps. And that part of you is terrified of what happens in those gaps. So you document. You record. You try to create evidence of your own existence. Proof that you're real. That you're here. That you're you."

The cameras. The photographs. The podcast where I talk about victims and justice and truth. All of it an unconscious attempt to bear witness to myself. To catch myself in the act of being.

Or not being.

The keystroke logger I'd found on my laptop—had I installed that too? One part of me spying on another, trying to catch myself in the gaps?

"I need to know what happened," I say. "I need you to tell me everything. February 19. What do you think happened?"

Eleanor looks at me for a long moment. Then she nods. Gathers the documents. Begins to walk me through the reconstruction.

"February 18, 2020," Eleanor says, pointing to the timeline she's assembled. "This is what I've been able to piece together."

She walks me through it. Morning: Lily made breakfast. Her journal was on the counter—she'd been planning to write about the greenhouse work later that evening.

Mid-morning: Lily and Marcus—me, the other me—began winterizing the greenhouse together. Heavy equipment. Cold. Hours of physical labor side by side.

"Sometime between eleven and one, something changed," Eleanor says quietly. "I don't know what. Stress, maybe. The isolation. The intimacy of working closely with someone friendly and trusting. Whatever it was, it flipped a switch. Marcus disappeared. And whoever took his place..."

She doesn't finish the sentence. Doesn't need to.

Afternoon: Lily went to the library. Maybe for a reference book about plants, maybe just to warm up. The killer followed. The attack was quick but not instantaneous. Four minutes. Long enough for her to understand what was happening. Not long enough to escape.

Evening into night: The killer disposed of evidence. Moved her body to the woods. Cleaned the crime scene. Efficient. Methodical. No panic.

"February 19 through 21, you stayed," Eleanor continues. "Wrote in Lily's journal—erratic entries about 'staying focused' and 'controlling the narrative' and 'can't let them see.'" She shows me the pages. My handwriting. But not my thoughts. Someone else's thoughts in my hand.

"Then on February 21, based on your email timestamps and the confused tone of your messages, I think that's when Josh came back." She looks at me. "When you woke up at Thornewood with no memory of how you got there or what you'd been doing."

So I ran. Made up an excuse. Left.

And forgot everything. Including Lily.

"She died not understanding why someone she trusted was hurting her," Eleanor says quietly. "That's what I can't stop thinking about. Those final moments. What she must have thought. What she must have felt."

I look at Lily's photograph again. Try to imagine being her. Twenty-two. Alone. Finally someone to talk to. Then hands on her throat. The terror. She didn't understand why. Nice guy. Helped with firewood. Talked about victims. Now killing her. Confused. Betrayed. Never save the forests. Never hike the trail. No time left.

I'm weeping. Can't stop. Can't breathe through it. Eleanor watches me cry and her face is stone.

"I can't..." I try to speak through the sobs. "There's no coming back from this."

"No."

"Everything I thought I was. Everything I believed about myself."

"Was built on bodies. Built on my sister's body. Built on six victims whose names you don't even remember."

"I don't deserve to live."

"No," Eleanor agrees. "You don't. But you're going to anyway. Because Lily believed in justice, and justice means trial and prison and living with what you've done for the next forty or fifty years. You don't get the easy way out."

I nod. Don't argue. She's right. She's right about everything.

Eleanor hesitates. Something shifts in her expression—reluctance, maybe. Like there's one more thing she doesn't want to say.

"There's more," she says quietly. "I found it during my investigation. About why you keep coming back here."

"I don't keep coming back. I'd never been to Thornewood before—"

"You have." She pulls out another document. Old. Yellowed. A police report from 1998. "A woman was murdered here twenty-seven years ago. In the east wing. Case was never solved."

I stare at the report. "That's on the list of incidents. The 1998 murder. I saw it in my research. What does that have to do with—"

"The victim's name was Rebecca Holloway."

The world tilts.

"She had a three-year-old son," Eleanor continues, her voice gentle now, almost pitying. "He was in the house when it happened. He witnessed something—the police reports are unclear on exactly what. He was too young to be a reliable witness. Too traumatized to speak for weeks afterward. He went into foster care. Was adopted a few years later."

I can't breathe. Can't think. Can't—

"Your mother was murdered at Thornewood, Josh. By your father." She pauses, letting that land. "You were three years old. You saw it happen—saw him kill her. The police could never prove it; he disappeared after that night, was never found. But the evidence pointed to him. And watching your father murder your mother... that broke something in you that never healed. Every time you have an episode, every time the other you takes over—you come back here. To the place where it started. To the place where you first learned what violence looks like from the inside."

The room spins. I'm three years old and there's screaming and blood and a woman who looks like safety is on the floor and not moving and I don't understand why and—

Nothing. The memory cuts off. Just darkness where the rest should be.

"That's why you keep returning," Eleanor says. "Not because you chose Thornewood. Because something inside you can't stay away from the place that made you what you are."

I'm on my knees. Don't remember falling. The floor is cold and hard and real, and I'm pressing my palms against it like I can anchor myself to something solid.

My mother died here. I was here. I watched her—

And now I've killed here too. Become the thing that destroyed me.

"What happens now?"

Eleanor stands. Starts gathering the documents, the photographs, the evidence. Organizing it all back into careful piles. The work of years, the work of love and grief and terrible dedication.

"Tomorrow morning," she says. "The library. 8am. You're going to sit down and tell me everything you can remember about the gaps. Every lost day. Every time you woke up somewhere you didn't remember going. Every unexplained injury, every missing memory, everything. I'm going to record it. And then I'm going to call the police."

"And if..." I swallow. "If the other me. If it tries to—"

Eleanor crosses to the kitchen. Opens a drawer. Returns with a taser.

"Then I do what I should have done the day you arrived."

She sets it on the table. The threat is clear. The promise is clear.

"One way or another, this ends tomorrow."

I should be afraid. Should be planning escape, planning survival, planning something. But all I feel is tired. Tired and empty and something that might be relief. It's ending. After years of not knowing, of missing pieces, of feeling like I'm not quite real—it's ending. I get to stop. I get to be caught. I get to finally, finally stop.

I stand. Look at Lily's photograph one more time.

"I'm sorry I don't remember her," I say. "I wish... I wish I could remember so I could..."

"So you could what?" Eleanor's voice is sharp. "Feel appropriately guilty? Understand what you took? Make it mean something?"

"Yes."

"You'll never understand. Because you can never know her. You can never know who she would have become. What she would have done. Who she would have loved. You took all of that and you don't even have the decency to remember doing it. And that's your punishment. You killed someone you'll never remember. Someone who deserves to be remembered. Someone who should be here."

I have no response. There is no response.

I walk to the door. My legs are like they belong to someone else. Maybe they do. Maybe I've never really belonged to myself.

At the threshold, I turn back. Eleanor is standing by the table, still holding Lily's photo.

"For what it's worth," I say. "The part of me that's real. The part that's here right now. I loved you."

Eleanor's face doesn't change.

"I know," she says. "That's what makes this unforgivable. Get out."

I go. Out into the pre-dawn gray. Out into the cold. The snow has mostly melted. Just patches left in the shadows. Spring is coming. Thaw is coming. Everything frozen is being revealed.

I walk back to the mansion. Don't look back at the cottage. Can't bear to.

Behind me, through the window, I see Eleanor's silhouette. Still standing. Still holding the photograph.

She's probably talking to Lily. Telling her it's almost over. I don't know. Can't know what she's saying. What she's thinking. I'll never know. Just like I'll never know Lily. Never know what I took from the world when I took her from it.

I climb the steps to the mansion. Go inside. To the library—where it happened, where I'll confess tomorrow, where everything ends.

The library was smaller than it had been that first day. The same dark wood, the same leather chairs—but now the walls seemed to press in, the shadows deeper. As if the house itself had been waiting for this moment. For us to finally stop pretending.

I sit in the chair by the window. Watch the dawn come. The sky lightens from gray to pale blue to gold. A new day. My last day of being almost-innocent. My last day of not-knowing turned to knowing.

Tomorrow I tell Eleanor everything. Tomorrow she calls the police. Tomorrow I stop running from what I am.

Tomorrow.

But first, I have to make it through tonight. Through these last hours of being Josh, the writer, the person I thought I was.

I close my eyes. See Lily's face. That smile. Those blue eyes. That aliveness.

"I'm sorry," I whisper to her. To all of them. To everyone I don't remember. "I'm so sorry."

The words disappear into the silence. Meaningless. Insufficient. Too late.

But I say them anyway.

Because it's all I have left.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 28: CONFESSION
## March 15, Evening — Eleanor

---

Thirteen days.

That's how long it's taken to get here. Thirteen days of recording sessions in this library—Josh recounting every blackout, every gap, every fragment of memory he could dredge up. Thirteen days of my hand cramping around a pen while I documented the confession of a man who didn't remember his crimes. Thirteen days of watching him unravel a little more each morning, his voice growing hoarser, his eyes more hollow.

I'd wanted to call the police that first morning. March 3rd, the day after I showed him the footage. But Josh had woken up disoriented, not recognizing me for almost an hour. A fugue state, or the edge of one. I couldn't put him in front of detectives like that—he'd have seemed incompetent to stand trial, and everything I'd worked for would have been for nothing.

So we waited. Recorded. Built the case day by day while I monitored him for stability and he fought to stay present.

Now the fire is dying.

I've been sitting in this library for an hour, watching the flames consume the last of the birch logs, listening to the house settle around me like a held breath. The leather wingback chairs face each other across the hearth—the same chairs where we sat that first night in October, when I poured him scotch and he told me about his research. When I looked into his eyes and saw nothing but earnest curiosity and what might have been loneliness.

When I believed I could do this without losing pieces of myself I'd never get back.

The clock on the mantel reads 8:47 PM. Outside, March wind scrapes bare branches against the windows. The thaw has turned the grounds to mud, but the roads are finally passable. For the first time in months, escape is possible.

Which means this has to end tonight.

My phone sits on the desk behind me, ready. The taser is in my right pocket, fully charged. My backup—a canister of pepper spray—is in my left. I've played this moment out a thousand times in my mind. The confrontation. The revelation. The justice Lily never got.

But in none of those imagined scenarios did my hands shake like this.

The library door opens.

Josh stands in the doorway, backlit by the hallway sconces. He doesn't move, doesn't speak. Just stands there like a man approaching the gallows, knowing there's no reprieve coming, no last-minute pardon.

He looks like hell. Hollow-eyed, unshaven, wearing the same clothes from yesterday. There's a fresh bruise on his left hand—knuckles split, like he punched something. The wall in his room, I'd guess. I heard the impact around 3 AM, when neither of us was sleeping.

"Eleanor," he says. My name sounds like an apology.

"Close the door."

He does. Steps into the library properly, and I see him register every detail—the fire, the chairs, the bottle of scotch I've set out but not opened. His eyes linger on the desk where I'm sitting, where my laptop is still open to the case file. Crime scene photos visible on the screen.

Lily's crime scene photos.

He flinches. Actually flinches, like I've hit him.

"Sit," I say.

He crosses to the wingback chair—his chair, from all those October nights—and sits carefully, like his body hurts. Maybe it does. I don't know what happens to him physically during the fugue states. That's not in any of my research.

The fire pops. A log shifts, sending up a spray of sparks.

We look at each other.

Nearly five months ago, I looked into this face and saw a monster wearing human skin. Tonight, I see a man who's just discovered he's the monster, and the horror of that knowledge is eating him alive from the inside.

I don't know which version is worse.

"I remember now," Josh says quietly. "Some of it. Fragments."

My heart kicks against my ribs. "Tell me."

He stares into the fire. His jaw works, like he's chewing words too terrible to speak. When he finally talks, his voice is barely above a whisper.

"Flashes. Like photographs. A woman's face—I don't know her name. Long dark hair. She was laughing, and then she wasn't. Then she was on the ground." He swallows hard. "My hands were on her throat. Pressure. Something alive becoming something that wasn't."

The room tilts slightly. I grip the edge of the desk.

"There's a basement somewhere. Concrete floor. Drain in the center. Everything is... organized. Methodical." His voice is flat now, clinical, like he's describing someone else's dream. "I know where the cleaning supplies are. I know the water runs cold from the left tap, hot from the right. I know the overhead light flickers before it turns on."

That's the basement of his Denver rental. I'd tracked that address months ago—had Rachel's contact photograph the exterior, pull the rental records. Now I'm picturing what the police will find when they go in with a warrant. Blood in the drain. Evidence he couldn't scrub away.

"Keep going," I say.

"I don't remember their faces clearly. Just... moments. A necklace catching light. Someone begging. The sound of..." He stops. Breathes. "The sound they make when they stop breathing."

Bile rises in my throat. I force it down.

"How many?" I ask. The question I've needed answered for two years.

Josh closes his eyes. "I don't know. I've seen the evidence you collected. The files on your laptop. Six faces. Six names. But I don't know if there are more. I don't know what I don't remember."

"Six people you attacked," I say, and my voice is steady. Clinical. This is the investigator talking, not the sister. "Five died. Kevin Chen survived—barely. A cyclist found him, started CPR. He uses a wheelchair now." I pause. "Four of the dead have physical evidence linking you to the scenes. The fifth has circumstantial evidence strong enough for prosecution. The earliest one we can prove is 2019. But there might have been others before that. Practice runs. Victims whose bodies were never found."

He opens his eyes. They're wet.

"Lily," he says. "Your sister. Tell me about her."

The sound of her name in his mouth is a physical assault. I feel it in my chest, a fist squeezing my heart.

"No," I say. "You don't get to ask about her. Not like that. Not like she's a story for you to understand."

"Please." His voice breaks. "I need to know. Did she... did she suffer?"

The question I've been dreading. The one I've researched obsessively, reading coroner reports until the technical language stopped meaning anything, until 'petechial hemorrhaging' and 'crushed hyoid bone' were just words, not descriptions of my sister's last moments.

"Yes," I say, because he doesn't deserve a comforting lie. "She suffered. Strangulation takes time. Long enough for her to know it was happening. Long enough for her to fight."

Josh makes a sound—not quite a sob, not quite a retch.

"They found skin under her fingernails," I continue, and there's cruelty in me now, a need for him to hear this. "She scratched you. Fought back. There was a defensive wound on her right palm where she tried to push you away. The coroner said she was conscious through most of it."

"Stop," he whispers.

"You don't get to tell me to stop. You did this to her. You held my sister down in this library—right there, by the window—and you killed her. She died looking at the mountains she loved. She died in a house she thought was beautiful. She died because you—"

My voice breaks. The clinical investigator fractures, and underneath is Lily's sister, the girl who braided her hair and taught her to ride a bike and promised their mother I'd keep her safe.

I failed.

Josh is crying now, silent tears tracking down his face. He doesn't wipe them away. Doesn't move.

"I'm sorry," he says.

"Don't." The word cracks like a whip. "Don't you dare. Sorry doesn't mean anything. Sorry doesn't bring her back. Sorry is what you say when you bump into someone at the grocery store, not when you murder someone's sister."

"I know."

"Do you?" I'm standing now, though I don't remember rising. "Do you really know? Because four months ago you were sleeping in her bed. You were walking the grounds where I scattered her ashes. You were touching me with the same hands that killed her. Do you understand how obscene that is?"

"Yes." His voice is barely audible.

"When did you know?" The question I've been saving. "When did you figure it out? That you were the one. That it was you all along."

He's quiet for a long moment. The fire snaps.

"The first time I saw her photo in your evidence room, there was this... recognition. Not a memory, exactly. More like déjà vu. Like I'd seen her before but couldn't place where." He drags a hand across his face. "Then the footage. Seeing myself on that camera. That's when I knew. When I couldn't deny it anymore."

"But you suspected before that. Didn't you?"

He nods slowly. "The gaps. The lost time. The way people looked at me in town, like they knew me but I couldn't remember meeting them. Finding things I didn't remember buying. Waking up exhausted, with dirt under my nails, with scratches I couldn't explain." His hands are shaking. "I told myself it was insomnia. Sleepwalking. Anything but... anything but the truth."

"Dissociative disorder," I say. "Severe. Brought on by childhood trauma—witnessing your mother's murder at age three. Your mind fractured to protect itself. Created a separate identity to do the things you couldn't face doing. The ultimate compartmentalization."

"I've been reading about it," he says dully. "After I saw the footage. It fits. All of it fits. The trauma, the pattern, the triggers. But knowing the diagnosis doesn't change what I am."

"No. It doesn't."

We fall silent. The clock ticks. Outside, something—an owl, maybe—calls into the darkness.

"Did you ever feel anything real?" Josh asks, and his voice is so small, so broken. "When we were together. When you looked at me like... like you might actually care. Was any of it real?"

The question is a knife between my ribs.

I want to lie. Want to tell him it was all investigation, all performance, that I felt nothing but revulsion every time he touched me. That would be easier. Cleaner.

But I'm so tired of lying.

"I don't know," I whisper. "I don't know anymore. I wanted it to be fake. I needed it to be. But somewhere along the way, the lines blurred. I'd be gathering evidence, watching you, waiting for you to slip up, and then you'd smile at me, or read something aloud from a book, or fall asleep on the couch with your face in the light, and I'd think..." I stop. Breathe. "I'd think, 'God, I wish he wasn't what he is. I wish we could have met some other way.'"

Josh closes his eyes like I've hurt him.

"That's what makes this unbearable," I continue. "Not that you're a monster. But that you're not only a monster. That there are parts of you that are genuine and kind and broken in ways that make me ache. That I can hate you and pity you at the same time, and I don't know which feeling is worse."

"Eleanor—"

"Don't." I hold up a hand. "Whatever you're about to say, don't. There's nothing left to say. We both know how this ends."

But even as I speak, I see it happening.

The change.

It's subtle at first—so subtle I almost miss it. Josh's eyes go distant, unfocused. His breathing slows. The micro-expressions that make a face human—the tiny adjustments of eyebrows, the slight parting of lips, the unconscious blinking—all of it stops.

His pupils dilate.

No.

"Josh." I keep my voice level, but my hand moves to my pocket. To the taser. "Stay with me."

He doesn't respond. His gaze fixes on me, but it's not Josh looking out anymore. Something is different. Wrong.

He stands slowly. The movement is too smooth, too controlled. The uncertain, guilt-ridden slump is gone. His posture shifts—spine straightening, shoulders squaring, head tilting slightly as if assessing me from a new angle.

Oh God.

"Josh." I keep my voice steady. "Can you hear me?"

His head turns toward my voice. The movement is mechanical, precise. He looks at me but there's no recognition in his eyes. No warmth. Just... evaluation.

I step back. My spine hits the bookshelf. "Josh, no. Come back. I know you're still in there."

He takes a step toward me. Then another. Each movement is deliberate, efficient. His hands hang loose at his sides—ready but not yet committed. His breathing has changed: slower, deeper, the rhythm of someone completely calm.

His eyes are wrong. Not blank—there's intelligence there. But it's cold. Clinical. The way a scientist might look at a specimen.

My hand closes around the taser. I pull it out, thumb the safety off. "Stop. Right there. I will use this."

He doesn't stop. Doesn't even glance at the weapon. Just keeps coming, slow and inevitable as a tide. His gaze tracks from my face to my throat to the taser in my hand. Calculating. Planning.

Three feet away. His eyes are still fixed on me, but there's no Josh in them. No recognition that we've slept in the same bed. No memory of my name.

"Josh!" I'm shouting now, desperate. "Fight it! Don't let it take you! You're stronger than this!"

For just a second, something flickers in his eyes—confusion bleeding into recognition, Josh fighting to surface through the fugue state.

Then it vanishes.

He's three feet away. Two. I can see his hands flexing, the muscle memory of violence. I raise the taser, aim for center mass.

"Please," I whisper. Not to the thing in front of me. To Josh, wherever he is. "Please come back."

And then—

Josh stops.

His whole body goes rigid. His breathing turns ragged, harsh. He's shaking, trembling like he's having a seizure. His hands come up to his head, fingers digging into his scalp hard enough that I see blood.

"No," he gasps. His voice—Josh's voice, breaking through. "No. Not this time. Not her. Not again."

He's fighting it.

I watch in horrified fascination as two identities war inside the same body. Josh staggers, crashes into the wingback chair. His eyes keep shifting—cold and distant, then agonized and aware, then cold again. Like someone's flipping a switch back and forth.

"Josh, hold on—"

"Can't—" He's gasping. "It's so cold. I'm so cold. It wants—" His voice changes mid-sentence, flattening to that mechanical tone. His eyes go distant again. Then he screams, a raw sound of agony, and the warmth floods back into his gaze. "No—fight it—can't let—"

Then he does something I don't expect.

He grabs his left hand with his right and squeezes. Hard. Finds the old scar on his palm—the one he told me was from broken glass as a child—and presses his thumb directly into it. I hear him scream, an animal sound of pure agony.

But his eyes clear.

"Pain," he chokes out. "Keeps me—here. Keeps me—"

He's hyperventilating now, dragging in air like a drowning man. His whole body is shaking. He slides out of the chair onto the floor, curls into himself, and keeps pressing that scar like it's the only thing tethering him to consciousness.

"Stay with me," I say, and I'm crying now, tears streaming down my face. "Josh, stay with me. Don't go. Don't let it take you."

"I'm here." His voice is raw. "I'm still here. Eleanor, I'm still—"

He collapses.

For a terrible moment, I think he's gone. That the fugue state has won. I stand frozen, taser raised, prepared to defend myself against whatever comes next.

But then I hear it: crying. Broken, gasping sobs that shake his whole frame.

Josh is curled on the floor of the library, bleeding and shaking and crying like his soul is being torn apart. Because maybe it is. Maybe fighting off the other identity is exactly that violent, that devastating.

I don't move toward him. Can't. My body is locked in place by fear and pity and revulsion and something that might be relief.

He stayed. He fought it and he stayed.

"I'm sorry," he's saying, over and over. "I'm sorry, I'm sorry, I'm so sorry—"

"Stop," I say. My voice sounds strange. Distant. "Just... stop."

He does. Falls silent except for the ragged breathing. After a long moment, he uncurls enough to look at me. His face is wet, his eyes red. But they're his eyes. Josh's eyes.

"Her name was Lily Ashford," he says. Each word is deliberate, weighted. A confession and a claiming. "I killed her. Right there, by that window. February 18, 2020. I killed her and I don't remember doing it, but I know it's true. I've seen the evidence. I've felt the truth of it."

I can't speak. Can't breathe.

"I killed them all," he continues. "Five dead, one survivor. Maybe more. I stole their lives. Took everything they were and everything they could have been, and I ended it because something inside me is broken and monstrous. And the worst part is that I don't remember their faces. I don't remember their names. They deserved to be remembered. They deserved to matter. And I took even that from them."

He's still on the floor. Hasn't tried to stand. Isn't even looking at me anymore—he's staring at his hands like he's seeing them for the first time.

"I'm sorry," he says again. "I know it doesn't mean anything. I know sorry is too small a word for what I've done. But I need to say it anyway. I'm sorry for Lily. I'm sorry for the others. I'm sorry for what I've put you through these four months. I'm sorry for existing."

The room is silent except for the dying fire and the clock and our breathing.

"Call the police," Josh says.

I blink. "What?"

"Call the police." He looks up at me now, and his expression is oddly calm. Resolved. "Turn me in. Have me arrested. I'll confess to everything. I'll plead guilty. I'll tell them whatever they need to know."

"Josh—"

"Please." And there it is again—that word, that plea. "Don't let me leave here. If you don't... if I walk out that door... it might happen again. The fugue state. I felt how strong it was. I barely fought it off this time. Next time I might not be able to. And I can't—" His voice breaks. "I can't let there be another Lily. Another person who dies because I'm too broken to fix."

My hand finds my phone in my pocket. The weight of it is surreal.

This is what I've wanted for two years. Josh confessing. Josh begging to be arrested. Justice for Lily, delivered on a silver platter.

So why does it seem like I'm the one being destroyed?

"You're asking me to do this," I say slowly. "To be the one who makes the call. Why?"

"Because I don't trust myself to do it." He's still looking at his hands. "If I make the call, I might change my mind. Might run. The fugue state might take over again. But if you do it... if you're the one who turns me in... then I know it'll stick. I know you'll make sure I can't escape. You'll make sure justice happens."

"This isn't about making it easy for you," I say harshly. "This isn't about easing your guilt."

"I know. My guilt doesn't matter. What matters is that I can't hurt anyone else. That's all that matters now."

I pull out my phone. Stare at the screen. The emergency call button is right there. Three numbers. That's all it takes.

But my finger hovers.

Two years. Two years I've spent hunting him. Planning this moment. Imagining the satisfaction of watching him get led away in handcuffs. The closure of seeing him pay for what he did to Lily.

But standing here, phone in hand, looking at this broken man on the floor who just fought off his own mind to keep from killing me—there's no satisfaction. No closure.

Just hollow devastation.

"I hate you," I whisper. "I hate you for what you did to Lily. For what you've done to all of them. For what you've put me through. For making me care about you when I should only feel revulsion."

"I know."

"And I pity you. I pity the child who watched his mother die. I pity the man whose mind broke to protect him from his own actions. I pity the fact that you have to live with this knowledge now, that you'll spend the rest of your life knowing what you are."

"I know."

"And I don't know which is worse. The hate or the pity."

Josh nods slowly. "Me neither."

I dial 911.

The phone rings once. Twice. On the third ring, a dispatcher answers.

"911, what's your emergency?"

My voice is steady. Clinical. The investigator speaking, not the sister, not the woman who fell in love with a killer.

"I need to report a murder. Multiple murders. I have a confession and physical evidence. The suspect is here with me now at Thornewood Estate, 4782 Mountain Ridge Road. He's not a flight risk. He's requesting arrest."

The dispatcher's voice sharpens. "Ma'am, are you in danger? Is the suspect armed?"

I look at Josh. He's still on the floor, still broken, still waiting for the consequence he knows he deserves.

"No," I say. "I'm not in danger. Not anymore."

I give the dispatcher the rest of the information. My name. Josh's name. The approximate number of victims. The jurisdiction. The evidence I've collected. It takes eight minutes. The longest eight minutes of my life.

When I hang up, the room feels different. Smaller. Like the walls are closing in.

"They're coming," I say. "Sheriff's department first, then probably state police. FBI will get involved once they understand the scope. You'll be in custody within thirty minutes."

"Thank you," Josh says quietly.

"Don't thank me. This isn't mercy. This is justice. And justice is supposed to feel righteous. It's supposed to feel like winning."

"Does it?"

I look at him—really look at him. This man who killed my sister. This man who held me in the dark and whispered secrets. This man who is both monster and victim, both perpetrator and casualty.

"No," I admit. "It feels like losing. Like everyone loses. Like there's no such thing as winning when the damage is already done."

We fall silent again. The fire has burned down to embers now. The library is growing cold.

"I should wait outside," I say. "By the door. So I can let them in."

"Eleanor." Josh's voice stops me. When I turn back, he's looking at me with an expression I can't quite name. Grief, maybe. Or regret. Or some emotion there isn't a word for. "I need you to know something. Before they come. Before this all becomes official and you can't talk to me anymore."

"Don't."

"I have to. Just this once, let me say it."

I close my eyes. "Fine. Say it."

"I loved you." The words hang in the air like smoke. "Not the fugue state. Not the other identity. Me. Josh. The part of me that's real, whatever that means anymore. I loved the way you looked at the mountains. The way you talked about your sister even when you were pretending she was just a case file. The way you made coffee in the morning. The way you fell asleep reading. I loved you, and I know I had no right to, and I know it doesn't change anything, but I needed you to know. I loved you."

Something inside me breaks. Some last wall I was holding up against the flood.

"I know," I whisper. "That's what makes this unbearable."

In the distance, I hear sirens. Faint at first, then growing louder. The sound of inevitability approaching.

Josh stands slowly. Carefully. Like he's not sure his legs will hold him. He moves to stand in the center of the library, hands at his sides, waiting.

I walk to the front door. Open it. The March wind rushes in, cold and clean. Outside, police lights are visible through the trees, red and blue painting the darkness.

Behind me, Josh speaks one last time.

"Her name was Lily Ashford," he says. "She was twenty-two years old. She loved the mountains. She was an environmental science student. She had a sister who loved her. And I took her life. I took all their lives. And I'm sorry."

The police cars pull up. Doors open. Voices shout commands. Footsteps on the porch.

I don't turn around. Can't bear to watch them put the handcuffs on him. Can't bear to see the moment it all becomes real and permanent and unchangeable.

"Ma'am?" A deputy is in front of me. Young, concerned. "Are you alright? Are you injured?"

"No," I say. "I'm not injured."

But I'm not alright. I'll never be alright again.

Behind me, I hear them reading Josh his rights. Hear the click of handcuffs. Hear his voice, calm and clear, saying "I understand" to each question they ask.

And I stand at the threshold of Thornewood Estate—the house where my sister died, the house where I found and lost and destroyed the man who killed her—and I watch the police lights paint the night in colors that look like endings.

The fire in the library has gone out.

The investigation is over.

Justice has been served.

And I've never been more empty in my entire life.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 29: BRIDGE
## March 16 — Josh

---

The holding cell smells like disinfectant and fear.

I've been here four hours. Metal bench bolted to concrete wall. Fluorescent lights that never turn off. A toilet in the corner with no privacy. Through the bars, I can see a deputy at a desk, filling out paperwork.

My paperwork.

The handcuffs are off but my wrists still ache where they locked. Left wrist worse than right—they had to tighten it when I started shaking in the police cruiser. Not trying to escape. Just trembling so hard they couldn't keep the cuff steady.

I'm not shaking anymore. I'm very still.

This is the stillness that comes after everything breaks. After you see yourself on camera footage doing things you can't remember. After you confess to crimes your conscious mind didn't commit. After the woman you love calls the police because you asked her to.

Because you begged her to.

Eleanor's face in the library keeps playing in my mind. That moment when the fugue state tried to take over and I fought it. I've never fought it before—never been conscious during the transition, never felt the cold rushing in and known what it meant.

I fought it and won.

Then collapsed and begged her to lock me away forever.

And she did.

The kindest thing she could have done. The only thing.

A door opens. A man in a suit walks through—late forties, tired eyes, briefcase that's seen better days.

"Joshua Caine?"

I stand. "Yes."

"Mark Brennan, public defender. They've assigned me to your case pending arraignment." He doesn't offer to shake hands. Sits on the bench, gestures for me to sit. "You're facing five murder charges and one attempted murder charge. Six confirmed victims across three years. The DA's going to push for—"

"I'm guilty."

He stops. Looks at me. "Mr. Caine, I haven't even outlined the charges yet."

"I know what I did. I'm guilty of all of it."

"You haven't been formally—"

"I attacked six people." My voice is flat. Strange. Like I'm reading from a script. "Sarah Martinez. Kevin Chen—he survived. Jennifer Wallace. Lily Ashford. Robert Fischer. David Park. Five died and one survived, and I'm guilty of all of it. I'll plead guilty to everything."

Brennan sets down his briefcase slowly. "Mr. Caine, are you aware that anything you say to me is protected by attorney-client privilege?"

"Yes."

"And you're aware that Oregon carries mandatory life without parole for aggravated murder—or indefinite psychiatric commitment under a guilty except for insanity finding?"

"Yes."

"And you're still telling me you want to plead guilty? No trial, no defense, no psychiatric evaluation to establish—"

"I'll do the evaluations" I interrupt. "If that's required. But I'm not fighting the charges. I'm guilty. I just need to make sure I'm locked up somewhere I can never hurt anyone again. Prison or psychiatric facility, I don't care. As long as I'm locked up for good."

Brennan studies me for a long moment. I can see him recalibrating, trying to understand what kind of client he's been assigned.

"The DA is going to push for life without parole. Unless the psychiatric evaluation supports commitment instead," he says finally.

"I'm not fighting for leniency. I want to serve the full sentence. I want to live with what I've done. That's the punishment I deserve—living with it, remembering their names, knowing what I am. Death would be too easy."

"That's not how most defendants—"

"I'm not most defendants." I look at my hands. The same hands that had almost closed around Eleanor's throat six hours ago. No—the other me's hands. The fugue state's hands. But they're attached to the same body. Same DNA. Same responsibility. "Mr. Brennan, I need you to understand something. I have a dissociative disorder. When I commit these crimes, I'm not conscious. Another identity takes over. I don't remember any of it."

"That's a strong psychiatric defense—"

"It's not a defense. It's an explanation. I'm still the one who did it. My brain, my body, my responsibility. I killed them. The fact that I don't remember doesn't change that they're dead."

Brennan rubs his face. Looks like he hasn't slept in days. "I've been a public defender for eighteen years. Never had a client like you."

"Lucky you."

"The DA's going to order a competency evaluation anyway. Multiple evaluations. You understand that?"

"Yes."

"And if they find you're not competent to stand trial—"

"They will. I'm competent. I understand the charges. I understand the consequences. I want to plead guilty."

He nods slowly. Pulls out a legal pad. Starts writing. "They'll schedule the arraignment after the psych eval. I'll request a psychiatric evaluation as part of the pre-trial process. If they accept the plea, we'll be looking at sentencing hearings, victim impact statements, psychiatric commitment versus prison. Could take months."

"That's fine."

"Your family—do you have anyone I should contact?"

"No. No one I'd want to put through this."

"Friends? Anyone who should know—"

"There's no one." And that's the truth. I built a life that was all surface. Colleagues, not friends. Sources, not companions. Just me and my work and the gaps where I disappeared into someone else.

Brennan finishes writing. Stands. "I'll be back tomorrow before the arraignment. Get some sleep if you can."

"Mr. Brennan?" I stop him at the door. "Thank you. For not trying to talk me out of this."

"Oh, I'm going to try," he says. "It's my job. But I'm also going to listen. That's part of the job too."

He leaves.

The door locks.

I lie down on the metal bench, arm under my head, and stare at the fluorescent lights.

Somewhere in Portland, Eleanor is probably giving her statement. Describing the investigation, the evidence she collected, the confrontation in the library. Telling them about Lily.

I wonder if she's crying. I wonder if she feels relieved or empty. I wonder if she hates me.

I hope she does. Hate is clean. Hate is simple.

Better she hate me than remember the moments when we pretended we were just two people who found each other in a cold house. Better she hate me than carry any love for what I am.

I close my eyes.

Even with them closed, I see her face.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 30: STILLNESS
## March 17 — Eleanor

The hospice room is smaller than I expected.

Private, at least. Window facing east—I'll get morning light, when there are mornings left. Adjustable bed, call button, monitors that will track my dying in precise medical detail. Bathroom with handrails. Chair for visitors I probably won't have.

"This is fine," I tell the intake nurse.

"Do you have someone we should list as emergency contact? Family member, friend—"

"Susan and Mark Ashford. My parents." I recite their number from memory. Haven't called them yet. Haven't figured out what to say. *Hi Mom, I spent two years hunting your daughter's killer by sleeping with him, and now the cancer I've been hiding is going to kill me too. How's Dad?*

The nurse makes notes on her clipboard. She's young—late twenties, competent hands, kind eyes. Probably sees death every day. Probably has strategies for staying kind despite it.

"The doctor will be in this evening to discuss your care plan. We'll start you on pain management, monitor symptoms. If you need anything—"

"I know. Call button."

She leaves.

I sit in the chair by the window. Look out at Portland spreading below—buildings and bridges and the river cutting through like a vein. I lived here for years after college. Taught high school English, had an apartment, a cat, a normal life.

That was Before.

Before the phone call from Detective Williams telling me they'd found a body at Thornewood Estate. Before I identified my sister's body in the morgue. Before I quit teaching and became a private investigator, using Lily's small life insurance payout and my savings to fund two years of tracking Josh Caine to a gothic mansion where I pretended to be a groundskeeper and fell in love with a serial killer.

The cancer showed up somewhere in the middle. Stage three when I found it, stage four within months. I didn't tell anyone. Didn't stop working. Just adjusted my timeline—solve Lily's murder before the glioblastoma kills me. Race against my own dying brain.

The oncologist in Medford thought I was out of my mind—living three hours from the nearest decent hospital, alone in the mountains with a man I was investigating. But glioblastoma doesn't wait for convenient circumstances. I scheduled treatments on Josh's supply-run days, drove to Medford and back while he was in town buying groceries. Told him I was visiting a cousin in Ashland. He never questioned it. The nausea, the fatigue, the headaches—I blamed altitude, stress, winter isolation. He believed me because he wanted to believe the simple explanation. People usually do.

I won the race.

Josh confessed. Got arrested. The case is closed.

And now I'm here.

My phone vibrates. Text from Detective Williams: *Got your statement typed up. Stop by tomorrow to sign when you're feeling up to it. Great work, Eleanor. Lily's case is officially closed.*

I should feel triumph. Pride. Relief. Closure.

I feel nothing.

Just tired.

There's a soft knock. The door opens. My mother's face appears, and the expression she's wearing—God. Like she's seeing a ghost.

Maybe she is. Both her daughters gone now, in different ways.

"Eleanor." She crosses to me, kneels by the chair. Takes my hands. "Why didn't you tell us? About any of this?"

"You would have tried to stop me."

"Of course we would have! You were dying and—and investigating a serial killer and—" Her voice breaks. "Eleanor, what were you thinking?"

"I was thinking about Lily. About making sure he couldn't do it to anyone else."

"At what cost? You're—" She looks around the room, the medical equipment, the hospice reality. "How long do you have?"

"Weeks. Maybe a month. The tumor's aggressive."

She makes a sound like I've hit her. Puts her face in her hands.

I want to comfort her. Want to say the right thing, be the daughter she needs. But I'm so tired, and I used up all my acting skills pretending to be someone else with him for nearly five months.

"Mom. I'm sorry. I should have told you. But I couldn't—I couldn't stop. Not until it was finished."

"Is it finished?"

"Yes. He's in custody. He confessed. They're processing the charges now. Six victims, including Lily. He'll never hurt anyone else."

She looks up. Her eyes are red, wet, devastated. "Was it worth it?"

I don't answer. Can't.

After a long moment, she stands. Sits in the visitor's chair. "Your father's in the parking lot. He couldn't—he needed a minute."

"I understand."

"Do you? Eleanor, we've lost both our daughters now. Lily to that monster, and you to—" She gestures helplessly. "To whatever you became hunting him."

"I'm still here."

"Are you? Because the Eleanor I knew would never—" She stops. Starts again, softer. "What you did. Sleeping with him. Lying to him. Using yourself as bait. That wasn't my daughter."

"No. That was Lily's sister. And she did what she had to do."

We sit in silence. Outside, Portland goes about its day. Cars and people and life continuing whether we're in it or not.

Finally: "He's had his first court appearance. The news is covering it. 'True crime podcaster arrested for serial murders.' They're calling you a hero. The detective who went undercover."

"I'm not a hero."

"Then what are you?"

I close my eyes. See Josh's face in the library, those last moments before the police took him away. See him shaking on the floor, fighting off the fugue state, begging me to call 911.

"Finished," I say. "I'm finished."

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 31: EVALUATION
## March 19 — Josh

The walls are beige. Everything is beige—walls, floors, ceiling tiles. They told me it's supposed to be calming. Non-threatening. But after three days, the beige starts to press in, suffocating.

Oregon State Hospital, evaluation wing. Room 14B. Single bed bolted to the floor. Toilet with no lid. Window that doesn't open, reinforced glass with wire mesh. Twenty-three hours a day in here. One hour in the rec room if I'm "stable."

I've been stable. Very stable. That's the problem.

Dr. Reeves comes at 9am, 2pm, and 6pm. Sits in the plastic chair across from my bed, clipboard balanced on her knee. She's in her fifties, gray-blonde hair pulled back, reading glasses on a chain. Kind eyes. I wonder if they teach that in psychiatry school—how to have kind eyes when you're interviewing a serial killer.

"How are you adjusting, Joshua?"

"It's quiet."

"Is that good or bad?"

I think about it. "Both. Quiet means I can't hide from what I did. But quiet also means the fugue state can't... it's harder for it to emerge here. The routine. The medication. It's—controlled."

She makes a note. "Tell me about the medication. How does it make you feel?"

"Dulled. Like someone turned down the volume on everything. But I'm still here. Still me. Just... softer around the edges."

"And the fugue state?"

"It's there. I can feel it. Like something sleeping just under my thoughts. But it hasn't tried to take over since—" I stop. Swallow. "Since the library. When Eleanor called 911."

Dr. Reeves leans forward slightly. "Eleanor Moore. Tell me about her."

My throat tightens. "What do you want to know?"

"Whatever you want to tell me."

I look at my hands. The same hands that had almost closed around her throat four days ago. No—the fugue state's hands. But they're attached to me.

"I loved her," I say quietly. "That sounds insane. Loving someone while hunting them. But I didn't know—I thought I was investigating her, and she was investigating me, and underneath all the lies, there was something real. Or I thought there was. Maybe it was another delusion."

"Do you think she felt anything real for you?"

The question breaks me. "I don't know. She was dying. Terminal cancer. She had maybe weeks left, and she spent them getting close to the man who killed her sister. I can't—I don't know how to process that. Was it all an act? Or did she feel something despite herself? I'll never know. She's probably—"

I stop. Can't finish the sentence. Eleanor dying alone while I'm locked in this beige room.

"That uncertainty is difficult," Dr. Reeves says gently.

"I deserve the uncertainty. I deserve to never know. I deserve all of this."

She makes another note. Asks more questions. I answer mechanically. When she leaves, I lie down on the thin mattress and stare at the ceiling.

The fluorescent lights hum. Constant. Never changing.

This is my life now.

It should be exactly like this.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 32: FINAL DAY
## April 3 — Eleanor

The morphine makes everything soft around the edges.

My mother is here, sitting in the chair by my bed. She's reading aloud—*The Secret Garden*, the book she used to read to Lily and me when we were little. I can't follow the words anymore. The tumor has taken language processing, left me with only impressions. But I hear her voice. Familiar. Warm. Safe.

The hospice room faces east. Morning light comes through thin curtains, turning everything golden. It's a beautiful day to die.

There's a soft knock. My father enters carrying two paper cups of coffee. He looks like he's aged ten years in the past week. Gray stubble, red eyes, shoulders bent. He hands one cup to my mother, sits in the other chair, takes my hand.

"Ellie-bear," he says softly. "We're here."

I try to squeeze his hand. My fingers barely move.

"I know you are," I whisper. My voice sounds distant, not quite mine anymore.

My mother sets down the book. Leans forward. "Do you need anything? Are you in pain?"

"No pain. Just... tired."

"That's the morphine, sweetheart. It's okay to rest."

But I don't want to rest yet. There are things I need to say. Things I've been putting off because saying them makes it real.

"Mom. Dad." The words come slowly, each one an effort. "I'm sorry."

"Sorry?" My father's voice breaks. "Eleanor, you have nothing—"

"I'm sorry I lied. About the cancer. About—everything. I should have told you."

My mother's eyes fill with tears. "We understand why you didn't. You had something you needed to finish."

"Did it help?" My father asks. "Catching him. Did it help?"

I think about this. About two years of hunting Josh. Nearly five months at Thornewood pretending to be someone else. Sleeping with my sister's killer to get evidence. Watching him break apart when he realized what he was.

"It helped Lily," I say finally. "She has justice now. He can't hurt anyone else. That's what matters."

"And you?" my mother presses gently. "Did it help you?"

I close my eyes. See Josh's face in the library, fugue state trying to take over, him fighting it with everything he had. See the moment he begged me to call 911. See his eyes when the police took him away—guilt and grief and something that looked like relief.

"No," I whisper. "It didn't help me. But it wasn't supposed to."

My father makes a sound like I've wounded him. "Eleanor—"

"Dad, it's okay. I knew what I was doing. I knew the cost. Lily was twenty-two. She had her whole life. I was already dying. It was a fair trade."

"Nothing about this is fair," my mother says fiercely. "You shouldn't have had to—" Her voice breaks. "Both my daughters. I'm losing both my daughters because of that monster."

"He didn't kill me. The tumor did."

"You wouldn't have gotten the tumor if you hadn't—" She stops. Breathes. "I'm sorry. That's not fair. Cancer isn't punishment."

"Mom." I look at her, try to focus through the morphine haze. "I need you to know something. What I did—sleeping with him, lying to him, using myself as bait—I know it was wrong. I know it was destructive. But I'd do it again. For Lily. I'd do it all again."

My father's hand tightens on mine. "That's what terrifies me. That you were so willing to destroy yourself."

"Not destroy. Just... use what I had left. I had months. Lily had decades. He took her decades. I used my months to make sure he couldn't take anyone else's. That's math, Dad. Cold math. But it's fair math."

"Eleanor—"

"Did they close her case?" I interrupt, suddenly urgent. "Officially? Did they close Lily's case?"

"Yes," my mother says, understanding what I need to hear. "Detective Williams called yesterday. Joshua Caine confessed to everything. Lily's case is officially solved. Your statement was entered into evidence. You did it, sweetheart. You got him."

The relief is physical. Warmth spreading through my chest.

"Good," I whisper. "That's good."

My father leans forward, elbows on his knees, face in his hands. His shoulders shake. I've never seen him cry before. Not at Lily's funeral. Not when they told us she was dead. He held it together for everyone else.

Now he's breaking.

"Daddy," I say, and my voice is small, a child's voice. "Please don't cry."

He looks up. Face wet. "I can't help it. I'm losing you. And I can't—I can't fix this. I can't make it better. I'm your father and I'm supposed to protect you and I can't—"

"You did protect me," I interrupt. "You and Mom. You raised me strong enough to do this. To see it through. That's protection. That's love."

My mother is crying too now. Quiet tears down her cheeks. "When you were little, you used to protect Lily. Remember? If anyone was mean to her at school, you'd stand up to them. You were always protecting her."

"I was her big sister. That's the job."

"Even now," my mother says softly. "Even dying, you're still protecting her. Making sure her killer faces justice. You never stopped being her big sister."

The room goes quiet except for the sound of their breathing. Mine is getting shallower. Harder. The morphine drip increases automatically. I can feel myself slipping.

"I'm tired," I whisper.

My father stands, sits on the edge of the bed, smooths my hair back from my forehead. His hand is large and warm and safe.

"Then sleep, Ellie-bear. We're right here. We won't leave you."

"Tell me—" I struggle for the words. "Tell me about Lily. When she was little."

My mother understands. Moves closer, takes my other hand. "She was three when she decided she wanted to save every caterpillar in the backyard. Remember? She made you help her build a caterpillar hospital out of shoeboxes."

I smile. I can see it—Lily's serious little face, her careful hands.

"We kept them in the garage," my father continues, voice rough with tears. "Twenty-seven caterpillars. Fed them leaves. Watched them turn into butterflies. When they emerged, you and Lily carried the boxes to the backyard and opened them. Let them all go free."

"She cried," I whisper. "Because she missed them."

"She cried," my mother agrees. "But you told her—do you remember what you said?"

I shake my head slightly.

"You said, 'That's what love is. Letting them go free even when you want them to stay. Because their freedom matters more than your feelings.'"

The memory surfaces. Lily's tear-streaked face. The butterflies rising into blue sky.

"She loved you so much," my father says. "She looked up to you. Wanted to be like you."

"I wanted to be like her," I whisper. "She was braver than me. Kinder. Better."

"You were both extraordinary," my mother says fiercely. "You were both my brave, brilliant girls. And I am so proud of you. Both of you. Do you understand? I'm proud of you, Eleanor."

Tears slide down my temples. "Even though I—"

"Yes. Even though. You did what you thought was right. You got justice for your sister. That took courage. That took sacrifice. I'm proud of you."

The room is getting dimmer. The morphine is a tide pulling me under.

"Mom," I whisper. "Don't let them make me a hero. I'm not a hero. I did what needed to be done."

"That's exactly what a hero is," she says softly.

My father's hand on my forehead. My mother's hand in mine.

"I love you both," I whisper. "Tell Lily—tell her I'm sorry it took so long. But I did it. I kept my promise."

"She knows, sweetheart," my mother says, voice breaking. "She's waiting for you. Just across the veil. She's waiting."

The light is fading. Not the room—something else. Something inside me going out like a candle.

"Into the wild air, sweetheart," my mother whispers, quoting Mary Oliver like she did at Lily's funeral. "Go to your sister. She's been waiting so long."

I try to answer.

Can't.

The morphine tide rises.

And I let it take me.

---

========================================

# CHAPTER 33: JUDGMENT
## April 8 — Josh

The courtroom is small. Not the grand room I expected from TV shows. Just a hearing room—judge's bench, a few rows of seats, fluorescent lights that make everyone look pale and tired.

Judge Marion Foster is in her sixties, steel-gray hair, reading glasses perched on her nose. She reviews the psychiatric reports without looking up. Four psychiatrists evaluated me over three weeks. Four independent assessments. All reached the same conclusion.

My public defender, Mark Brennan, sits beside me. He fought me on this—wanted me to plead guilty except for insanity, the formal legal plea that would route me to psychiatric commitment. I refused at first. Wanted to say "guilty," plain and simple. Take full responsibility.

"It IS the guilty except for insanity plea," Brennan had explained, patient as a man talking to a wall. "That's the legal mechanism. You're not claiming innocence. You're acknowledging the crimes while the court recognizes your psychiatric condition. It's how you end up in a treatment facility instead of general population. Where the fugue state could emerge with no doctors, no monitoring, no safeguards."

He was right. So I agreed to the legal framework. But in my heart, the plea is guilty. No exceptions. No excuses.

"Mr. Caine," Judge Foster says, finally looking at me. "I've reviewed all four psychiatric evaluations. All four doctors agree: you have a dissociative disorder, severe and episodic, resulting from childhood trauma. You experience complete amnesia during fugue states. However, all four also agree that you are currently competent to stand trial. You understand the charges against you. You understand the consequences of a guilty except for insanity plea. Do you agree with these findings?"

I stand. "Yes, Your Honor."

"Very well. We're here to enter your plea. Count one: aggravated murder in the death of Sarah Martinez. How do you plead?"

"Guilty except for insanity." The words taste like ash. I want to just say *guilty*. But Brennan is right—this is how I keep the monster contained.

"Count two: attempted aggravated murder of Kevin Chen?"

"Guilty except for insanity."

She goes through all six counts. I say the words to each one. My voice doesn't shake. I owe the victims' families that much—to say it clearly, without hesitation, without excuse.

When we finish, Prosecutor Reyes stands. She's young—mid-thirties, dark suit, tired eyes. "Your Honor, the State accepts the defendant's guilty except for insanity plea. Given the psychiatric evaluations and the defendant's full cooperation, we recommend indefinite commitment to Oregon State Hospital's high-security unit."

I feel something loosen in my chest. Not relief. Just... certainty. They're going to lock me somewhere with doctors and medication and monitoring. Not general population. Not a cell where the fugue state could emerge unchecked.

"Sentencing hearing is scheduled for June 15th," Judge Foster says. "At that time, victim impact statements will be heard and final sentencing determined. Mr. Caine, do you understand?"

"Yes, Your Honor. Thank you."

"Don't thank me, Mr. Caine. Five people are dead and one barely survived your attack. Their families deserve justice. What we're doing here is ensuring you can't create more victims while addressing your psychiatric needs. That's not mercy. That's public safety."

"I understand."

She dismisses court. The deputies lead me back to the van. Back to the evaluation wing. Back to room 14B and its beige walls.

But as I leave the courtroom, I see them. The gallery. Faces full of rage and grief.

Kevin Chen in a wheelchair, service dog at his side. Alive because a cyclist found him in time and started CPR.

Sarah Martinez's daughter, thirteen years old, crying into her grandmother's shoulder.

Jennifer Wallace's students, holding photos of her.

Roy Hendricks from Thornewood, the handyman who'd noticed something off about me during my stay. He'd given a statement to the prosecution—told them how I'd asked too many questions about the disappearances, how I'd seemed obsessed with the trails where bodies were found. How he'd thought I was just a writer researching a book, not someone who already knew where the bodies were. He looks haunted now, like he's wondering if he could have stopped me sooner.

I want to say something. Apologize. Explain.

But there's nothing to say that would mean anything.

So I look away and let them take me back to my cell.

---

## June 15 — Josh

The victim impact statements take three hours.

The courtroom is full. Every seat taken. Families of the dead. Survivors. Press in the back row. Judge Foster warned me this would be hard. That's an understatement.

Kevin Chen speaks first.

He wheels himself to the front of the courtroom, service dog—a golden retriever named Bailey—walking beside his chair. He's thirty-four years old. Graduate student. Runner. Or he was, before.

"Your Honor," he begins. His voice is steady but his hands shake on the wheelchair arms. "On November 3rd, 2019, I went for my usual morning run in Forest Park. It was 6am. Overcast. I was listening to a podcast. Training for a half-marathon."

He pauses. Bailey nudges his hand. He takes a breath.

"I remember passing someone on the trail. A man. Looked normal. Running clothes. I nodded at him. Kept going. Then—nothing. I woke up in the hospital three days later. The doctors said I'd been strangled. That I'd been dead for two minutes before a cyclist found me and started CPR. That another thirty seconds and I wouldn't be here."

He looks at me. I force myself to meet his eyes.

"I don't remember the attack," Kevin continues. "I don't remember fighting. But my body remembers. I have nightmares every night. Wake up gasping, clawing at my throat, feeling hands that aren't there. I can't be alone in the dark. I sleep with the lights on and Bailey watching. I'm thirty-four years old and I'm terrified of the dark because of what happened in those missing minutes."

His voice strengthens. Gets harder.

"I'll never run again. The lack of oxygen damaged my spinal cord. I have partial paralysis from the waist down. They're hopeful I'll regain some function, but it's been over five years and I still can't stand without help. My running career is over. My independence is gone. I moved back in with my parents because I can't live alone anymore."

He's crying now. Not wiping the tears away. Letting them fall.

"But I'm alive. Sarah Martinez isn't. Jennifer Wallace isn't. Lily Ashford isn't. The others aren't. I'm alive because someone found me in time. Because Eleanor Moore's investigation led to his arrest before he could come back and finish the job. I'm alive, and I'll spend the rest of my life grateful for that and guilty that I survived when they didn't."

He wheels back to his seat. Bailey presses against his leg.

Judge Foster calls the next statement.

Sarah Martinez's daughter stands. Thirteen years old. Brown eyes. Hair in a neat braid. Her grandmother's hand on her shoulder.

She unfolds a piece of paper. Her hands are shaking so hard the paper rustles.

"My name is Maria Martinez," she reads. "I'm thirteen. My mom was Sarah Elena Martinez. She was thirty-four when she died. This is the letter I wrote to her but can never send."

Her voice breaks. Her grandmother squeezes her shoulder. Maria takes a breath and continues.

"Dear Mom, I'm in eighth grade now. You missed my seventh grade graduation. You missed my first dance. You missed my thirteenth birthday. You'll miss everything. High school. College. My wedding someday. Everything.

"Grandma says I look like you. She shows me pictures and says I have your smile. But I don't remember your smile anymore. Not really. It's been over five and a half years and I'm forgetting. I hate that I'm forgetting.
