"I remember other things though. How you made me pancakes every Sunday. How you sang in the car even though you were terrible at singing. How you read to me every night before bed even when I got too old for it. How you called me your little light.

"You coached my soccer team. You showed up for every game, every practice. And someone killed you and left you in a ditch like you were nothing. But you weren't nothing. You were my mom. You were everything.

"I'm so angry, Mom. All the time. Grandma says it's normal but it doesn't feel normal. It feels like I'm full of broken glass. I'm angry at him for killing you. I'm angry at you for going to that trail alone. I'm angry at God for letting it happen. I'm angry at myself for forgetting your voice.

"The therapist says the anger will get easier. I don't believe her.

"They caught him. The man who killed you. That's supposed to make me feel better but it doesn't. It doesn't bring you back. Nothing brings you back.

"I love you, Mom. I miss you every single day. I'll miss you every day for the rest of my life.

"Love, Maria."

She folds the letter carefully. Looks at Judge Foster. "My mom was a good person. She helped people. She didn't deserve to die scared and alone. He took her from me and I'll never get her back. That's all I want to say."

She sits down. Bursts into tears. Her grandmother holds her.

I can't breathe. Can't look away. That's my fault. That little girl's grief is my fault.

More statements follow. Jennifer Wallace's students with their video—forty-seven faces talking about how she made them love reading. Robert Fischer's husband describing twenty years together, gone. David Park's boyfriend, voice barely audible, describing the last text message David sent before his run—the one that made the prosecutor cry. David Park's parents, speaking in Korean through a translator, holding each other up.

Then the prosecutor stands. "Your Honor, we have one written statement from Susan Ashford, mother of Lily Ashford and Eleanor Ashford."

My chest tightens. Eleanor.

The prosecutor reads:

"My daughter Lily Grace Ashford was twenty-two years old when Joshua Caine murdered her at Thornewood Estate in February 2020. She was an environmental science student. She wanted to save forests. She called me every Sunday at 4pm. We would talk for an hour. About her classes, her friends, her dreams. She had so many dreams.

"Lily died terrified and alone. The coroner said she fought. Bit her tongue, scratched her attacker, struggled before losing consciousness. My baby girl fought for her life and lost.

"My other daughter, Eleanor Rose Ashford, age thirty-two, died on April 3rd, 2025—less than three weeks after Mr. Caine's arrest. Eleanor spent years tracking Mr. Caine after identifying him as her sister's killer. She became a private investigator. She tracked him across five states. She set up surveillance at Thornewood Estate. She posed as a groundskeeper for nearly five months under the name Eleanor Moore, gathering evidence while dying of brain cancer that she hid from everyone, including me.

"Eleanor sacrificed everything to catch her sister's killer. Her health. Her peace. Her soul. She used methods that horrify me. She became someone I barely recognized. The investigation destroyed her as thoroughly as Mr. Caine's hands destroyed Lily.

"I have lost both my daughters because of Joshua Caine. One to murder. One to the pursuit of justice that consumed her from the inside out.

"I do not forgive him. I will never forgive him. But I understand that he is ill. That his mind is broken. That the man who killed my daughters may not be the same man sitting in this courtroom today.

"I don't know if that matters.

"All I know is my daughters are gone. Both of them. And I will carry that absence every day for the rest of my life.

"That is all I have to say."

Eleanor's dead.

The words don't make sense at first. I hear them, but my brain can't process.

Dead. April. Weeks after the arrest.

Eleanor's dead and I'm only just now finding out.

I think of the package she'd mentioned—the evidence she mailed to Rachel. My agent had called the DA's office in late March, Brennan told me later. Frantic. Holding photographs and documents she couldn't understand, demanding to know what was happening. By then, I'd already confessed. The package was redundant. But Eleanor had made certain—even dying, even not knowing if she'd live to see the arrest—that the truth would surface regardless. She'd built failsafes into her failsafes. She'd left nothing to chance.

The rest of the hearing happens around me but I'm not in the room anymore. I'm in the library at Thornewood, watching her dial 911. I'm in the cottage, waking up next to her. I'm in the snow, listening to her laugh. I'm everywhere we were together, and she was dying the whole time, and I never knew.

Judge Foster is talking. Something about indefinite psychiatric commitment, high-security facility, no possibility of release without court approval. Life sentence.

I don't care.

Eleanor's dead.

"Mr. Caine, do you have anything to say before sentencing is finalized?"

I stand. My legs barely hold me.

"I killed them," I say. My voice sounds distant. "I took their lives. I destroyed their families. I don't remember doing it, but I know it's true. I'm guilty of everything I'm accused of and more. I deserve to be locked up forever. But I need the court to know—" I stop. Breathe. "I need Eleanor Moore's family to know that she's a hero. She sacrificed everything to catch me. She was dying and she still finished the job. If there's any justice in this world, it's because of her. Not because of me. I'm just the monster she caught."

Silence.

Judge Foster looks at me with an expression I can't read. Pity, maybe. Or disgust. Or just exhaustion with a system that produces men like me.

"Joshua Caine, you are hereby sentenced to indefinite psychiatric commitment at Oregon State Hospital, high-security unit. You will remain in custody until such time as the court determines you no longer pose a danger to society—which, given the nature of your disorder and crimes, may be never. Do you understand?"

"Yes, Your Honor."

"Then we're adjourned."

They lead me out. Back to the van. Back to the evaluation wing. Tomorrow I'll be moved to the permanent ward.

But all I can think about is Eleanor in April, dying in a hospice bed while I was being evaluated by psychiatrists. Dying without me there—not that I had any right to be—because I destroyed her as thoroughly as I destroyed my victims.

She caught me.

And it killed her.

That night, in my cell, I write a letter I'll never send:

*Eleanor,*

*I'm sorry. For everything. For Lily. For making you into someone you weren't. For not realizing you were dying. For loving you when I had no right to.*

*You won.*

*I hope wherever you are, Lily's with you. I hope you're both at peace.*

*I never will be.*

*Josh*

I fold it carefully. Put it under my pillow.

Tomorrow I'll rewrite it. And the day after. And the day after.

But I'll never send it.

She's gone.

They're all gone.

And I'm still here, locked in a cell, reciting names of people I attacked but don't remember. Five dead. One survivor left in a wheelchair.

This is justice.

This is what I deserve.

Eleanor made sure of it.

---

========================================

# EPILOGUE

---

## Part One — Eleanor
### March 29, 2025 — Five Days Before

They wheel me to Lily's grave on a Saturday.

Late March in Portland is uncertain. Some trees budding, others still bare. Spring trying to break through. My parents walk behind the wheelchair, their shadows stretching long in the pale afternoon light. They've aged ten years in the past two.

My mother asked me yesterday: *Was it worth it? What it cost you?*

I didn't have an answer she wanted to hear.

**LILY GRACE ASHFORD**
**1997-2020**
**Beloved daughter and sister**
**"Into the wild air"**

"We'll wait over there," Mom says, giving me privacy. Dad squeezes my shoulder once, then follows her.

I sit with the headstone. Cold granite. Five years of weather already softening the carved edges.

"It's done," I tell her. "He's been arrested. Six victims confirmed. All the cases closed. He'll never hurt anyone else."

The wind picks up. Bare branches click overhead like bones.

I should feel triumph. Relief. Victory.

I feel finished.

"I did terrible things to catch him, Lil. Things that would horrify you." I close my eyes and see Josh's hands on my skin. The weight of him in the dark. The way he looked at me like I was the only real thing in his world—and maybe I was, because his world was built on forgetting. "I slept with your killer. Fell for him, maybe. In another life where none of this was real, where he was just Josh and I was just Eleanor..."

I open my eyes. Just wind. Just stone.

"But that world doesn't exist. And I finished it. For you."

I wait for something. A sign. Lily's presence. Anything.

Nothing.

Just the cold granite under my palm. Just my breath fogging in the March air. Just the tumor in my brain, patient as winter, counting down.

"I'm scared," I whisper. "Scared there's nothing after. Or scared you're there and you saw what I became. Scared you won't forgive me."

The wind dies. Silence.

"I love you. I'm sorry. I'll see you soon."

I touch the headstone one last time. My hand is shaking—the tremors are worse this week.

"Time to go, sweetheart," Dad calls.

"Goodbye, Lily."

They wheel me back to the car. I don't look back.

That night in hospice, the nurse dims the lights. Pills that don't cure, only quiet.

Mom and Dad will go home to a house with two empty bedrooms now. In a few weeks, just one.

Josh is at the state hospital for psychiatric evaluation, awaiting trial. Six families have answers. Closed cases.

I did that.

I destroyed myself, but I did that.

Maybe that's all anyone gets after tragedy. Not healing. Not peace. Just the knowledge that you did what you set out to do.

Finished.

That has to be enough.

I take a breath. Let it out slowly.

Close my eyes.

---

## Part Two — Josh
### September 2025

The pills come at seven-thirty.

I've learned the schedule by heart. The medication rotation. The therapy hours. The exact dimensions of my room—twelve by fourteen, window too small to fit through, door that locks from the outside. Small details. Safe details. The kind that don't lead anywhere dangerous.

Dr. Nguyen watches me swallow. Risperdal, Depakote, Zoloft. I don't fight it anymore.

"Good," she says, checking my mouth. Routine humiliation I've earned. "Dr. Reeves at ten."

She leaves. Lock clicks. Final.

I sit at my desk. Notebook already open.

This is the ritual that matters.

**September 15, 2025**

**The Names:**

**Sarah Martinez.** 34. Single mother. Disappeared October 2019. Dark hair. Small scar above left eyebrow. She coached her daughter's soccer team.

**Kevin Chen.** Was 28. Former graduate student. November 2019—survived. Uses a wheelchair now. Studied mathematics. Has a service dog named Bailey.

**Jennifer Wallace.** 41. High school English teacher. May 2020. Taught AP Literature. Her favorite poet was Emily Dickinson.

**Lily Grace Ashford.** 22. Environmental science student. February 2020. Thornewood Estate. Eleanor's sister. Wanted to work for the Park Service. Called her mother every Sunday. Died in the library where I slept, where I touched her sister, where I pretended to be human.

**Robert Fischer.** 56. Retired postal worker. January 2021. Volunteer trail maintenance. Had three grandchildren. Liked to hike alone. That's how I found them, the police say. The ones who went alone.

**David Park.** 38. Software engineer. August 2022. Training for a marathon. His boyfriend's victim impact statement made the prosecutor cry.

Six people I attacked. Five lives ended. One shattered but still breathing.

I write their details every morning. Someone needs to remember them as people, not as my victims. It should be me. It has to be me.

I don't remember killing any of them.

That's not an excuse. That's the horror I live with.

---

Dr. Reeves comes at ten. She's carrying two coffees, which means this isn't our regular session.

"Josh. I read Susan Ashford's victim impact statement from your sentencing. About Eleanor."

The name lands like a stone in still water.

"She died in April," I say quietly. "Not even three weeks after my arrest. I found out at the sentencing. I never knew."

"You loved her."

"Yes. We were both lying to each other, and somehow it was real anyway."

"Do you still love her?"

"The part of me that's real, that's aware, that's sitting here—yes. I love her. I'll die loving her." I close the notebook. "That's my punishment. Loving someone I destroyed."

"Love isn't supposed to be punishment."

"It is when you're me."

She leaves. Lock clicks.

I open my desk drawer. Take out the letter I've rewritten forty-seven times.

*Dear Eleanor,*

*I've tried to write this a hundred times. Nothing I say matters. Nothing I could offer equals what I took from you.*

*You asked me once if I dreamed. I do now. I dream of the library. Of you. Of Lily, though I never knew her—never knew her consciously, but my body knew the room where she died, knew the estate, knew enough to come back.*

*In the dreams, I remember. When I wake, I don't.*

*I don't know which is worse.*

*You gave me something I didn't deserve: the truth about myself. Most people live and die without that clarity. I'll live and die with it. That's justice.*

*The part of me that's real loved you. Still does. That's my curse.*

*I hope you found peace. I hope Lily forgives you.*

*I never will forgive myself.*

*Josh*

I fold it carefully. Put it back in the drawer. I'll rewrite it tomorrow, and the next day, and the next. But I'll never send it.

She's already gone. Has been for months now. And I'm still writing love letters to a ghost, confessing what I can't confess to the court: that somewhere in the investigation, in the pretense, in the lies we told each other, I fell in love with her.

Really in love. The kind that lasts.

And she was falling for a serial killer while hunting a serial killer, and I was falling for my hunter while hunting her, and Lily was dead in the ground, and it was all so perfectly, horrifically wrong that there's a kind of symmetry to it.

Tragedy as architecture.

---

That evening, I take my pills. Climb into bed.

The fugue state is still there. I feel it sometimes—a flicker at the edge of awareness, like seeing movement in peripheral vision. The other me, dormant but not gone. Waiting, maybe. Patient as winter.

The medication keeps him asleep. But some nights, just before the pills pull me under, I think I feel him stir.

I don't tell Dr. Reeves about those moments.

Some cages need to stay locked from both sides.

Before I turn out the light, I say the names:

"Sarah Martinez. Kevin Chen. Jennifer Wallace. Lily Grace Ashford. Robert Fischer. David Park."

Five people who should be alive. One who barely is.

That's my life now. Pills and names and locked doors. Every morning I write what I remember about the people I killed. Every night I say their names out loud.

It should be exactly like this.

It will be exactly like this.

Forever.

---
